The Call of the Wandering Jew.
The Red Page
Hot lava floivs in my veins to-night,
My nerves are jangling mad,
The Joy of Life is a tinsel gaud.
The sweetest songs sound sad.
/ feel the drag of the Wanderlust,
I see the ghosts stream by,
I hear the call of old Ahaswer,
And I must go or die.
A horde of ghosts from the grave tramp past,
And each one turns and stares,
And each one smiles and beckons me
To join with my forebears.
And each one stands and tells his tale,
Then grimly passes by,
Each stops and speaks and beckons me,
And I must go or die.
“A slave, new freed, from the land of Ra
/ journeyed the desert on.”
“I hung my harp on a willow tree,
And wept in Babylon.”
“I saw him die on the thorn-crowned Cross.”
‘‘l writhed 'nea.th Roman whips.”
“My blood dyed red the stones of Tyre.”
'T groaned in Punic ships.”
“I wandered forth when the Holy Flame
Lit torches in Castile.”
“I wore the badge of the Ghetto's shame.”
“I died, but ivould not kneel.”
“I fought the Moor in the streets of Fez.”
“/ heard the ‘ HEP’ of hate.”
“J came afresh out of Muscovy.
But yesternight’s Jew bait.”
U I wrote joy songs for the world to sing.”
“I kept a gambling hell.”
“I caught the musio the angels love.”
“I lived to buy and sell.”
“I raised the cm of the toilers' woe.”
“A lord of gold was I.”
~Now Heine, Mendelssohn, Marx with these.
Are tramping, tramping by.
Begrimed by muck of centuries,
.And smoke of holy stakes,
Each bends his head, like the osier,
That bows but never breaks.
The nations rise, the nations war,
The nations fade and die,
They mock, they Mil, but the Jew heeds not,
Goes tramping, tramping by.
Alone comes One who had loved all men,
The man of peace and tears;
His eyes meet mine, and I rise, and stand
Perturbed by doubts and fears.
“Oh! speak the words that tvould clear the
way !
Speak! speak! one word!” I cry;
His eyes are dimmed, but His voice is mute
And He goes tramping by.
But I was born in this Southland sweet,
In it to manhood grown;
I love this land, as I love my life,
I call this land mine own.
Yet, here, to-night, my blood runs maa,
To go with these and roam,
To wander off with these gaunt, grim ghosts
That ever seek a home....
NATHAN F. SPIELYOGEL.
in “Our Gum Trees.”
In Our Gum Trees and (the inevitable)
Other Verses (Harris, 491 George-street,
Sydney, Is.), Nathan F. Spielvogel has not
achieved his patriotic desire “that Young
Australia should have metrical recitations
on subjects that would appeal to their love
of their homeland.” For his verses are
good verses, but bad recitations. Most
good verses are. Spielvogel is more patri-
otic than poetic; but Australia needs bar-
racked more than she needs bards. His
work is slight, but pleasant; and occasion-
ally, as in “The Call >of the Wandering
Jew” and “The Golden Days of Ballarat,”
he puts on wings. Note the swing of this
stanza from the latter poem:—
Then swift to work with pick and spade,
Ye must he men in this hold brigade,
Ye must he men if ye would raid
The flowers that hloom on Ballarat.
Ten thousand hees on the honeycomb ground,
Seeking the honey in shaft and mound.
Richest of honey that ever was found!
IIo! Ho! for the honey of Ballarat.
Usually, however, he prefers to saunter
along pleasant highways or loiter in the
eucalyptus shade. He has much facility,
some imagination and a fine fervor for Aus-
tralia. This little book is welcome.
George Moore
Salve, the second volume, of Qeorge Moore's
trilogy, Hail and Farewell (Heinemann, per
Lockley Library, Sydney), continues his
account of his connection with the Irish lit-
erary movement. It begins with his return
to Ireland in consequence of a ghostly com-
mand, and concludes with his renunciation
of Roman Catholicism and his reception into
the Protestant Church. Both are matters of
tremendous importance to George Moore. In
between there are alarums and excursions of
his soul. , When, in response to the super-
natural voice in London he went to Ireland,
to his amazement he found that Ireland did
not particularly want him. All he hoped for
was “a welcome and some enthusiasm; no
bonfires, torchlight processions, banners;
bands, Cead mille failte’s, nothing of the
kind, only a welcome.” He admits that he
was modest, considering the sacrifice he was
making. But when he pompously took a
contribution to a Nationalist paper he was
told that his article would be “considered.”
Moore was rightly shocked.
But M provided compensations, for he took
Moore off to call upon the ancient Irish
gods. JE knew the addresses of these gods—
they mostly lived in caves or on mountain
tops; but when these devout tourists
called, the gods were always out. Moore
failed eatirely to see any of them, though he
has a suspicion that managed to get into
partial communication with them. This
critic suspects that Ail's converse with the
gods was just about as complete as that of
the average Sydney telephone subscriber
when he raises his exchange and is blithely
told “all lines to Mosman engaged.” Ap-
parently M found all lines to Irish gods
engaged. For this annoying interruption of
the divine telephone service Moore blames
two Wowsers whom they met in the neigh-
borhood of the shrines. But the real reason
of their failure is plain, though unguessed
at by Moore. These two devout
pilgrims actually rode to the haunts of Irish
divinity on bicycles! No Irish god could
stand cycling pilgrims. The account of this
absurd tour is gravely set down by Moore.
His egoism is not afraid of becoming ridi-
culous.
Through the book there are delightful de-
scriptions of nature, and wicked descrip-
tions of Moore’s friends. M is the only one
of these to whom Moore can fit no witty,
wounding word. But Edward Martyn is still
“dear Edward.” Incidentally, Moore dis-
covered a new cure for consumption in Ire-
land
“And what cure is that?” Edward asked,
innocently.
“Holy orders. Now, listen! I’ve in-
quired the matter out, and have succeeded
in discovering .the fact that for the last 100
years no archbishop has died from consump-
tion, nor a bishop, nor a parish priest, only
two or three out-lying curates. Therefore,
my letter to her Excellency is a serious ad-
vocacy that all Ireland should take Orders,
those who want to lead celibate lives remain-
ing or becoming Catholics, those who wish
to enter the marriage state remaining, or
discovering themselves, Protestants. In this
way, and only in this way, will her Excel-
lency be able to kill a fatal disease and rid
Ireland of religious differences.”
Then came Moore’s greatest discovery.
About one-fourth of Salve is given up to the
evolution of this discovery, and the effect
its disclosure had on all the characters “on”
in this, the second, act of “The Soul of
George Moore.” It began with the text,
“Dogma corrodes the intelligence,” and led
him to the belief that the Catholics had no
literature, that 95 p.c. of the world’s litera-
ture has been written by Protestants and
agnostics, “that Catholicism hasn’t produced
a readable book since the Reformation.” He
examines and discusses the literature of each
country in turn, and with each step in his
researches he finds further evidence in favor
of his theory.
And, suddenly, Moore sees that his great
discovery must lead him into unexplored
country. For Moore had come to Ireland
on a sacred mission to further the renais-
sance of the Irish language; and if Ireland,
being Catholic, cannot produce literature,
where, precisely, does George Moore and his
mission come in? He is overcome by the
conclusion to which his theory has driven
him. “My life,” he exclaims, “has been
sacrificed for a bubble.” But a helpful
thought comes. “I haven’t examined the
question thoroughly. I may discover some
Catholic writers.” And he departs from
“dear Edward,” “thinking how I had been
ensnared, and wondering -vyho was the
snarer.”
France and Spain he had searched with-
out finding his Catholic author. England
was Protestant all through. Scandinavia
was, of course, out of the question. Russia
—Tourguendff he reckoned an agnostic
writer, and Tolstoy, “for his lack of belief
in the Resurrection, would have been denied
Christian burial by St. Paul.” “My dear
readers,” he sighs, “it seems impossible to
discover a Catholic writer of importance in
Europe.” But there remained Germany.
Hot-foot, he seeks out a German scholar;
but he cannot help him.
“Then,” says Moore, “I’m done for. I
was led into this country in the hopes of re-
viving the language. It seemed to me that
a new language was required to enwomb a
new literature. Ireland will not forego her
superstitions for the sake of literatura.” His
main contention is that “the Catholic may
not speculate ; and the greatest literature has
come out of speculation on the value of life.”
His brother, a Catholic, champions New-
man, whereupon George subjects Newman’s
Apologia to a detailed criticism. He finds
fault with Newman’s style; and “it is the
mind that produces the style.” And Moore’s
brother remarks, “It is barely credible....
I could write as well as that myself. .. .
What surprises me is the lack of distinc-
tion.”
His quarrel with his brother over Cathol-
icism and his indictment of their schooldays
in a Catholic school are done with a masterly
hand —the picture of that school will not
soon fade from the reader’s mind. Moore
decides that he must declare himself a Pro-
testant, for his soul’s sake, but such a step
would so grieve his brother that Moore de-
termines to leave Ireland. But the impulse
publicly to state his position persists; and
Moore decides to read the Bible through.
He approached the Bible in a twofold spirit:
“As a man of letters, and as one interested
in religious problems.” His criticism of the
Bible is cheerfully and wittily shocking—
Moore is not to be recommended to the re-
ligiously minded. The closing pages of Salve
tell of the struggles of Moore’s soul to accept
all the demands of Protestantism, but ulti-
mately he is convinced. But it was touch
and go. For Moore wrote to the Archbishop
stating his desire to be received in the Angli-
can Church, and his Grace referred him to
Moore’s parish priest! It seemed to Moore
that such a conversion as his could only be
managed by headquarters. For Moore leaves
his readers no doubt but that the soul of
George Moore is the one important fact in
this chaotic universe. And it is, and rightly
should be —to George Moore. His delightful
egoism presumes that his reader has the
same tremendous conviction. The climax of
this great spiritual adventure is “to be con-
tinued in our next,” for Vale, the conclud-
ing volume of the trilogy, is announced to
be in preparation. The suspense is terrible.
Kinetic and Potential Speech
In his Portraits and Speculations (Mac-
millan, per Dymock’s Library, Sydney),
Arthur Ransome puts forward his theory
of the differing value of words, according to
their potential or their kinetic energy, as an
infallible standard for the criticism of
poetry. Kinetic energy is force actually ex-
erted, potential energy is force that a body
is in a position to exert. Ransome applies
these terms to language, and defines the
medium of literature —words —as, a combina-
tion of kinetic with potential speech. Purely
potential speech may be found in music, or in
listening to a poem in a language that one
does not understand. Ransome takes Blake’s
“Tiger! Tiger! burning bright” as an ex-
ample. “The kinetic base of that stanza is
only the proposition to a supposed tiger of a
difficult problem in metaphysics.” (“What
immortal hand or eye could frame thy fear-
ful symmetry?”) But above, below and on
either side of that question, completely en-
veloping it, is the phosphorescence of another
speech, that we cannot so easily overhear.”
Kinetic speech may be translated without
loss from one language, to. another. Poten-
tial speech cannot. This is the reason why
Shelley denied the possibility of the transla-
tion of poetry. Why is it, then, that there
are poems that many men cannot perceive to
be poetry, and other poems, once poetry, now
no longer so? Applying Ransome’s formula,
we find that the nearer poetry approaches to
kinetic speech, the more easily is it appre-
hended by the multitude. Kinetic speech
secures its effects by the bare presentation of
facts, situations and stories, hence the wide
popularity of Longfellow, Scott and
Macaulay. Whenever poetry of high poten-
tiality is widely read it will be found that its
potential speech is condoned for its large
public, or hidden from it, by more than usu-
ally vigorous kinetic speech.
“The kinetic speech of a poem is under-
stood by all; the potential depends for its
apprehension upon the taste and the know-
ledge of the reader. Words must have for us
the associations that they had for the poet.
In time these things change. Unpopular
poetrv becomes quite popular, and, indeed,
no longer poetry, as it loses, through usage or
forgetfulness, its proximity to the condition
of potential speech The Elizabethan dra-
matists lost their potential, and were judged
only by their kinetic, speech during the 18th
century. They were considered coarse and
bloodv-minded, because there is rapine and
murder in their plays. Lamb restored to
them the potentiality they had lost.”
So we come to an understanding of the
Symbolists. They sought to write poetry
that should be purely potential; and Ran-
some points out that in the .revision of cer-
tain of his poems MallarmS tried to elimi-
nate. bit by bit, the whole structure of kinetic
speech that had been in them. But either
potential or kinetic speech carried to the
extreme cannot be poetry—it is either un-
intelligibility or bad prose.
♦
Chesterton has discovered, in The Vic-
torian Age in Literature (Home University
Library). that the novel is the female of its
species*. “This is the first fact about the
novel, fhat it is the introduction of a new
and rather curious kind of art; and it has
been found to be peculiarly feminine, from
the first good novel by Fanny Burney to the
last good novel by Miss May Sinclair. The
truth is that the modern novel is a. new
thing; not new in its essence, but new in the
sense that it lets loose many of the things
that are old. It is a hearty and exhaustive
overhauling of that part of human existence
which has always been woman’s province, or
rather kingdom; the play of personalities in
private, the real difference between Tommy
and Joe People put the matter wrong
when they say that the novel is a study of
human nature. Human nature is a thing
that even men can understand. Human
nature is born of the pain of a woman;
human nature plays at peep-bo when it is two
and at cricket when it is 12; human nature
earns its living, and desires the other sex,
and dies. What the novel deals with is
what women have to deal with ; the differen-
tiations, the twists and turns of this eternal
river.... The novel of the nineteenth cen-
tury was female; as fully as the novel of
the* 18th century was male.”
The Man Who Got Thinking
The Royal Road, by Alfred Ollivant
(Methuen, per Dymock’s Library), is good,
sustaining food. It is the story of the life
death and resurrection of Teddy Hankey'
of London.
“Teddy Hankey was the new sort of
working-man—a product of the towns and
twentieth century. He was not burly •
he was not beery ; and he was not slow of
mind or body. There was nothing about
him of the navvy. His strength lay in his
hands and not in his arms and back and
legs. In his life he had never swung a
pick or sledge ; never driven a spade home;
never clung to the jolting plough as it
sheered its shining way through seas of rich
red tilth. His working clothes were not
earth-soilea and russet-hued—he was too far
from the old brown mother to know kiss of
hers; nor was he ever gartered beneath the
knee. Corduroys were unknown to him;
his working duds had once been his Sunday
suit. This working-man was not the machine
that wrings its riches from the earth as his
forebears had been ; he was the man behind
the machine. In Europe and America
there were hundreds of thousands of him.
.... Anybody less akin to the John Bull of
history and tradition it would be hard to
conceive. Beef and beer had not gone to
his making. He was compact of jam, fried
fish and peppermint.... Cocky and courage-
ous, he was a bounder of the class that can
bound without offence, because it is so
clearly their nature so to do.”
And, with quiet art, Ollivant shows
us Teddy from birth to death —and
after; and the simple story is one
that is full of pathos. Teddy Hankey is
well paid as a skilled hand at a splitting
machine in a tannery. He meets his girl,
marries, has a child —all cheerfully and
cockily. But then something happened in
his heart. His little girl goes to school.
“He dimly felt that he had lost something,
that a new element was stealing upon Pis
life; and that things would never be again
quite what they had been.” Teddy, like
his Cockney class, was cursed with an im-
agination. * He began to have a dim com-
prehension of the industrial system which
had produced him, and which would just
as casually throw him away. He discov-
ered that with his responsibility to his wife
and child he could not afford a breakdown
in health. The abyss yawped. ”A man of
the same mind in a strohger body would
have read, worked and -wriggled out of the
ranks of those who live by their hands into
the ranks of those who live by their brains.
As it was, when he came home of evenings
from the factory after his 13 hours in the
stench of raw hides, he had no energy to
spare for books and study. What little was
left in him went to Meg. and her amuse-
ment.”
His wife would watch him anxiously as
he sat in silence, musing
“ ‘What is it, Dad?’
“His answer never varied. It was ‘I got
thinking.’
“It was not peculiar to him, that trouble.
It was common to the workers all the w T orld
over. They got thinking.”
“Something had crept insidiously between
the workers and the old Brown Mother from
whom they sprang, to whom they belonged,
and who belonged to them of natural right
something invisible, intangible, yet very
strong and deadly. Swiney (a Socialist
friend) spoke of it as Capital.”
“That slow unveiling of his economic eyes
w T hich conies to-day to every intelligent
worker, when the first flash of youth has died
away, and he begins to feel the w T eight of the
yoke upon bis neck, seeing life no longer as
a game, but as a grim reality, was coming
to Teddy Hankey....He began to realise
that he was not a free man walking the
earth and rejoicing in his liberty; but a tiny
cog in an immense machine without a heart
over which he had no control, and in the pro-
fits of whose labors he hardly shared; and
that if he ceased to run easily and well, tne
Engineer with the Grimy Fingers would re-
move him from his bearings and chuck mm
on the scrapheap. That sense had come to
Teddy Hankey which comes increasingly to
millions in our day—the sense that he did
not belong. He felt himself a foreigner on
earth.”
So pitilesslv the inevitable tragedy rolls to
its terrible close. The author has no briet
for the workingman; he merely shows witn
out bias the working of the soulless system.
Indeed, when Teddy loses his job for a ton-
night owing to incipient tuberculosis. UUi-
vant is careful to point out the difficulty his
employer has in taking him back. *° ,
place has been filled by an even more skilled
worker, and one equally in danger of droi
ping back to the abyss.
The cog in the wheel, owing to a flaw m
its construction, gets to its appointed P
on the scrapheap, and Teddy tries to d
himself and his child. He is , sa^®( | 0 f
great-hearted slum doctor, and, dying
tuberculosis, loses his bitterness. learns aga n
to love his wife and child, and ends m »
heart-wringing mood of acceptance o
defeat. This is the “resurrection of ,led«s’
Hankey. and it is depicted with rare artist y
The book convinces.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Morning in Arcady.
mu’ were all the juices of the air
-that splashed in heavy drops against the
world,
The while Aminta sat amid her hair,
Her every rebel tress with purple curled.
et was the milkman on his morning round,
Hut wetter the dank wight whose wavering
key _
No hole in all the painted portal found
lo ope the lock of domesticity.
Four dripping panels shut him from his
home,
nd £ oiled the malten courage that he bore;
And he, alack, too full for sound or foam,
Swayed as the night tide on a windless
shore.
i The d °or swung back, Aminta’s splendid
I eyes
Hazed on the ruin thirsty night had made.
She stooped as one who snatches some rich
prize
From odorous sadness of a garbage glade.
No hint of wrath she spake, nor any sigh
Escaped the scarlet word-way of her lips.
She smote him not, nor asked the how or
why
Of morning’s ruin and his thought’s eclipse.
But gracious liquid in a shining bowl
She poured for him with tender, loving zeal —
The wounded portions of his draggled soul
I hat blended nectar oft had power to heal.
She peeled him of his dripping raiment
drear,
| Rubbed dry the oozing wildness of his head,
j Soothed his uneasy murmurs, made him
cheer,
And tucked him softlv in a grateful bed.
For thus the pleasant dames of ancient days
Were wont to hold their heroes’ hearts in
fee
Through all the tangled paths and twisted
ways
When life was young and wet in Arcady.
Oh, damp were all the juices of the air,
lhe world was moist without and eke
within.
While bright Aminta ’mid her shining hair
Heard music in the stumbling feet ~f sin.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.
Fable of Nature and the Maiden.
A Maiden, rapturous and intense,
Grown conscious of her Innocence,
Strolled in a Meadow one glad Day,
And watched swart Yokels making Hay.
The Air was Wine, the Sky was blue,
Nature luxurious chuckled through
The breeding Earth —a Thing, you'll note,
To flutter any Petticoat.
A Lark sang Praises overhead,
And cried that grim Despair was dead
A brood of Linnets in the Hedge
Twittered, and gave a perfect Edge
To that Keen Moment of Delight
To heighten joyous Appetite.
A Curate, passing down the Lane,
Flicked at the Daisies with his Cane,
Then paused beside the Gate to mark
The Rustics, while the 'quiring Lark
Paused not, but beat against the Sky,
Exultant in his Minstrelsy.
The Curate sensed these things, maybe,
And turned with heightened Pulse to see
The Maiden rapturous and intense,
Just conscious of her Innocence.
Dame Nature laughed. Perhaps she knew
(Being so old) a Thing or two.
She laughed to see the 'wakened Maid
So radiantly half-afraid.
She laughed to feel the thrilling Flood
Of Summer’s Glory in her BLod.
(Nature, however old she be,
Loves stinging Joy like you and me.)
The Curate sighed with sudden Spite....
His Heart so full, his Purse so light!
He’d woo the Maid (he told himself)
Had he fit Furnishing of Pelf.
She eyed him shyly as she stood,
A winsome Bit of Womanhood.
Ah, how the Sky was blue, so blue!. .. .
Ah, how the Lark was pleading, too !
The Curate sought his _ humble Home,
And bent above a musty Tome.
So cramped the stony Path he trod,
He dared not grasp that Fruit of God,
Nor dream (for very Prudence’ sake)
Of tender Damsels just awake.
Though all his Manhood irked by Drouth
Yearned for the Glamor of .-her Mouth,
The wistful Wonder in her Eyes
Just opening to Paradise.
The Maiden, tremulous with Glow
Of this new Music, turned to go,
And in her Father’s Garden met
An old Man, very staid and set.
(Vile Gold had he in plenty, and
He owned a mighty Stretch of Land.)
Long had he wooed her all in vain,
And now he pressed his Suit again.
The Maiden’s sweet Lips fell apart,
While in her strangely fluttering Heart
The Magic of the. Summer grew.
(Domed Heaven was a singing blue.)
He wooed, and she could not resist,
But let her shy. sweet Mouth be kissed ;
And so, to end this futile Strife,
She told him she would be his Wife.
She robed in Spring, he for his Shroud
Preparing. Nature laughed aloud
With strident Laughter clear and dry.
The Lark was sobbing in the Sky.
Moral (for shallow Striplings who
Deem that all Fables are untrue) :
If you’d succeed in Love, be sure it
Is foolish to become a Curate;
But still, however well you waltz,
Dame Nature oft will fl"v you false.
So, when you woo vour destined Maid,
If surely you would snare the Jade,
(Though this your simple Faith may shock),
it
Is best to have a well-lined Pocket.
MAOBILAND.
FRANK MORTON.

THURSDAY, APRIL 10, 1913.

A MEMORY.
At a special meeting of the executive of the Freetrade and Land Values League it was unanimously decided that the secretary should write to all Federal candidates for New
South Wales, requesting an answer to the following questions:-
1. Are you in favor of reducing the cost of living by abolishing Customs and Excise taxes now imposed upon food, clothing, raw materials and tools of industry?
2. In e vent of the tariff being re-opened, will you vote consistently to enlarge the free list, and, if that fails, in any case tor the lowest proposed tax?

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there ; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, APRIL 10, 1913.
THE WHOLE GOSPEL OF
CONGESTION.
There dropped into Sydney recently a cer-
tain Haggard —front name Rider, and a
man of some tonnage in his own country—
who warned this community to “people the
land” instead of devoting itself too exclus-
ively to the heaping up of one big, bloated
city in each State. Thereupon the big Tory
papers of the big congested cities spoke
kindly of Haggard, whose front name is
Rider ; and declared that his words were the
utterance of wisdom, which, seeing that the
Drophet who spoke them was imported from
Freetrade England, could hardly be other-
wise from the Tory standpoint. That is to
say, these papers backed up the imported
prophet in a general way, but as their own
interests lie with the congested cities they
mostly hastened to add that the protests
against the congestion policy might be over-
done, and that it wasn’t advisable to carry
zeal to excess, and that haste might be exag-
gerated, and reform might lead to undigni-
fied perspiration, and it was always advis-
able to take all the circumstances of the case
into consideration. As the Sydney Tele-
graph put it: —•
To apply to the relative proportions of country
and city populations judgments based upon standards
of . earlier times would be misleading. If nations
which depended mainly on trade were for that reason
on the road to decaj 7 , what would be Britain’s state?
We real se—no one can help realising—that to the
healthy progress of Australia the development of her
back-country is vitally necessary. But there has
been too much talk about the disproportionate
growth of Australian cities, and especially of Syd-
ney. Cities simply focus the country about them;
what they are it has made them, and thej’ are as
necessary to it as it to them.
These are non-committal words, and they ex-
press in a general way the attitude of the
Tory city press. For the larger the propor-
tion of the population of N.S.W. or Victoria
that is compressed into Sydney and Mel-
bourne the larger is the handy and easily
available clientele of the established Sydney
and Melbourne papers, and the greater their
profits. And if every attempt to establish big
cities that are not Sydney or Melbourne is
heavily sat upon in the Congestion interest,
the less risk these papers have of serious
opposition. In other words, they hold, by
reason of the Congestion policy, a most re-
markable sort of monopoly, and they advo-
cate the policy which has made that mono-
poly. Consequently it is not advisable to
regard them as wholly unbiassed witnesses.
Sometimes there is almost a temptation to
regard some of them as unprincipled liars.
* * * *
The Congestion policy of three Australian
States has no existing precedent on the face
of the earth. There have been city States
like Carthage and Venice and Florence and
the final tail end of the Greek Empire which
consisted, at one time or another, almost en-
tirely of a huge metropolis and a back yard,
but they are dead. It is natural that they
should be dead. Taking the unhealthy case
of Sydney, it is estimated that, during the
year 1912, the population of N.S.W. in-
creased by 80,230 persons, and that 43,000
of this increase Avent into Sydney and its
suburbs (about 150 square miles), which left
37,230 people as the new population gained
by the other 310,200 square miles which
make up the State. Yet it is the boast of
such Congestion papers as the Sydney Tele-
graph and Herald that Australia is, and
should be for many years to come, essentially
a land of primary industries —that it should
depend on wheat-raising and meat- and wool-
growing, and mining, and other country jobs.
And the Protectionists, who don’t believe in
the importing policy of the Telegraph and
similar Foreign-trade rags, are addicted to
remarking —theoretically—that even though
a State should make its own boots and hats
and engines, there is no reason why these
essential industries should be all crowded on
to one spot, and the raw material which they
require carried absurd and useless distances
at great cost to that spot, and the finished
product be similarly dragged at needless ex-
pense away from that spot, and the artisan
be driven to pay twice the rent he ought to
pay by reason of the policy which crowds all
of him on to the smallest possible space or
spot, and leaves the rest of the. country
empty. Yet, despite all the professions of a
deep desire to fill up the vacant spaces almost
everybody seems to be toiling with might and
main in the Congestion interest.
It is a startling fact that, despite all the
vast borrowing that has been done, all the
railway building, all the land legislation and
all the alleged opening up of the country,
Sydney stands, at each succeeding census,
for a larger and larger proportion of the
N.S.W. population. If the movement con-
tinues at the present rate a time must come
when there will be practically nothing in the
great Cornstalk country except Sydney. On
paper that city doesn’t stand for quite such
a large percentage of the local community
as Melbourne or Adelaide does, but in reality
its case is the worst of the three. In its line
there is nothing else like it on earth. If the
area that is called Greater Sydney covered
as many square miles as Greater Melbourne
or Greater Adelaide it would represent not
less than half the population of the State.
Already it embodies 2i times the land values
of all the other municipalities put together.
It contains most of the private wealth of the
State, all deposited conveniently on the sea
shore where the first foreign enemy may get
at it on the first day of his arrival. There
isn’t a N.S.W. bank or a life or fire or other
insurance concern, or a trustee company, or
a newspaper possessed of any serious influ-
ence, that isn’t located there. There are very
few business firms of importance outside the
big city, and with the possible exception of
Newcastle there is hardly a port outside it
whose foreign trade isn’t perishing—or
already perished. There is practically no ex-
pansion of manufactures except in Sydney,
and that city possesses to-day about three-
fourths of the manufactures of the State. In
fact, it would be only necessary for an enemy
to take possession of this accessible and ill-
defended fragment of ground, and in one day
the boasted Ma province would get back
almost to the aboriginal or fried ’possum
stage of existence. It would have its rail-
way communications cut in practically every
direction. It would lose all its machinery of
government and almost all its manufactures
and its commercial facilities, its cash, its
credit and its defensive system. After that
it wouldn’t be so very much further advanced
than it was in Cook’s day —in fact, for pur-
poses of defence it would be further behind.
The attack would be stronger and the resist-
ance, by reason of the insufficiency of ’pos-
sum, would shoAV no corresponding improve-
ment. Now that Sydney has become such a
gigantic monopoly that it absorbs more than
half the new population—and unless the
movement is checked it will probably soon
absorb the whole of it and perhaps even more
-—the situation becomes full of possibilities.
When this question is raised the Tory city
papers which make stupendous fortunes out
of the Congestion device invariably reply that
the matter can’t be helped. The position
hasn’t been artificially created but has arisen
naturally. Providence did it, in fact. It is
one of the inevitable concomitants of our
complex modern civilisation —and so on. Just
here it may naturally be asked why the same
sort of disease hasn’t arisen naturally in any
other country, and why it isn’t the natural
concomitant of civilisation anywhere save in
N.S.W., Victoria and S.A. England, for in-
stance, is 300 times as thickly peopled as
Australia, and it is natural that a vastly lar-
ger proportion of its people should be dri\ 7 en
to live by town industries than is the case
here; yet it hasn’t one and only one big city
and trade centre, and its bloated metropolis
represents a very much smaller proportion of
the total inhabitants than either Sydney,
Melbourne or Adelaide. The same statement
applies to every other European or American
State. It also applies to every Australasian
State —Westralia, Tasmania, Queensland
and Maoriland —which hasn’t deliberately
laid itself out to concentrate everything in one
exaggerated capital, and devised its railway
system with that end in view, regardless of
the huge loss inflicted on the people by need-
less distances and needless haulage. Unless
it can be proved that N.S.W., for instance,
owns a special kind of civilisation that is like
nothing to be found except in a small corner
of Australia; or that 400 miles in N.S.W. is
really less than 100 miles, so that long haul-
age is cheaper than short; or that such ports
as Port Stephens and Twofold Bjiy don’t,
and never did, exist; or that all the coast of
the State, apart from Sydney, is disease-
smitten, leprous, accursed and uninhabitable
—-unless one or more of these things can be
proved the proposition that the One Big City
policy is the natural outcome of the country’s
conditions is silly and childish lying. As a
matter of obvious fact, the commerce which
might have been at Port Stephens or Two-
fold Bay, because these were the handiest
outlets for the adjacent districts, was artifi-
cially killed just as was the commerce of
Antwerp when the Dutch closed the river
against shipping, or the trade of Carthage
when the Romans obliterated the place. In
proof of which let anyone send a ship to Port
Stephens, and try to get the Railway De-
partment to take a cargo of wool to that ship
by the line which doesn’t exist —there is a
new line close at hand, but it takes an ela-
borate and costly circuit to avoid about the
best port in the State —and ask that Depart-
ment to load the vessel by means of the ab-
sent crane on the non-existent wharf. And
if he can find any disability about the place
save the careful and elaborate disqualifica-
tion which prevents the railway and the crane
and the wharf he should inform the Con-
gestion press of the fact and gladden its heart
accordingly.
The case against Congestion is, briefly,
that it retards settlement by creating a vast,
costly artificial remoteness between many of
the fertile areas of the State and the only
permissible outlet for their surplus products ;
that it leads to unhealthy overcrowding; also
that it is a most dangerous policy of putting
all the public eggs into one basket. The case
for it is that it is an old and habitual crime ,
that it protects a few loud-mouthed and
shameless Tory publications from serious
competition ; that it is good for the private
landlords of Sydney, of whom the State Pre-
mier is one; and that it saves a lazy, effete,
discredited, politically rotten and politically
unscrupulous Cabinet the trouble of living
up to its alleged principle of State land
ownership. And there or thereabouts the
whole story of Congestion begins and ends.
THE N.S.W. MINISTRY—SO FAR AS
IT HAS GOT.
Now that nine months of N.S.W.’s current
financial year have drifted by, some people
here and there begin to ask the famous Mad
Ministry where it thinks it. is going. So
far as things have travelled the ordinary
revenue has improved by £348,905, as com-
pared with the corresnonding period of last
financial year, which means that the income
has been growing at the rate of £465,200
per annum. Even if that was a lonesome
and solitary increase it should help materi-
ally to keep the State out of the ditch. But
it isn’t lonesome or solitary. It is part of a
boom movement which has gone on without
interruption since 1903. But it unfortu-
nately happens that the bottomless pit of
the McGowen Cabinet seems capable of
swallowing up all increases, and leaving
very little that is tangible or permanent or
reproductive to show for them—for such
things as its friend Nielsen’s famous trav-
elling job with £4 per day for “expenses”
can hardly be called either permanent or
reproductive. The ordinary expenditure for
the nine months has expanded enor-
mously as compared with the corres-
ponding period of last year, and
already the outgoings are over £2,100,000
ahead of the receipts. So far, N.S.W.
is shaping well for a shortage of
£2,500,000 on the year—the most prosperous
year in the matter of revenue that the State
has seen or heard of up to date. And what
the Mad Ministry proposes to do about it
that mad institution doesn’t even mention.
In fact, it never touches the subject except
in the most general way, and it alludes to
eight or ten millions more or less as if they
were a matter of no real importance.
The McGowen Cabinet has been men-
tioned in a charitable sort of way as the
Mad Ministry, but in reality the Scab Min-
istry might be a more fitting term. A scab
is understood to be one who goes back on
sound labor principles, and on this basis no
blackleg who has been kicked out of a
union, no cheap laborer, no sweater’s em-
ployee or strike-breaker has “scabbed” more
strenuously than McGowen, Holman and
co. They attained office on certain recog-
nised conditions, and unless they were politi-
cal traitors and Parliamentary liars they
were bound by these conditions. One of
them was a pledge that, for the future, loan
money would be expended less recklessly
than in the past, and only laid out on works
that would pay full interest on their cost
and a small sum—l per cent, was men-
tioned —towards a sinking fund. The prom-
ised economy of loan funds is missing. The
Government has joyously “blewed” about
£6,500,000 of borrowed money in nine
months, which means that it is pawning
posterity at the rate of about £8,600,000 per
annum. In the old bad days of Tory boom
and Tory extravagance, the very worst year
N.S.W. ever had showed a loan expenditure
of something under £5.000,000. And even
Toryism—not being then edrrupted by
McGowen and his dreadful friends—
was shocked to some extent at its
own doings, and repented more or
less, and so far cut down the lOU
that in one remarkable year that document
amounted to only a little over £1.000,000.
The charge against the present shameless
and backsliding combination is that it got
into power with a promise to reduce borrow-
ing, and it has much more than doubled
borrowing. It went back on its solemn
league and covenant almost on the day it
got into its billet, and every succeeding day
it went back on it more and more. It is
customary to complain of the falsehoods
that the capitalistic press tells about Lahnr
but in this matter the falsehoods &
allied Labor, as represented by McGowp*
the landlord, and Holman, the
told about itself would be hard to equal’ \l
toi the assuiance that loan funds
only to be expended on works that won a
pay full interest and sinking f um i it
been so utterly thrown overboard that the
sinking fund is hardly even mentioned now,
days—in fact, the amount out of
revenue in reduction of debt appears to he
less now than it was last year; and the
►State s so-called “permanent and renrod ~
tive works” are yielding less net revenue
than they did 12 months ago. If this whole-
sale and retail repudiation of the I abm-
programme doesn’t qualify McGowen" and
co. to be described as the Great Scab
Cabinet (or Scabinet) the party’s vocahn
lary is in need of revision.
* * * *
There was a time when N.S.W. almost
swore off the evil practice of floating loans
in London, and selling its children into finan-
cial slavery to the foreign capitalist, just
as the Circassian used to sell his daughters
to the foreign Turk. It will be remembered
to the discredit of the McGowen Govern-
ment that it undid this comparatively good
deed, and resumed the old practice of beg-
ging from Bull-Colien of England. Also
it has run down the credit of the State till
the price of Bull-Cohen’s aid to his mendi-
cant offspring has risen to a figure unknown
for very many years past. Yet despite all
the loans that have been floated, loan
expenditure, like ordinary expenditure, his
kept ahead of receipts, and in order to fill
the. gap the Treasurer has done marvels in
the way of laying his hands on trust funds,
savings bank deposits, small balances belong-
ing to lunatics, and such-like trifles. Even
the penny of the blind beggar would hardly
be beneath his consideration. In fact, after
all the formal borrowings (over £6,000,000
in nine months) there still remains more
than £4,650,000 due on small casual promis-
sory notes, and when they are allowed for
there is mighty little trust money that
James McGowen hasn’t got his hands upon.
Also, all the funds that the Commonwealth
had to spare in a temporary sort of way
out of the Federal bank-note fund have been
gladly accepted, and recently Australia saw
the remarkable spectacle of the Queensland
Government offering its allegedly big and
affluent relative a trifle of “monish” to
go on with —of course at a price. A whole
lot of remarkable and humorous things hap-
pen in N.S.W. these days.
The alternatives ahead of the State are
bitter poverty and hard retrenchment, or
else public insolvency. No State can go on
spending more every year with nothing
tangible to show for it, and having a bigger
deficit every year, and borrowing on a larger
scale every year, and borrowing at higher
interest every year, without some kind of
stoppage resulting. Apparently the shiftless
and hopeless Premier has realised something
of this sort at last, and he has proposed to
flee from the wrath to come. In other
words, he threatens to quit his political job
and retire before the smash and the great
discredit. He isn’t really an old man. for
his 60th year is still some distance ahead,
but he is a supreme failure, and it is not
good for any failure to wait until the public
bas a chance to tell him frankly what it
thinks of him. Some people may not greatly
admire the courage of the captain who
leaves the sinking ship, but everybody must
recognise his prudence. McGowen, during
his brief term of office, has given his followers
and admirers a good time with money raised
regardless of consequences. If he leaves
now he can say that things were good while
he was in charge, and he can blame his suc-
cessor for the consequences. Anybody who
is swift enough on his feet can be an ab-
sconding political debtor. The job of leav-
ing posterity to pay the bill, and not being
there when the bill arrives- is within the
scope of any fleet-footed character. Such
places as South America are pervaded by
people who left before the bill arrived. If
James McGowen retires between now and
election day he will at least get a fairly good
send-off from the Tory press. In his main-
tenance of the Congestion policy; in his
refusal to create State-owned cities at the
neglected ports of N.S.W.: in his reverence
for the interests of the Sydney landlords; in
his clamor for a mean and hopeless Federal
capital; in his narrowness and parochialism;
in his borrowing and booming—in all these
things he has been a Premier after its own
heart. So it will write a pleasant but
guarded epitaph above his political dust.
And then the people whom he fooled will be
left to consider how 7 to clear off the dead
horse which he didn’t take away with him
in his retreat.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Greece and its Marine Jest.
It has been the fashion with a certain un-
patriotic gang of politicians and human sun-
dries to heap a crude variety of scorn on tne
small beginnings of Australian naval defence.
To these people the fact that the Australian
fleet won’t be born full-grown —as it any-
thing was ever born full-grown —has been
advanced as a reason why it shouldn t fie
born at all. In its early stages it will be
quite unfit to face single-handed the navy or
a Great Power, or even of something less than
a Great Power, and this fact is advanced as
sufficient cause for derision. True, the first
English navy was quite unfit, for many years,
to face the fleets of Norway or the Greek
empire, and even in Plantagenet times Eng-
land was glad to hire marine aid from v emce.
And there are people still living who remem-
ber when the German navy was so small anu
futile that Denmark blockaded the German
coast, and a British Prime Minister
at the German salt-water banner as a P 1
flag.” Therefore there are precedents to
Australian’s small beginnings as a sea M° w
—so many precedents that only the mo
thick-headed of critics can be oblivious
them. x\.nd within the last few months hi -
tory has been busy supplying further mi ■
trations of the case, and showing that e\
a baby fleet may be a matter of infinite i
portance. When the four Confederate
with 10,000.000 people among them, took tne
chance of war with the Unspeakable 1
and his population of 24,000,000 they b .
wanted ships, and only Greece had any
of a sea armament. Greece is a very, ' •
small country, and very barren, and natui. .
very poor. It isn’t a patch even on -


tralia for population, and is not within a
hundred centuries of it for wealth. And the
necessity for keeping up a disproportionately
large army as a safeguard against the Otto-
man had further helped to starve its naval
force. Still Demetrius Piiriedfisii got his
infant squadron to sea in haste, and Abdul
Ballast and Abdul Bilgewater did the
same with the Ottoman navy. The two
sized each other up, and then Abdul Bal-
last and Abdul Bilgewater found it was
time to run for shelter. The Greek fleet
might be, according to the doctrines of the
Australian anti-Defence party, a jest which
had no business to be loose on the waters
without its mother to look after it, but it
was the better jest of the two, and Deme-
trius at the guns showed every resolve to
make the best of whatever chance he had. His
outfit might be only worth 2d., but then its
enemy was only worth lid., and that odd Id.
made all the difference. In the circumstances
the Greek fleet served its purpose just as well
as 20 Dreadnoughts would have done. With
two more good ships the Turks might have
driven Greek commerce off the sea, spoiled
all the Greek schemes for annexing Turkish
islands, and perhaps kept 50,000 Greek sol-
diers at home doing garrison duty in sea port
towns. As matters stood it was Turkish
commerce that was bumped hard on the seas.
Incidentally, whenever the Greeks had a
minute to spare they captured another Turk-
ish island, helped themselves to whatever
arms and ammunition the little garrison pos-
sessed, diverted the local taxes into their
hungry treasury, and gathered recruits among
the wildly applauding inhabitants, and while
they did this the disgruntled Mohammedans
could do very little save look on. By reason
of a few very inferior ships the whole Greek
army was able to go to the front, for even
the comic opera fleet could keep the peace on
the Greek coast against the variety show
fleet of the Turk. The same humble disar-
rangement of ships was able to assist at
bottling up for a time a huge Turkish army
on the Gallipoli peninsula, where the alleged
Caliph’s soldiery became steadily wetter,
colder, hungrier, more dispirited and less
useful. When it was necessary to rush Ser-
vian reinforcements to the front in haste it
was the Greek naval attitude which made
the short sea-route practicable, and incident-
ally it is mentioned that it was the Servians
and their guns which were the last straw on
the back of the Turkish camel at Adrianonle,
wherefore that place surrendered the other
day with 60,000 soldiers and 640 valuable
cannon. As already mentioned, according to
the position of the Australian anti-Defence
push Greece has even less right than Aus-
tralia to have any sea-power at all, for it has
far less means of supporting anything of the
sort, and it hasn’t a hundredth part of Aus-
tralia’s chance that its navy will ever be
equal to that of a Great Power, or even of a
fairly strong secondary Power. But Deme-
trius did his best, and as it happened Pro-
vidence was good to Demetrius as it often
is to those who do their best. His force was
big enough for the job it had to do, and
it was a job on which immense. issues
depended. It saved probably twice its
value directly, and a lot more indirectly.
The record of the Balkan scrap is full of en-
couragement for those who believe in making
a good beginning with a system of national
defence, even though—the race of the pro-
phets being extinct —no one knows at the
moment exactly what the ending may be. It
is quite possible for a big city to be held up
by an iron dredge with a few guns on it if
the owners of the city hadn’t the foresight to
lay in some guns and a dredge of their own.
And then that big city feels very small and
mean, and all the world laughs at it. The
moral of which is that in defence matters
almost anything is a little better than no-
thing, and that the gods are often unexpec-
tedly good to the community which goes the
whole hog. even if it is a small community
and can’t go any great size of a hog.
Our Daily Strike.
Considering that almost everybody in and
around Sydney is, or has recently been, or
expects soon to be on strike, and that a
large section of the population of Newcastle,
Broken Hill and the Illawarra district is, or
was, or soon may be, in a similar situation,
the time appears to be ripe for some dis-
course about the case. At present the
Labor party owns the Parliament and the
Government of N.S.W. Through them it
bosses the railways, and the latest big strike
concerns the railways. Through them, also,
it controls the whole machinery of Indus-
trial Arbitration, which was devised to
make strikes unnecessary, illegal and impos-
sible, and it is on chronic strike against
Arbitration. It is also supposed to own
and control Attorney-General Holman, the
eminent Laborite who recently declared in
London that Arbitration had abolished
strikes in the land of the. Ma Community.
By reason of its ownership of Parliament
it lately rushed, or shambled, through the
State Legislature a Gas Bill of sorts, w'hich
was to put everything right in the gas indus-
try, and the gas strike which followed made
this new and allegedly perfect arrangement
sit up suddenly and look foolish. The
whole question boils down to two proposi-
tions : If unionism believes that the strike
the simple old device of industrial warfare
—is the best way of settling differences, then
it should call on its Ministry and its 1 ar-
liamentary majority to wipe all Arbitration
devices off the local statute' book; and it
should cable to Attorney-General Holman
and tell him not to be an absurd ass in
London. Then the position would be clear
and honest. On the other hand, if the
unions still believe in Industrial Arbitra-
tion, but consider that they haven t aot the
right variety of it, they should explain what
is the right variety, and see that the
Labor Government and the Labor I ai^ ia '
mentary majority supplies the toods. lhe
present situation is anomalous. The P ar ty
in power seems to be, a great part of the
time, on strike against itself and its own
methods. It rushes into industrial warfare,
apparently on the plea that it and its I ar-
raent and its Government are such darned
fools that it has to live in almost chronic
insurrection against them in order to get
its rights. It gets awards and it makes
agreements, but amid the prevailing chaos it
pays so litle attention to them that an im-
pression begins to arise that no possible
agreement is worth more than a Bengalee s
oath or a Mexican President s billet. Cer-
tainly in many of tlie recent disturbances
the strikers appear to have had a good case
—often a very good case—when their hoin,
wages and general conditions were ta ven
into account. But when Labor owns t t
Parliament and the Cabinet, and can make
and unmake the whole system of Ir mstrial
Arbitration it should be able to j ut these
matters right without running amok after
the manner of a bovine animal in a china
shop, and declaring war, not only avainst
the employers, but against the whole com-
munity, Labor supporters included. Unless
the party is prepared to announce that it
really prefers war to peace, and strikes to
arbitration, and that all its professed advo-
cacy of wages boards and such-like is gross
hypocrisy, the only possible explanation is
that the party doesn’t believe it is fit to
govern, and that it regards its Parliament
and its Ministry and its own programme
as failures. There is some reason in the
spectacle of the defeated minority resorting
to civil war. But when the victorious
majority does it, the situation is grotesque.
The almost certain downfall of the N.S.W.
State Labor Government will be largely
due to the deafening advertisement its own
supporters have supplied re its incapacity,
and their own incapacity. The unions, like
Dogberry, write themselves down an ass.
The community finds strikes a costly nuis-
ance. One reason why it put Labor in
power was that Labor might give itself com-
plete justice—all the justice it thought it
required—by peaceful means. And the com-
munity is savagely disappointed to find that
the apparently infallible remedy is a failure,
and that the unions strike a little more furi-
ously, if such a thing be possible, against
their own crowd than they did against the
other crowd.
“Constructive Statesmanship.”
Of all the Tory comment which was made on
.Toseph Cook’s exposition, last week, of the
Liberal policy for the forthcoming three
years, the most priceless, in the way of
broad humor, was certainly that of the S. M.
Hekald. With every appearance of gravity,
this irrepressible old journalistic wag said
(4/4/T3) :
There is a note of constructive statesmanship in the
policy of the Liberal leader that we look for in vain
in that of Labor.
Properly to appreciate the sardonic bitter-
ness of'the above jest, it is necessary to con-
sider Joseph’s recent effort in detail more
or less. A number of institutions are to be
“maintained” —the Federal Arbitration Court,
Old Age and Invalid Pensions, a White
Australia, Compulsory Military Service, the
Australian Navy, and sundry other things
which no office-seeker in the possession of
his faculties would dare, at this time of day,
to talk of overturning. Also, “the policy of
the present Commonwealth tariff is to be
preserved,” which is a civil way of mention-
ing that the sweated goods of the cheap-
labor nations overseas are to be suffered to
pour in on us as heretofore ; and “the States
are to be secured full independence in local
affairs,” or, in other words, all attempts from
any quarter to validate such enactments of
the old, or Deakin Liberalism, as the New
Protection, a Federal Rule, or the Aus-
tralian Industries Preservation Act, will be
resisted by the New, or Cook Liberalism, to
the ultimate gasp. None of the things which
have been, at various times, represented as
Curses, Menaces and the like, by the people
whom Cook now represents are to be at-
tacked. In fact, they are to be cherished.
And one new measure is foreshadowed. It is a
hilarious fact that in all the vast welter of
speech which Joseph poured forth on Thurs-
day night last one piece of legislative reform,
and one only, is promised. According to
Joseph :
We propose to mature along lines suggested by the
experience of other ccntries a comprehensive scheme
of national insurance, providing for sickness, acci-
dents, maternity, widowhood and unemployment on
a contributory basis.
Britain’s National Insurance Bill has been
denounced for the palpable Socialism that it
is by every consistent anti-Socialist in the
United Kingdom. But let that pass. Aus-
tralia has long ceased to expect anything re-
sembling honest anti-Socialism from the
bogus anti-Socialists of its Federal and State
Houses. Cook’s lonely proposal is open to
harsh criticism as a most fraudulent piece
of confidence trickery on other grounds.
National Insurance, on a contributory basis,
is carried out in other countries —which is
to say, in Germany and the United King-
dom —on the following lines: the employee
is forced by law to contribute a certain sum
per week to a* provident fund, for the benefit
of himself and his family : the employer must
provide a somewhat similar sum ; while the
State makes up the difference with a contri-
bution that is equal to the other two or
thereabouts. In Britain and in Germany it
is possible for the central Parliaments to
enforce such an Act. Not so here. The
High Court has declared that, by virtue of
the Trade and Commerce clause in our Con-
stitution, the Australian Parliament though
it has power to make laws in respect of trade
and commerce between the States, has no
power whatever to interfere with any con-
cern which operates within a State. To in-
dicate the distinction between the conditions
here and abroad it is enough to remark that
whereas the British Parliament is fully em-
powered to force, say, an Edinburgh hotel-
keeper and his employees to join in any form
of National Insurance that the Legislature
puts on the Statute Book, the Australian
Parliament has no more power to so inter-
fere with such a business, or with any other
business that is confined to one State, than it
has to legislate for the well-being of Russia.
To sum the matter up, Cook, in the unlikely
event of his ousting Fisher from office, may
so violate his individualistic principles as to
pass an Insurance Bill. But unless he also
gets the Federal Constitution amended by a
general Referendum of the electors, the mea-
sure will be quite futile and valueless. Now
he firmly opposes any Constitutional altera-
tion, and, most of all, one that touches the
trade and commerce clause. Which is to
say, his talk of introducing a system of
National Insurance is the rankest sort of
false pretence. The rest of Joseph’s policy
consists partly of assurances that he will do
what his opponents are already doing (the
promotion of immigration, for instance, which
has never been brisker in the history of Aus-
tralia than it is to-day), and partly of pro-
mises that he will oppose things—a raised
tariff, the strengthening of the Federal Par-
liament, with the object of validating social
reform measures, and so on. To call such
aspirations “constructive statesmanship”
well-nigh amounts to insulting language. A
politician, one-half of whose programme con-
sists of maintaining existing institutions,
while the other half consists of opposing new
ideas, isn’t a constructive person. He is a
cul de sac.
Selling Australia a Dog.
The Bulletin’s Retired Admiral writes: —
The Navy policy speech of Winston Churchill
(First Lord of the Bull Admiralty) the other night
in the House of Commons, reads very much as if
Australia had been sold a dog. Which has been
the not uncommon experience of the Commonwealth
in Quite a lot of its dealings with the dear old
Grandmother Land. In all good faith Australia has
been carrying out its naval defence policy on the
lines endorsed by the Imperial Defence Conferences
of 1909-1911, and has given patriotic proof of its
intention to move on towards providing the founda-
tion of a powerful fleet for the preservation of the
White Man in the Pacific. But, until such time as
that defence has been provided for locally, the obli-
gation was supposed to be with Britain to afford a
certain sense of security. The form of the obliga-
tion was not all that Australia could have desired ;
still, it was something. And the fact that Bjjll
has now definitely thrown it on one side is another
significant pointer to just how much is likely to
be expected* in that quarter if a war scramble is
suddenly sprung upon the Empire.
Bull undertook (or it was understood that he
did) to fit out two fleet units —one in China waters,
with Maoriland’s Dreadnought as the flagship, and
one in Indian waters. For some time past it was
evident that Bull had no intention of keeping his
share of the “honorable understanding.” While Aus-
tralia was hard at graft preparing, as a dutiful son
should, to help the old man in the direction the old
man had pointed out, the aged reprobate broke faith.
Left him, in short, to shift for himself, with the
bald unofficial intimation that things were different
now, or the time was geting ripe in Europe (nobody
seems to care how it is progressing in Asia), or
something equally vague and unsatisfactory. Now,
Winston Churchill puts the position beyond doubt.
Said he (per cable) :
“If the pressure at the decisive theatre continued
to grow it behoved the oversea Dominions to pro-
vide for their own and the common security by form-
ing local navies, or, what was more effectual, by
additions to the Imperial navy, in order to preserve
its world-wide mobility.”
Which goes to explain why Maoriland, with its
Dreadnough “addition” to the Imperial navy, and
Canada with its proposed three-Dreadnouvht “addi-
tion,” have been receiving the high spot light of
Imperial approval lately—additions to the navy are
now more favored than local units. Bull is in
fear at home. White Australasia is in fear at home,
too, but that isn’t worth mention, unless its relief
is provided for in the latest proposal of the Admir-
alty to form the Canadian Dreadnoughts (if the
gift is made), the Malay States’ Dreadnought and
Maoriland’s Dreadnought into an Imperial squadron,
based on Gibraltar. As Churchill says:—
“They would occasionally visit the Dominions to
show the flag [say, about twice a month or cen-
tury] and be ready [say, at a moment’s notice] to
operate at any danger point at Home or abroad
[say, at Australia’s open north].
No wonder Australia’s Government hustles out the
statement made by Prime Minister Fisher, on
Monday night, that rapidity of expansion is to be
shown by the Commonwealth in local naval defence.
Clearly we are on our lonesome at this end,

Defence Hint from this paper’s “Old Sol-
dier” :
The recently-fallen Adrianople—one of the two
entrenched nosit.ons which the Turks have held
throughout the war —affords an object lesson in the
value of the new kind of fortified position with a
town for its “nucleus, and a chain of detached forts,
each capable of defending itself for a time, scat-
tered at various distances round the central town
and at various intervals from one another. It is neces-
sary in these days of long-range rifled guns to keep
the besieger so far away from the centre of an en-
trenched position that he cannot bombard it with
any effect. As the range of rifled guns increases, the
forts must be moved further and further from the
nucleus town if it is to be really safe from bombard-
ment. Just now, if the besieger can place and work
his batteries within 7000 yards of the central town,
he can make himself tolerably troublesome to its
people. What he has to fear is the fire of the guns
in the forts, or in the subsidiary batteries that will
be thrown up in advance of them when the attack
begins. The besieger ought always to be able to
establish a superiority of fire and gradually advance
his batteries. He ought, if he has means and skill
enough, to be able in time to take some of the forts
one by one, but if they were well defended it would
be at heavy cost both in men and material. The
Adrianople forts were built all in earthwork, with
underground barracks and stores, in the beginning
of the war with Russia in 1877. They were too
near the town, which was thus exposed to damage
by bombardment, from which it is said to have
suffered a good deal.
The cable tells us that the Turkish gunners fired
slowly to save their ammunition, which was running
short. That is where the besieger has the advantage
every time. He can, if the road is open behind him,
renew his stock of ammunition and food, and even
bring un fresh men to make good his losses, and re-
place those who are worn out with the hard work
in the trenches, while the besieged, when they are
once surrounded, must do as they best can with what
they have, unless their friends outside can collect
force enough to break through the line of invest-
ment and bring them more supplies and fresh men.
The advantage of the defence is decisive for the
time, but if nothing of the sort is possible the de-
fenders will get weaker and weaker, while the guns
of the besiegers are knocking their earthworks out
of shape, and they are less able every day to go on
repairing the damage that the guns are doing to the
works. It is not only the fighting, but the down-
right hard and exhausting work that has to be done,
renewing smashed gun-carriages in the batteries,
bringing up new guns to replace those which have
been made unserviceable, and perforrrrng a hundred
other duties that are essential to keeping the defence
alive. The besieger is sure to win if he is not hur-
ried ; but the Bulgarians and Servians \yere hurried
—or thought so—at Adrianople. So they drove their
men on to storm the only half-ruined forts. Whole
companies and battalions are said to have been wiped
out by the fire from the forts. Perhaps Australia
will note that little fact, since it is really deter-
mined to defend itself. Why should it not make up
its mind to provide for the defence of its big sea-
ports by a ring of earthworks, far enough out to
protect them from bombardment or from a sudden
rush ? It would not really cost much. Anyhow, it
would be far cheaper than buying that big
“manoeuvre area” at Liverpool.

Sydney Telegraph performed a notable feat
(2/4/’l3) in the way of compressing a
quantity of mendacity into a very small
space. Discussing Fisher’s policy speech
disclosures it said :
The New Protection is an undeveloped, unexplained
device to give a share of the tariff benefits to the
workmen and the consumer. The party now frankly
confesses that, whether it can elaborate a system
of New Protection, or not, etc., etc.
Is the New Protection “an undeveloped and
unexplained device,” and a system which has
yet to be elaborated? The untruthfulness of
such a contention is as obvious as the side of
a gaol. The New Protection is well under-
stood in Australia, and it has been carefully
explained and approved, not merely by Labor-
ites, but by the leaders of Liberalism both in
the Federal and State Parliaments. Alfred
Deakin was the author of it, and he ex-
pounded and upheld it in many an eloquent
address both in and out of Parliament. W.
H. Irvine, M.P., gave it his (dessing and
warm approval on many occasions. Ex-
Miristcr of N. S. Wales, said as
far back as 1911: —
The proposed amendments consist, firstly, of power
to give effect to the New Protection policy. I am a
Freetrader, but if we must have heavy duties....
then I believe in the manufacturer’s employee get-
ting a fair share of the profits made—-a fair and
reasonable wage. If the New Protection were a
separate issue at the Referenda, I venture to think
it would be carried by a large majority.
The Australian Government has no need to
cast round for a means of “elaborating” a
system of New Protection, as is suggested by
the Telegraph. The thing was done when
the Excise Tariff Act. 1906, was passed.
The High Court declared it invalid, owing to
the presence of the Trade and Commerce
clause in the Constitution. _ And there is
only one way by which this well-developed
and exhaustively explained, and highly ela-
borated and likewise widely approved system
may be rendered effective —namely, by an
affirmative vote to the Trade and Commerce
clause amendment proposal next May.
The distressful Sydney Ferries, Ltd., hav-
ing been driven to pay its deck hands as
much as Bs. per day, has replied by collect-
ing from 15 to 58 p.c. more from its regular
customers. The co. has got so into the habit
of making money that it can’t bump against
even a misfortune without trying to coin it
into dividends. In seven years it has added
the Kanimbla, Kuba, Ivirawa, Kaikai,
Kailoa, Kirrule, Iviandra, Kosciusko and a
few horse-punts, etc., to its fleet, at a cost
of about £150,000; it has added £46,262
(net) to its cash balances; it has put about
£15,000 (net) into wharves, plant, etc.; and
£42,684 (net) has gone into the purchase of
Harbor foreshores and other landed pro-
perty. The total of these four items alone
is £253,946. And all the capital the share-
holders have put up in that period is
£134,389. This means that in the past seven
years this company’s customers have not
only supplied a dividend of about 14 p.c. on
the shareholders’ capital actually paid, but
have also provided £119,557 of assets. Or
put it another way: of the £150,000 which
the Kanimbla, Kubu. Kirawa, Kaikai,
Kailoa, Kirrule, Kiandra and Kosciusko
have cost, the co. has nrovidecT £30.443 and
the co.’s customers £119,557 in addition to
the dividends. It is absurd, of course, to
expect that a co. with such a record could
afford to pay its deck-hands 10 p.c. more
wages without requiring its customers to
pay anything up to 58 p.c. more for the use
of a ferry which they have paid for out of
their own pockets.
A Dominant Note.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(1) The Australian is a loafer. (2) The Aus-
tralian is selfish. (3) Thank God the Maorilander
is not as the Australian. — Synopsis of the published
views and opinions of T. L. Mdls, Esq., of Maori-
land.
What is the dominant note of Australian scenery ?
That which is the dominant note of Edgar Allen
Poe’s poetry—Weird Melancholy.— Marcus Clarke.
Our forests, they tell us, are solemn, our
mountains are secret and stern ;
There is l&lackness and gloom in our gorges
—all is dust in our Poesy’s urn!
Thus the Millses and Clarkes by the
column, as they rise in their turns to
explain—-
(What a cheerful procession of Borgias,
and how vast are their howls of dis-
dain !)
There is death in the poems that we grow—
’tis a story of sullen despair
That is told in the desolate verses our
Daleys and Bradys prepare!
John Milton’s “L’ Allegro” and “Comus’i
distant, of course, from our scope :
Still, in spite of these creakings of hearses,
we brandish large symptoms of Hope!
We regret that our landscape is ghostly, and
the flights of our white cockatoos
Grieve us deeply indeed. We ask pardon for
the leaps of our grey kangaroos!
Our sun sinks instanter, and mostly our hills
seem to frown on the scene,
Whilst the strength of this land seems to
harden into men who are sudden and
Our soil has no past, has no story—kooka-
burras discordantly shriek;
Yet the trend of this nation is upward ahd
most unmistakably Greek!
The secret foundations of glory are laid in
the depths of our hearts,
And the spielers whose footsteps spiel Cup-
ward are this land’s least signficant
parts!
Our nation looms vague and gigantic, so the
critics who bound on its marge
See the husk that’s imported and British—
not the soul of Australia at large !
So they jump and they howl, corybantic,
and they fashion with brimstone and
gall
Long screeds which describe us as skittish
and careless, disloyal and small!
And we laugh—there’s no end of our grin-
ning, no end to this continent’s smile ;
For these Persons are most entertaining,
and they help to amuse us awhile!
It is there that we’re patently sinning—each
scandalised Britisher knows
That this land is a wilderness, paining John
Bull with its smoodge-lacking pose!
Still, we note that the Islands Explosive,
which crawl on their stummick to Bull,
Show a sudden if late disposition to copy
our notions in full!
Whilst the voice of a desolate Joseph is
raised where the titles are stored,
It is Massey’s and Allen’s ambition to
jump on the projects of Ward !
So we smile, as I mentioned, and also hear
hearty Canadian yells
For a fleet on our Commonwealth’s pattern
—disloyalty patently tells !
And ’tis strange that Field Marshals should
call so for armies made after our plan—
Can it be that Britannia’s the slattern, and
the loafing Australian the Man?
Vast shadows infesting our spaces, lone
horsemen ’twixt moonlight and dawn —
Can it be that we’ve shaped us a nation
whilst Maoriland leapt to the pawn?
How queer are our gumtrees —who traces
hieroglyphics so strange in their guise ,
Can we really expect approbation in the
Cockneyfied Britisher’s eyes?
Unsoothed bv the flames of the martyr, slow-
grilled at the Ignorance-stake,
Can it be that our mood is reliant, our man-
hood erect and awake?
No Propputy-vote in our Charter —oh, say,
can this miracle be ;
Is Australia the good-humored giant, too
large for Pig Island to see?
