WHERE
EVERYBODY
GOES
“ J.R.” : Thus Governor Strickland, at
Sydney Town Hall, the other night:—
We have seen that missionaries and their work
have support, both moral and financial, not only
from an eminently religious people like the British.
Etc., etc.
A comparatively insignificant section of
Britishers continue to support the futile
business of despatching parsons to convert
the heathen. The huge majority, finding
that the heathen is rarely converted, and
that, when he is, the process usually turns
him into a liar and a thief or worse, have
lost interest in missionising. In most parts
of the earth where the good old Union Jack
flies, the missionary and his efforts are a
stock joke on about the same plane as the
mother-in-law one that has come down to us
from the pleistocene period. So far as official-
dom is concerned, if there is one class of
persons against which the British Govern-
ment has set its face like a flint in its deal-
ings with its polychrome empire, it is the
missionaries. In India it has persistently
ignored them, and ignores them still. It
Will not even subsidise their schools, though
it generously subsidises Hindu and Moslem
schools. A similar attitude is observable
elsewhere. Ask any British official you
chance to meet in a nigger-infested country
what he thinks of the local missionary, and
the odds are 10 to one you will find his
synonym for the holy man is “blanky nuis-
ance,” or words to that effect.
«
Frank C.: The Fusion candidates continue
to be sorely afflicted with a great lack of
intellect, accentuated by a plentiful dearth of
ideas. Thus Senator McColl, at Ballarat,
decrying the sanest part of Fisher’s policy,
the expenditure on defence (out of revenue,
by the way) :
In 1902 Mr. J. C. Watson said that £500,000 a
year was sufficient for Australia’s defence, but lately
Senator Pearce said that £5,000,000 was not too
much. What a change !
Poor dull McOoll can’t see that things are
now vastly different from what they were in
1902 —that Japan has licked Russia, and a
lot of other things have happened in 10
years. And consider his financial ideas: —
Mr. Fisher’s note issue had largely brought about
the financial stringency. The banks had to pay Mr.
Fisher 10 millions in gold for his notes, and thus
half their reserves were taken away. That meant" less
money was available.
Does the foolish old man really believe that
banks lent out the money they held as
reserves against their note issues? Listen
again to the outpourings of Palmer, M.P.
for Echuca :
Mr. Fisher claimed that his Government did not
go to the money-lenders of the world, but simply
borrowed from itself. True, but Mr. Fisher might
have added: “You cannot take some millions of
money out of circulation without contributing to the
dearness of money.”
The hopelessly muddled rail-straddler seems
to think that when Fisher borrows from
himself, he hoards the money away in a pri-
vate place. Bull Cohen has lent tens of
millions to himself, and the scarcity of
money in Britain must be something appal-
ling by now; yet it contrives to lend a pound
or two even to outsiders at times.
Died on Monday “Jim” Anderson, K.C.,
who might have got the vote as Adelaide’s
most popular citizen —a fine sportsman,
with plenty of public, spirit, and in court
an agile cross-examiner and a dashing
speaker. His wife is a daughter of Judge
Gordon (S.A.), and father-in-law and son-
in-law had more than most men in com-
mon.
Another curse came home to the import-
er’s roost and crowed dismally a few days
ago. It appears that last year there was a
shortage of cement in New South Wales,
and the Public Works Department manfully
grappled with the difficulty by importing
supplies from the obliging and cheap-labor
foreigner and lifting them over Australia’s
non-Protective tariff wall. By-and-bye, the
temporary rush for cement ceased, but the
Department’s orders from abroad continued
to arrive with enthusiasm and expedition.
Thereabouts, the local manufacturers came
to the conclusion that they might as well go
out of business until the importations ceased,
but, finally, rather than close their works,
they agreed to pay £22)0, the cost of 27,000
barrels of cement under order, on condition
that the Works Department cancelled its
foreign orders. So local industry once more
gets a show.
H. H. Champion writes:—
In a recent issue there is a par. about the
Andersen case, in which my name is mentioned. I
have no time to show how many misstatements it
contained, but would ask you to print the following
matter, dated 25th February, 1913, from T. Tunne-
cliffe, M.L.A., who asked on what terms Andersen
could be free from detention in the Ivew Asylum as
a lunatic (the Dr. Jones referred to is the Inspector-
General of the Insane for Victoria:—“l saw Dr.
Jones yesterday (Monday afternoon) in the presence
of Mr. Callaway, Under-Secretary, and he promised
me that he would give Mr. Peter Andersen a full
discharge, which could first be read by Mr. Holden-
sen (Danish Consul) or myself. This he would hand
to Mr. Holdensen or myself or any responsible per-
son on a guarantee that it would not be delivered to
Mr. Andersen until he was out of the Commonwealth;
that is, I take it, after the boat leaves its last port
of call within the Commonwealth.” I thought this
delightful. Andersen is a lunatic while in Australia,
but could be certified to be sane if he will only go
away.

“Puss in Boots” still hunts the unshod
mouse of public applause at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney), and overtakes the animal fre-
quently. She has pursued her calling for
many weeks, and no news of her probable
cessation is yet to hand. The advt. says
that the show gladdens 14,000 people every
week, and even if it really gladdens only
13,999 people, this paper won’t argue about
the discrepancy. In a time like the present,
when the weather is bad. and domestic ser-
vants are almost unattainable, and a new
strike happens almost every week, no one
save a madman would look for needless
quarrels merely because the weekly attend-
ance at “Puss in Boots” is one individual
less than the management says it is.
The Crystal Palace broke out into colors
this week with ‘“The Doctor’s Patient.” On
the whole, these artificially-colored films look
more perfect than the kinemacolor, though
evidently they can’t be turned out as
rapidly. But, apart from the fine coloring,
the doctor’s patient has little to recommend
it. “The Suez Canal,” at the same theatre,
provided some good scenes of that warm
spot. “The Mystery of the Elk Bank,” at
the Colonial, was another of the detective
yarns wherein, for the very manyth time, the
’tec., after being almost eaten alive by the
secret society, comes home to the missuus
with another batch c f scalncj.

Cummin and Seaham, who are half-
brothers to Gowome and Gitwork. are the
latest excuse for taking the girl to the
Tivoli. They come unburdened with super-
latives, and merely book themselves as
knockabouts, and, particularly in a hat
manipulating act, they offer a very tooth-
some mixture of nonsense and novelty. Tay-
lor and Arnold, the ragtimers, make their
third re-appearance, and look capable of
making a thirteenth without causing the box-
office receipts to wilt. The Clark-Owen turn,
Henri French, Will Whitburn and Alex.
Prince are other baits held out by the man-
agement. A matinee is being given this
(Thursday) afternoon, with the object of
helping the stranded Atkinson’s circus
people out of the soup that bad Melbourne
weather and a two-weak treasury landed
them _ in. The programme will be a pot-
pourri of selections from current shows.
The National Amphitheatre is featuring
a perfect herd of new stars this week. The
pride of the collection is Paul Gorden, the
world’s greatest wire-walking drunk. Many
an honest, right-thinking citizen has felt asi
though he were walking on a wire when he
was merely proceeding along his own garden
path. Paul does the actual thing, and does
it well. Associated with him are Spenser
Kelly and Marion Wilder, who sing dis-
tractingly, Mathews and Mack, travesty
stars, and a dozen others of merit, or there-
abouts.
At the Palace, “A Woman of Impulse”
will be introduced on Saturday night in the
place of “Dr. Wake’s Patient” by the Hamil-
ton-Plimmer co. The piece made many
friends some little time back when it was
presented by Talbot, Ltd., at the same the-
atre. There will be four nights only of im-
pulsive lady, as the season must be closed
on Wednesday in order that the co. may
reach Melbourne in time to open on the fol-
lowing Saturday night. The ponderous
leaps of the Sydney-Melbourne express are
responsible for some very fried language by
show managers these days.
Fred Niblo. of the mournfully beautiful
clothes, and Miss Josephine Cohan, of the
wistful smile and the scrubbing-brush, sup-
ported by their menagerie of wild “rubes,”
continue to attract audiences to “The For-
tune Hunter” at Sydney Criterion. And
if among the menagerie one only knew
which animal was which actor, this
critic might be able to pat several on the
back and prod a few with a vicious umbrella.
But there are 15 of them, and the writer is
no good at logarithms.
The Adelphi, having collected its reserve
supers, finds that its supply of kings, prime
ministers and wood-cutters will last for an-
other eight nights ; then, there being no one
else worth killing, “From Convent to Throne”
will itself die a natural death. “The
Woman Pays” is announced as the successor.
This is said to be a local production dealing
with the Sydney Ferries’ season tickets.
At the Little Theatre “The Man on the
Box” has not yet gone on strike, nevertheless
in these infectious days, when Sydney lives
in a state of chronic upheaval, it is a wise
management that has another money-maker
ready for an emergency. Wherefore, the
second production is being prepared. It is
to be “Fanny’s First Play,” which received
the most exclusive approval of the purple
when George and Mary commanded Granville
Barker, who was producing it at the Ivings-
way Theatre, London, to stage it at Downing-
street for the special edification of members
of the Amalgamated Union of Monarchs,
Princes, Gentlemen Ushers, Dukes, Lady-
Helps, Duchesses and Royal Unemployed
visiting the Big Smoke at Coronation time.
Altogether, the piece ran into 600 perform-
ances in London, which is classical business
for comedy. The date of its introduction to
Sydney is not yet announced. Meanwhile,
“The Man on the Box” is plying for hire
nightly and on Thursday afternoons.

Bronson (Leslie Holland), a pugilist (Edward

“Lizzie” is still sweating blood, or rum, or
whatever liquid it is that pours from the
cuticle of an able-bodied female hippo., at
Wirths’ circus entrance. Madam Garcia
descends nightly the chute of death, and
death retires with a hurt feeling to consider
why a lady whose motor-car throws three
somersaults in the air shouldn’t become his
prey. La Belle Victoria smiles as charm-
ingly as ever while she swings her Mexican
beauty to and fro on the slackest of all slack
wires. The Flying Codonas whirl in the
air, blindfold and otherwise ; and the clowns
—well, the clowns and Polar bears are just
clowns and Polar bears. Which is to say
that Wirths’ circus is still going strong in
the Belmore Markets, Sydney, and endeavor-
ing, with marked success, to make good its
modest claim to be the greatest show on
earth. In defence of its general character,
the writer offers it his sworn testimonial
as the least smellful circus he has ever met.

“The Belle of New Vork” will ring at
Sydney Royal for the last time on Friday of
next week. The public will tear itself re-
gretfully away from Ichabod Bronson. He
is a rare old pietist, not exactly a Chadband
or a Pecksniff or a Heep or a Mawworm.
but a snufflebuster who knows the joy of
life —a festive,_ long-legged Wesleyan with
hop-leaves in his hair, and yet not so many
hop-leaves as to be in danger of degenerat-
ing into a Stiggins. Bronson may be de-
scribed as the leaping or ballet-dancing
hypocrite, and he is one of the few hypo-
crites who tend to make their job popular.
Ichabod _ was probably at his best when
Oscar Girard did him, and as Girard was a
dying skeleton in those days, the fact was
all the more remarkable. Something else
will follow “The Belle” at the Royal, but
on this subject a certain scarcity of infor-
mation prevails so far.

City, means a big success. With the multi
tude increasing, and no other house compete

attaching to the play that is made out nf »
novel. It has not the directness, for ex
ample, of Claudian,” perhaps the neared
previous thing to it; and it weakens down
from the first two strong acts. But it is a
fine spectacle, and has a fine cast, the lavish
ing of good men on little parts being remark

Evidently “The Count of Luxembourg,”
which derived its music from the composer
of “The Merry Widow,” is not destined to
outrival its relative. There are some pretty
waltz and other dance strains in the “play”
introduced to Australian at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.) last Saturday, but the performance
left no haunting echoes in the ear, nor did it
ever begin to look like a record-breaker. Of
novelty in story, characterisation, or irrele-
vant items there is none whatever. A hand-
somely 7 decorated show, containing many
lively legs, two or three passable songs and a
lot of fitful bustle, is a thing to be seen, of
course. Having seen “The Count of Luxem-
bourg,” the most willing slaves of comic
opera will soon forget it. Heavy-footed
Talleur Andrews is utterly unsuited to the
name part, and Florence Young, as the
heroine, can give him no assistance to speak
of in the mazy waltz. Their waltz up the
broad, easy staircase at the back of the Grand
Duke’s reception hall was painfully labored
on the first night. The briskness of W. S.
Percy, as the Grand Duke, seems mechanical
rather than snontaneous, whilst his allusions
to his fluttering old heart grow wearisome.
Sybil Arundale, in a colorless soubrette cha-
racter, is merry and bright, but the music
asks more of her singing voice than she can
give, and her share of the dialogue is dull
stuff, like everybody else’s share. Phil Smith,
the new comedian, who paints his face a
strawberry tint and terminates many of his
sentences with a partial snigger or chuckle,
is nimble on his feet and funny in spots. At
a first hearing, one compares him favorably
with Bertie Wright, insofar- that his buffoon-
THE COUNT OF LUXEMBOURG.
To the studio of Brissard (an ignoble son
of Art
Who should have been preparing little din-
ners a la carte)
The Williamsonian chorus, in a foolish sort
of way,
Came in Carnival disguises and pretended
to be gay.
They frivolled round a table,
Raising Cain if they were able,
And they made an awful babel when pre-
tending to be gay.
The student Count of Luxembourg, a stony-
broke young man,
Consented to be wedded, like Don Caesar do
Bazan,
To a girl whose name he knew not, nor her
eyes had ever seen.
He pushed his hand in marriage through an
intervening screen.
The Count was paid to do it,
And the prima-donna knew it,
So they sang a bridal duet whilst the screen
was in between.
The bantam Grand Duke Rutzinov, a cousin
of the Czar,
Though somewhat more than old enough to
be the lady’s Par,
Was booked to take the Countess for his
wife in legal course,
If her marriage with the stranger could be
ended by divorce.
But they met and were delighted,
When they found they were united,
And the ducal hopes were blighted re that
pre-arranged divorce.
Brissard, the cook-shopkeeper—with a nose
as red as flame,
And tricks of style suggestive of a Christmas
panto dame—
Paired off with Sybil Arundale, who capered
to and fro,
And shook the entertainment when its pulse
was beating slow.
But the guesters and guestesses,
•And the dancing and the dresses,
Were the principal successes of an unexcit-
ing show.

At the Bijou, the demand for “The Graft-
ers” is still running to eight performances
a week. A “Texas Tommy Dance” by the
entire company, is a newly-added link in
the Grafter chain or vaudeville-comedy
items, and the silly old belief that no profit
could come out of a venture at the Bijou is
now quite faded and gone, like the last Rose
of Summer.

. T l!r e l J ova bl e characters in “Milestones,”
at Melbourne _ Royal, are endearing them-

Parramatta, Joe Cook, explaining the
Liberal programme, nearly wept when he
talked of the sinfulness of the jLabor party in
attempting, to. “interfere” with the magnifi-
cent Constitution, so “clear, precise and logi-
cal, that was “adopted” only 12 years ago.
It may be a sacrosanct document, and it may
be particularly wicked for mere mortals to
amend it only 12 years after it got into oper-
ation ; but, in that case, what can we think
of the infamy of the Deakin-Cook Govern-
ment which carried through a referendum
tor the alteration of the same Constitution
in two places when the sacred thing was only
nine years old !
A wail about scarcity of employment was
recently upheaved in Melbourne, and, of
course, the Government was urged to borrow
a new heap of money and start building a lot
ot probably unprofitable public works. It
would be interesting to know how Melbourne,
or any other old place in the Commonwealth,
expects, under the present tariff, to avoid
the unemployed trouble. In 1910 this fool
country imported £42.700,000 worth of goods,
which was £42.700.000 worth of work it
didn t have. In 1911 it imported £48.000,000
worth of goods, which was £48,000,000 worth
fro’^A^i :ave awa y- In 1912 it imported
t 02,000,000 worth of goods, which repre-
sented £52,000,000 worth of honest toil
deliberately thrown overboard. A commun-
't.v of that sort deserves to be unemployed.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The Imperial (Hyde Park) Roller Rink Co.
opened for the 1913 season on Monday evening’.
General-manager Bendrodt was on his old stand at
the top end of the palatial marble steps. Here, he
extends the glad hand to the plain citizen or’the
naughty Miss, and passes on the Order of the Boot
to the joyous hooligan. The Imperial Roller Rink
has the unrivalled advantage of having, been built
tor its present purpose. In this it is unlike rnanv
minor places of amusements such as small local
concert halls which have apparently been designed
tor hen roosts or sewing bees. The Imperial maple
floor is as perfect for roller skating as experts can
make it. Under its milk-white surface three sub-
floors or catacombs (to bury the loud noises of many
leet) give an exhilarating spring to the roller-
wheels, and assist the beginner to fall softly over
his own feet. A thousand skaters glide comfortably
°y e r fhis Tinker’s dream of delight. On the opening l
night Youth and Beauty swished round under a
starry firmament of electric bulbs, to the strains of
a fine orchestra.

Stargazer’ : Hugh-and-Cry Mclntosh’s Beni Zoug
Zoug Arabs have discovered how the stirring of an
audience may be accelerated and amplified by weird
sounds. These nimble Africans toss themselves
through the atmosphere with much variety and aston-
ishing velocity. Yet their turn would gang in dead
silence but for the unusual interjections of the full
strength of the company. When a man is not rip-
ping across space, like a disordered comet, he is
standing in the wings, assisting to make unchristian
clamor, with the result that an acrobatic act takes
on the atmosphere of a national jamboree of South
Sea cannibals, and the blood of the audience leaps
and bounds in half-anticipation of a terrible sight
that will end in the smallest Arab being bitten to
death by his rabid companions.
“J.S.R.”: I fancy I can see what G. Shaw’s
trouble is when he has to circularise Big Fog audi-
ences as to how to behave. The Shaw play being
now quite the correct thing in alleged smart circles,
all sorts of affluent but wooden-headed persons turn
up, and in order to profess that they can see the
joke, force themselves to laugh like all the hyenas
on earth. They don’t see the joke, but think that
they ought to. Hence the unnatural and distracting
noises. No Shaw play appeals to any irresponsible
emotion in a way to compel a demonstration. Shaw
tickles the mind with subtleties for internal consump-
tion, and not outward explosion.
“Snell”: From “topical allusions,” good Lord de-
liver us! We complain of the vapid trash that
comes to us from far lands, but the imported bosh
does not lie so heavy upon me as the imbecility made
on the premises by Antipodean experts. Take the
case of “The Grafters,” at Melbourne Bijou. It
came as a company of strangers to a strange land,
and it was infamously beguiled into spotting its in-
tensely American burlesque with trifling Australian
gags, glaringly foreign, and pitifully weak. If one
cannot rise above bald references to O’Malley’s hat
or crude allus ; ons to the baby bonus, in Mercy’s
name be dumb!

Why? 1 saw an Australian film last night. It re-

with some unusual horses and a few sloppy per-

dont recognise it there, and so we don’t get the
same shock.

(Queckett) and Gwenn (Admiral Rankling). Wini-
fred Emery came to Australia with her father, that
excellent comedian Sam Emery, son of a great one
John, of the same ilk. Sam and WSnifred, now Mrs’
Cyril Maude, were so hard run in Melbourne that thev
were reduced to acting at L. L. Smith’s Polytechnic
m isourke-street. Sam’s Peggotty was a fine per-

Harniet. Boucicault is, of course, our old friend

Schoolmistress” in Australia. Mrs. Brough was the
schoolmistress, Brough acted Queckett, and Charley
Brown was Admiral Rankling. He never gloated
more over a part, which is saying a good deal, for
he was a great gloater. Brough’s Queckett was great.
Vou could never surpass Brough as a puzzled person.
Boucicault is a pastmaster of technique, but Brough
had the finer sense of humor and the face to paint
it on.
Back in Australia, after nearly seven
years divided between Paris and London,
H, S. Power, the Adelaide painter. Always
preferring animals as his subject, he has
made, big cheques latterly in England, doing
favorite horses and hunt-meets for country
gentlemen—including the Speaker of the
Commons and King Edw-ard, who gave a
special commission for an Australian bul-
lock-team. The artist proposes to give one-
man Power exhibitions in Sydney and Mel-
bourne, as well as Adelaide.
Died at Pakenham (Vic.), aged 78, Francis
Parry Atkinson, who has had his fingers in
printers’ ink hereabouts for 59 years. He took
eight months to come out in a 102-ton
barque. When he had got over the motion
of the ocean he joined first the Melbourne
Hebald and, later, the Abgus. Thence he
went to Maoriland, and presently rose to the
eminence of Government Printer of Fiji.
Back again in Melbourne, he toiled in the
Government Printing Office, and there de-
signed the first postal note that Victoria ever
issued. He also HANSABD-ed to some extent.
Though Victoria’s disastrous land boom
of a generation ago .is almost forgotten, a
few of its little relics are still being put
away to. rest. One such was finally covered
up in discreet silence recently in the shape
of a declaration of a first and final divi-
dend of 23-40ths of a penny in the £ in the
estate of the late Dr. George Lefevre. The
estate, when sequestrated in ’93, showed lia-
bilities £113,000, assets £83,433; but,
through the subsequent drop in values, it
only pans out a halfpenny in the pound. It
~ hardly worth all these years of bother.

THE TRULY GREAT ACTOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The actor of 20 years ago was better than the actor
of to-day.— Mr. Walter Bentley before a Wages Board.
The actor of the other day
Mas everything that heart could wish.
He had a manner and a way,
And gave his splendid tail a swish
Wheh striding histrionic boards,
And dealing with the ruffian hordes.
His roaring shook the mimic scene,
Expressing all of human woe.
Imposing was his brow and mien,
And awesomely he dragged his toe.
Then his digestion, too, was good—
If need arose he fed on wood.
His art was serious, intense.
Well drilled he was. He had to learn
With action fine to suit the sense.
He did his Hamlet to a turn ;
And pretty ladies loved him so,
Some five-and-thifty years ago.
The actor of the olden time
In all his parts was truly great.
In diction he was quite sublime,
In gesture so appropriate.
His like the world will never see.
I ought to know, for I am he!
VIC.

From last Saturday’s S. M. Herald :
In the party are four singers, namely, Messrs. P.
J. Gilbert (tenor), P. H. Metcalf (tenor), C. M.
Keeler (baritone), and E. W. Peck (bass) ; and they
gave a taste of their quality. So pleased were their
hearers that an encore was vociferously demanded.
The verve oi F. B. Smith (their leader) is both
physical and spiritual. In gymnasium trunks he
would look like a pugilist or a wrestler. His
shoulders resemble the crags of a mountain, and his
legs are of amazing circumference. At college he
rowed in the regular crews. He also boxed with his
pile-driver fists. Trained, he ought to be able to
throw a horse and pull a loaded waggon uphill, etc.
The stuff about the singers is not, as might
be imagined, the work of one of Hugh D.

Mclntosh’s publicity agents. F. B. Smith
is not a Stadium star newly-imported by
Snowy Baker. It is simply the preliminary
whoop of the la' est band of Yankee evan-

The French had a lovely windfall last
week, when the latest German war-Zepoe-
lin, lost in a fog, descended in a fortified
French area and was immediately seized by
the military. The Germans on board were
in uniform, and the air ship was of the
latest pattern and had quite a number of
secret devices. They are secret no longer,
the French having made the most of their
opportunity. The German air ships hold
the world’s record for long-distance travel-
ling, and British and French engineers have
been quite unable to unravel the mystery of
their success. Now the Frenchmen know
anyhow, and the applause is something tre-
mendous.
Two cases from the Sydney Police Courts
this week by way of keeping up their repu-
tation :—•
Edward Snowden and James Walker were sentenced
at the Central Court to six months’ imprisonment on
a charge of stealing 54ft. of rubber hose from the
Glebe Public School.
Archibald Palmer, a dairyman living at Rook-
wood, was charged at the Children’s Court, Parra-
matta, with cruelty to a ward of the State. Dr.
Brown, on the witness-stand, said it was the worst
case he had ever seen. He thought the bruises had
been caused by the lad being hit with the handle of
a whip as well as the thong. The lad must have
suffered a great deal, and he had been in the Parra-
matta Hospital for 10 days. The dairyman was
fined £lO, in default three months’ imprisonment.
Which is eloquent of the difference in value
between rubber hose and mere cow-slaves.

One of the original houses built on Syd-
ney’s “Golden Corner” (King and George
Streets) by Joe Wyatt, in 1834, is to come
down, Waters, Limited, being about to re-
build. Nearly opposite is the last bit of
“Old Sydney” in the locality—a house at
one time occupied by Woolley,
an ironmonger, and afterwa •ds
the Golden Fleece Hotel. E\ ry
place in old Sydney seems. to
have been a hotel at one time
or another. In 1834 Wyatt
gave £55 10s. a foot for the
George-street frontage of the
corner —hence “Golden Corner,”
because the price was then
reckoned prodigous. It would be worth that
much per half-inch now. #
In its comments (2/4/’l3) on the ap-
pointment of Justice Rich to the High
Court Bench, Sydney Telegraph demon-
strated, with exceptional force, its Plaza
Toro-like capacity for leading public opinion
—from behind. An extract: —
The High Court, it cannot be repeated too often,
is not only the highest legal arbiter within the Com-
monwealth, but the warden of the Federal Constitu-
tion. If its members are not to be as capable law-
yers as ive have, it had better hot exist. So far,
they have been among our best, and with this latest
appointment there is nothing to find fault. But
with the course of action which led up to it, it is
another matter. Two out of the three High Court
appointments were such as to suggest that they had
not been made with that care which a Government,
actuated by the sole desire of strengthening the
Bench, would have exercised. One appointee has re-
signed, and the Federal Government has now in that
case corrected its mistake. But what about the other.
One who was unacquainted with the rail-
sitting, Jump Jim Crow methods of our
local Tory dailies might imagine from the
above that the Telegraph had been hostile
to the Piddington-Powers appointments from
the start. What were the facts? The
’Graph announced those appointments on
February 14 last. It allowed Attorney-
General Hughes to say of Mr. Piddington
in an interview :—-
He was a man of the highest capacity, with a pro-
found knowledge of law. He also had in a marked
degree the calm and judicial temperament necessary
for the high and responsible duties of a justice, and
was a man of great force of character. He would
prove himself worthy to rank along with those dis-
tinguished men who have hitherto exercised the
functions of High Court Judges.
On its own behalf it mentioned Mr. Pidding-
ton’s “brilliant career at Sydney Univer-
sity,” and his signal success at the N. S.
Wales Bar. And for Mr. Powers it had
nothing but praise. During a month or
more, while this paper and others were
strenuously denouncing the new appoint-
ments as an insult to the High Court and
to the collective intelligence of the electors,
the King-street organ lay low. And now,
when the shouting and tumult have died
away, to all intents and purposes, it takes
up a very resolute attitude indeed, and says
in effect that the appointments which seemed
to it good two months ago are really rotten.
* * * *
According to the decision of an Adelaide
court, the “entirely given up” of the baby-
advertisements means, legally, just nothing
at all. In this case, the mother paid £l5
before the birth of the child to have it taken
off her hands for ever, and signed an agree-
ment to that effect. But when it was a
month or two old she went to visit it, grew
fond of it, and finally carried it off. The
foster-mother —a highly respectable woman
—claimed 19 guineas for her damaged affec-
tions and six guineas for the pram, in which
the infant travelled; but the Court, while
allowing that she had “all the merits of the
case,” said that the Law does not allow a
child to be “sold,” and she could claim for
nothing but sums out of pocket, and the £l5
should cover that. Tableau. Re-union of
mother and cheeild. Foster-mother in back-
ground paying Costs of both lawyers. Cur-
tain.
MUTUAL EXPECTATIONS.
HE: “ You know I expect to
be rich what the old man
pegs out."
SHE: " So do I; he proposed
to me this morning."
One of the first official acts of Victoria’s
new Police Commissioner should be to invite
excuses from a plain-clothes constable whom
a P.M. lately stated had committed a “cruel
and unjustifiable act” in publicly telling a
fruit-hawker that he “did not wish to speak
to him as he was a convicted thief.” As the
hawker had but one mark against him, and
that was nearly 10 years old, most men
would have allowed the dust to remain un-
disturbed. But the Victorian “force” seems
to act on the principle that, if it can’t catch
new criminals, it can at least goad old ones
into activity. The P.M. promptly dismissed
the charge of “insulting language” against
the hawker. He had called the Law s
minion a “cowardly blackguard.” Has
Sainsbury much use for officers who can be
called cowardly blackguards with impunity?
Dr. Green, who bishes for a crust at Bal-
larat, has just maintained the high level of
inaccuracy reached in most clerical utter-
ances. Said he: “The Christian Church
recognises no lawful dissolution of marriage
for any cause whatever, except the one
specified by Christ Himself, i.e., adultery.”
As a matter of fact, the Greek Church,
which numbers something over 100,000,000
adherents, recognises a whole heap of rea-
sons other than post-nuptial giddiness. Also,
the Scotchbyterian Kirk allows divorce for
desertion, and reckons it has Scriptural
authority for doing so. Since Dr. Green is nre-
sumably aware of these facts, it would
seem that he regards everybody outside the
Anglican sect as heathens, whose chances of
bliss beyond the undertaker are not worth
considering.
From the Oldest Inhabitant:—
More history! S.M. Herald, in an article on
birth of the Bank of N S. Wales, says that Robert
Lowe, who afterwards became Lord Sherbrooke and
was Chancellor of the Exchequer in England, was one
of the original shareholders. The bank was launched
in March, 1817. Robert Lowe was born in 1811
Apparently, therefore, this “original” shareholder was
just six years of age when he became an investor
As a matter of fact, Mr. Lowe didn’t arrive in Svd
ney till 1842. Robert Lowe, of Birling, Bringellv
may have been an original shareholder; but the
Bringelly Lowe was quite a different person from the
great albino financier who proposed to tax matches
and cats.
“Quidam”: In the Russo-Jap.
war the European nation did no-
thing to write home about in the
way of applying science to the
saving of soldiers’ lives. But the
Japanese, having grasped the
great principles of sanitation and
antiseptic treatment, applied
them so thoroughly that in all the
annals of war there never was so
little death, and never did such
a large proportion of wounded get
back to the firing line. In their
navy, the sailors were compelled
to take carbolic baths and put on
sterilised clothes before an en-
gagement, so that no germs
should be carried into wounds by
projectiles, themselves sterilised
by their lightning rush through
the air. Their turning -of the
teaching of sanitary science to
their furthest possible use was
the principal factor in the Japan-
ese success, so much so that the
saying that “Lister won the
war” is current among scientific
observers. All of which is merely
preliminary to the question whether this re-
quirement is pushed far enough to the front
in our own scheme of Defence. The writer,
for instance, recently heard a military in-
structor in a Victorian town —and a person
who thinks no small beer of himself, too-
stating dogmatically that water that had
filtered through Bft. of earth must be abso-
lutely devoid of disease, germ, and, therefore,
necessarily safe for drinking. That a man
whose job it is to teach our youngsters
how to keep themselves alive while
making it unpleasant for the other party
should have such a pitiably ancient belief
makes one think hard. To send a soldier
out into a campaign armed with pre-historic
lore of this sort is bound to result in his
rapidly becoming a dead soldier, and dead
soldiers are the kind that win campaigns for
the other side.
A beautifully-planned scheme for an alibi
went wrong the other day, all through that
inconvenient person, the man with a mem-
ory ; and now one Smith, of Melbourne, is
lamenting his wasted ingenuity. Recently
Smith, having transacted certain matters
with a Caulfield business woman, returned
to the city per train with her. Knowing
she was going home again by the next train,
Smith hired a swift taxi, whipped out to
Caulfield in 15 minutes, annexed some
jewellery he had noticed near a convenient
windowq and landed back in town about the
same time as the lady was entering her
house. The scheme was elegantly worked
out, and her visitor was the last person she
suspected; but the chauffeur, seeing the
report of the robbery, remembered his fares
haste to reach Caulfield and his swift
return, and mentioned the circumstance.
The rest was easy.

SUITABLE REFORM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Barrister Maxwell, addressing the Indeterminate
Sentences Board, in Melbourne, the other day, de-
clared the penal system of Victoria to be “rotten.”
“Every circumstance in Pentridge tended to crush the
individuality of a man.” He advised taking prisoners
away from everything suggestive of their downfall.
He would give them decent clothes, “ even going so
far as to allow them to pick their own pattern and
choose their own ‘cut.’ ”
We know the tailor makes the man —
This much has often been admitted.
The color, pattern and the plan
Of vests and coats and trousers can
Do much to elevate the bloke
Who’s sadly doing time in choke.
No chap is good or nimble-witted
Who’s suit is cheap and badly fitted.
So give the burglar nice white ties,
And evening togs in which to sup, too,
The penal system that is wise
Provides smart shoes, the proper size,
And saucy bonnets —little loves! —
And natty, lemon-yellow gloves.
The girls should have a swagger pup, too,
Of breeding—something to live up to.
The well-dressed man can do no wrong;
Beau Brummels don’t break into houses.
So let the convicts go it strong
In skirted coats, cut wide and long
(Fine feathers make fine bird 3, we’re told).
Then if a fellow gets too bold,
Embezzles, burgles, or carouses,
Straight hang the man who made his “trouzes!”

Lawyers give good advice occasionally. A
Melbourne citizen lately limped into the
casualty room at the General Hospital and
exhibited a leg showing a gaping wound
which looked like the climax of an argu-
ment with a shark. “Dog bit it,” he said
laconically. “Went to a lawyer. He
advised me to come here.”

“Hullo”: I am thinking of starting a cor-
respondence course in Bottle Messages. The
usual dead marine came ashore the other day
with a pencil note in
his innards : “We are
lost; there is no
hop e. —G.W.E., S.S.
Waratah.” It is like
all the other bottle
messages. It gives no
details, it is signed
merely with any ini-
tials that the writer
found handy, it sends
no dying message to
wife or relation, it
gives no directions for
rescue. In fact, it is,
like its tribe, as vague
as a spirit message
from Stead. If a pas-
senger on the Wara-
tah wanted to send a
message, he would ad-
dress it properly,
would indicate what
had struck the vessel
and what chance there
was of rescue, would
state the geographical
position of the ship,
and, above all, would
carefully sign in full.
But, simply because
m y correspondence
class has not been
started, the practical
jokers 'who pursue this
profession never add a
single human touch.
I guarantee that a
three-weeks’ course
would equip any stu-
dent to compose a
message that would thrill the world. Lists
of passengers and crews of all lost vessels
are supplied with the course, also specimen
letters to cover every conceivable catastrophe
at sea, and 18 more.
When Panos Bollieros came to Melbourne
to sella da banan’ he brought with him sweet
memories of Theodora Dendiros, whom he
had left behind in Greece, where old Ther-
mopylae used to be, and where the klephts
grow. As soon as he had settled down, he
wrote to Theodora and invited her to come
and marry him —and take the shop for her
trouble. She came. But before she
had; come, another damsel had stolen
Bollieros’ heart —and then closed on a
rival’s offer of an icecream cart. Bol-
lieros was thereby so upset that when Theo.
came he wouldn’t marry her, let alone give
her the fruit shop. She didn’t trouble about
Bollieros, but she went to law about the fruit
shop, and got a verdict for £3OO. Mistrustful
of courts. Panos had got in early and sold
the shop for £6O and cleared out. But Greek
had met Greek. Miss Dendiros sooled on the
law, which grabbed him, brought him back,
and told him to pay up at the rate of £4 per
month —in default three months’ hard. At
latest, he was inquiring the whereabouts of
the man who said that there were points
about having loved and lost. He wants to
argue the case with him —with special refer-
ence to a profitable banan’ shop. To love
two wopien, and lose both of them, also a
steady business and a lawsuit and £3OO, is a
one-sided transaction.
LUCID.
“Hello, Joe, hear you're on strike .”
“ Yes.”
"That are you out for?”
"Dunno! But we're not going back to work
till we get it.”
Broken Hill has been enjoying another
strike, strikes and processions being almost
its only diversions. The trouble this time
started on the Silverton Co.’s railway—the
one link between the big mining field and the
outer world—and that meant the closing-
down of the mines as soon as the ore-bins
were full. Broken Hill almost scorns to
strike over such a sordid matter as extra
pay: nearly all its outbursts have been over
some showy frill or other. This time it was
over the employment of an alleged non-union-
ist here and there. The unionists, declaring
that they were tired of battling for benefits
that non-unionists shared in, gave all hands
till April 1 to come in ; and practically all
hands came in to save trouble. The explo-
sion happened mainly because a few petty
bosses didn’t reckon that they should . be
branded and belong to the mob. It is annoy-
ing, no doubt, that a man should get benefits
that others who are no better off pay for;
but the trouble about roping in members
wholesale under threats of various sorts is
that the unions become filled with people
who are not unionists. Fpr two or three
years before the Broken Hill strike which
lasted five months and ended in utter defeat,
the mining cos. conceded the point that all
hands should be members of a union; but it
didn’t take long after the strike lasted to find
out that a whole pack of them weren’t union-
ists. To rope everybody in is all right; to
settle down comfortably after everybody is in
is simply suicide.
A RHYME OF THE TROUGH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Persons who dine in some of the citv restaurants
ar- of:.en reminded of the that 'others have
dined before them. Some months ago regulations
were gazetted with the view of securing the cleanli-
ness and freedom from contamination of articles of
food. Persons who read the regulations, however,
are firmly of opinion that they are not generally
enforced. — Aft lbourne Arcus.
What time our Greek restaurateur serves up his
splendid scran,
Is there a citizen would stoop to sniff his frying-
pan ?
When scents of rich, well-seasoned food come warmly
on the air,
Say, why should eighteenpencers peer too closely at
their fare?
What though the linen’s overripe, the fish-forks
edged with green ?
All’s well ! The regulations say our feedware must
be clean
Though things may not pleasant where the meat-
fly builds her nest,
And where the cupboard-rat is bold, and where great
* spiders rest
Among the long-discarded bones of old, forgotten
ham,
And where, in summer-time, a myriad ants explore
for jam,
And where the cockroach hath its lair, beneath grim
tins of fat—
Let be! The regulations deal with everything like
that.
What though young oysters, newly scalped, upon old
oyster-shells
Recline? What if the butter-dish in sweetness scarce
excels ?
What though the Dago maul new bread with rude,
microbey mits ?
Although on ware washed yesteryear the cheese of
ages sits
Droning the same old dirge of burial-day awaited
long ?
It s quite all right! You see, the regulations say
it’s wrong.
Smeared china, shady tablecloths and sluggish salad-
ing,
Wild kitchens all gummed up with what the chang-
ing seasons bring,
Frayed food, matured potato-peel, with cobwebs
high and low,
Soiled aprons underneath the stove, since some cooks
like ’em so,
Bacteria in the bread-bin, tame germs busy in the
grime?
Who cares? The regulations now protect us every
time 1

A queen am I.
My palace boasts a hundred carven rooms;
A chain of jewels clicks whene’er I move,
And they must fear me. . .even when they 10ve...
The smirking crew that pander for my smite.
And all the while
I long to lie
Hearing a bird-song buffetting the breeze
Or the Green Harper stealing through the trees
Face down, among the cool, wet violet blooms...
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Art Society’s Exhibition.
This year s exhibition of cabinet pictures,
from the brushes of members of the Royal
Ait Society of New South Wales, was
opened to a breathless and impatient
public at the Pitt-street (Sydney)
rooms this week; and the solitary ex-
cuse for it that this*-* deponent can
discover is a portion of the water-color
department. A strong foundation of inter-
est and charm is built up there by Arthur
Burgess, Lister-Bister, Percy Spence, James
Lnsp, D. G. Reid, Chas. Tindall and J. A.
Bennett. But the oils section is as deplor-
able as the representations of truth and
honesty done by the hired liars of the anti-
Australia party. Burgess, with three marine
pictures of workman-like touch, makes most
or the others look like clumsy students,
iercy Spence obtains exemption from this
®P IDIOII with a phantasy which he has titled
Evensong of the Sea.” The face and
figure of the girl (presumably Davy Jones’s
most attractive choir lady) are painted with
eil n lC i skill* but the large, dead hunks of
rolled-up sea, also the uncompromising, tidy
bubbles, are so unsatisfactory as to call for
the services of.. a furniture-removing con-
tractor. James R. Jackson, too, puts a little
spirit into one or two of his small land-
scapes, but not enough to soften their unnat-
urai reserve. YY ith a few other minor excep-
tions the remainder of the work is crude,
with a particular weakness for falsity of
coloring. The difference of opinion, for
instance, as to the exact shade and amount
of washing blue employed when the waters
of 1 ort Jackson were mixed, is bewildering.
The most striking result in the exhibition
has been obtained by Burgess with the
water-color, “Pacific Shores.” It palpitates
with that spirit of the South Seas which
made even the bald-headed suburban readers
of Stevenson and Louis Becke regret the fate
that led them from a career of blackbirding,
treasure-seeking, pirating or plain devilry.
Off the low shore lies a white, silent, adven-
ture-hinting schooner. Along the inevitable
reef stretches the far line of lazy surf.
Between it and the white beach, the clear
water. Above and below the unforgettable
tropic blue of the sea and sky. And, impreg-
nating all, the heat haze of the sun-high
hour. The effect is striking, but a closer
appreciation of the work is impossible owing
to the disheartening light on that side of
the gallery. The same artist has two fine
pictures of the Australian war-ship Mel-
bourne, which are similarly handicapped.
Close by are Lister-Lister’s set of ’scapes,
each characteristically pleasing. There are
12 of them, which is a risk to take with
familiar goods on a dull market. In the
same locality a fine richness of color relieves
the otherwise Christmas-almanacky effect of
“The Rose,” by Percy Spence, and his talent
for color treatment is well matched by
another old hand, .T. A. Bennett, whose pair
of heads, “Esther” and “Nancy,” are most
pleasantly reproduced. And, of course,
there are specimens of James Crisp’s mag-
pies—no R.A.S. show would be complete
without them. Also, honorable mention
must be delivered to D. G. Reid and Chas.
Tindall. Others range from fair to medium,
with a downward tendency. In the black
and white section a couple of etchings
(“Steps, Gloucester Street,” for preference)
by Alfred Coffey stand out amongst a weak
lot. But the many failures of the exhibi-
tion are no excuse for not seeing the few
successes. Rather are they an urgent appeal
for a heap more encouragement—especially
from the unpatriotic Affluent.


A GREAT IDEA.
Now, turn to the typical Labor leader or other such demagogue of the so-called “advanced” or “progressive” sort. Is not the great secret of his success his facility
in the manufacture of political bogies, in which he persuades the unthinking workers to believe. —Melbourne Argus.
The Argus, walking abroad in profound meditation, has a brilliant idea. “Bless me soul, why ever didn’t I think of it afore?
I’ll accuse the Laborites of breeding bogies, so I will.”

WAITRESS: “Bill of fare, sir?"
MULGA JIM: “I pays no bill till I gets my feed!”
“Ned McNicholl” : Can sympathise with
“Kimbolton” (B. 6/3/’l3) for cursing at mules.
Only knew one of them, but she taught me the
entire mule family’s box of tricks. She carried
more swags and more tucker from Bulloo Downs
than any BDI horse, and kept fat on nothing when
the horses were dying in “the corner.” She was old
when I knew her—old in years, older in sin—and her
name was Jenny (of course), and there’s not a
pack-horse cook that ever left Bulloo Downs for
Adelaide with Jones, Green and Sullivan cattle
that Jenny hasn’t kicked, bitten or otherwise mal-
treated. If all the language that has been hurled
. ~ , . j t A
at that animal were collected in one blaze it would
scorch the whiskers of the stars. Dunno whether
she is alive now, but, if she is, she is carrying
swags for Kidman; and if she is really and truly
dead (that is if a “mewil” ever does die) 4 I’ll bet
“Kim” two drinks that she’s kicking, biting and
making trouble for a phantom cook on one of
David Jones’s cattle camps.
“Ginger”: For “Hager” (B. 13/3/’l3) to
say that wheat-threshing is harder toil than chaff-
cutting is simply a method of directing public atten-
tion to his igr.orance and inexperience. Thresher
work is hotter and dustier, but easier, than chaff-
cutting, particularly where the elevators can be
lifted high enough to do away with the pitch-hole.
And if “Rager” humps his drum over Cooiamon
(N.S.W.) way, he’ll find the chaff-bag contains
more stitches than the Chapman sack of wheat. “L.
Soup” (same issue) writes with a local and limited
knowledge of the game. None, of the cutters quoted
by him (bar one) are in the “gun” class. Several
others get through from 600 bags a day upwards.
Mackey’s will run a thousand bags through in a
full day; and Chandler, Logan’s sewer, will sew
and stack that many. And there are others who
can do the same. I’ve followed the cutter in the
five States, and the Ganmain-Coolamon men are the
ringers of the Commonwealth. About those 27
stitches. If Logan, whom I’ve mentioned before, saw
his sewer turn out a bag with only 10 stitches and
two “lugs,” he’d bash the aforesaid sewer with a
boiled gohanna or something. But why wrangle
over a few stitches or a few bags either way, and
lose sight of the more important fact that the bag-
sewer does the solid yakker of two men for 10£
hours a day for the wages of one? It is up to some-
body to pass the cutter-boss an Irish whisper that
it is rank sweating, and that the way of the sweater
is distasteful to all good Australaian blokes, coves,
etc.

“Konini” : Another historical record went
up in smoke recently at Gisborne (M.L.). This was
(or was claimed to be) the carved wooden bucket
used on the “big” canoe—presumably the Arawa- —
on the voyage from Hawaiki to what is now Maori-
land. If it was the genuine implement, it was no
doubt used to bail the canoe out when the craft
looked as though it was going to be en-
gulfed in “the steep descent where the
world ends.” All, of course, because of the
eternal woman. Tama-te-kapua, when he took
the Arawa away from Hawaiki, also took the wife
of one Ruaeo, an attention which somewhat jarred
Rua. The latter speedily got to work with some of his
devilments, and about the same time the same un-
canny things were set in motion by another gent,
aboard the canoe, whose wife had also strongly at-
tracted the tropical Tama. Between them the Arawa
was rushed into a whirlpool, so that the water was
almost instantly over the bows. It was the pitiful
crying of the women and children that saved the
party. The aggrieved tohunga aboard found his
heart softening to the wailing, wherefore he stood up
and spat on his hands, so to speak, and changed the
aspect of the heavens. The storm ceased, the canoe
was hauled out of the whirlpool by incantation, and
all sailed on peacefully to Aotea. That was only
one of the strange things that happened on the
big canoe, but it is not nearly so strange as the
saving of one of the buckets to be burned at Gis-
borne when a small house fired. I must admit that
that part of the business appears somewhat doubtful
to me.
“N. or M.”: Here’s another unusual way
of capturing fish, as told of Binghi by the late-
lamented Martin Cash, ex-bushranger, Van Diemen’s
Land, in his Memoirs. He relates that while he
was stock-keeping on the Hunter River (N.S.W.),
he saw the natives obtain a plentiful supply of
mullet, which were never known to bite at the
hook, in the following manner:—
They procure a quantity of the lignum vitae bark,
which they chop into shreds with their tomahawks;
they then dig a hole, in which they make a fire,
afterwards taking out the ashes and filling the hole
with the bark, placing a layer of ashes on the top.
After subjecting it to this process for about an
hQur they J tak e o ut the bark and put in into small
nets, with which they plunge into the waterholes,
an d in a few minutes the mullet appear on the sur-
face and are thrown out on the bank without the
slightest difficulty.
Has any Abo.-reader heard of this method being
employed, or did the Irishman with the financial
name imagine it ?
FISHING.
“What are yer fishing tor, mate?"
“ Oh, anything I can catch." (After a pause) "What are you?”
“Scrapiron”: I observe that “Cavalry-
man” comes back to his charge against the Aus-
tralian as a horseman, and tells “F. Brown” that
he (Brown) doesn’t know what he talks about.
Brown’s talk was to the effect that “Caval-
ryman” was a plumber. I’m with Brown ;
and on behalf of a number of bushmen here-
abouts (which is Somewhere Away to Blazes
out of Winton, N.Q.) I desire to tell the
said “Cavalryman” that his remarks are
rubbish, particularly when he says that on
sheep stations 90 p.c. of the inhabitants
never see a horse that a child couldn't ride.
He will be telling us next that he has seen
the monkey-dodgers mustering sheep in sul-
kies, as according to him the sheepman rides
mostly in buggies, sulkies or on bicycles.
Now, I am a sheepman, and have lived
among sheep, horses and cattle since I could
toddle, so I guess that I ought to know
something about the game. And if “Caval-
ryman” will saunter out here we will oblige
him with a mount a child can’t ride, but
which is, nevertheless, used to do station
work. I won’t say anything about the Eng-
lish fox-following riders because I’ve met
only two of them; but if they were a fair
sample of the fraternity, “Cavalryman” can
have my share of the lot when it comes to
really and truly riding. If these station
workers Outback aren’t horsemen, then I’m
afraid there are no horsemen left. We get
all sorts of blokes from Europe and Eng-
land, and some of them can ride a bit, I ad-
mit ; but I’ll bet a small bundle they would,
in their turn, be the first to admit how
much they can learn from the Australian
Billjim.
“Ginger” : My cobber Nugget is, like my-
self, a mere specimen of the plain, common or vege-
table variety of swagmen, but he is an artist in his
line. Here is a list of the articles Nugget rolls up
every morning into just a decent-sized swag: one 6 x
8 tent, one pair double blankets, two suits, one pair
trousers, one working hat, one cap, one pair boots,
one hair brush, one clothes do., one b’acking do.,
one tin cobra, two flannels, three pair socks, one
towel, six collars, four neckties, four fishing lines,
six cod hooks, one mosquito net, one writing pad,
one packet envelopes, one pen and ink, four photo-
graphs, one bundle letters, one bottle painkiller, one
needle case, one reel thread, two pipes, three candles,
one pair scissors, one box pills, four shirts, two
packets tape, one pillow, one razor, one shaving
brush, one camp mirror. He is adding thereto a
rug and a rain-proof macintosh. The commiss-
ariat department, divided into two bags (one over
each shoulder), consists of tea, sugar, flour, salt, one
flour bread-brownie, mutton, one tin pepper, one tin
curry, a few onions and spuds. A frying-pan, pint
and two billycans complete the outfit. I put the
clock on him the other morning, and he rolled up
the whole caravan in a tick under two' minutes. If
a woman can be found to cram the above list into
the same space I’m prepared to rat on the Labor
party and vote Vida Goldstein for the Senate.
“Chops and Steaks”: Our cow-cocky
friend could tell us something useful in this meat
versus vegetable controversy. The cow, a vegetarian,
grinds her food; she does practically no biting.
A BLOKE’S LUCK.
FIRST BEER CHEWER : “Money torks orlright.
Blokes like us never have a charust in this
world."
SECOND DITTO : “ That's Gord's truth, mister.
Now Urn the champeen beer drinker of
Orstralia, but I carn't get enuff beer to
prove it.”
The cocky friend will know that. Carnivorous dog, pn
the other hand, doesn’t grind at all. Simply because
he can’t. Man is intermediate between the cow and
the dog. His teeth are adapted for both actions —in-
cisors for cutting, molars for grinding. This alone is
sufficient proof of the fact that he was designed to
handle a mixed diet. Again, the amylolytic
(starch-splitting) ferment, ptyalin, found in human
saliva, is totally absent in herbivora. It’s no use
tooting individual cases of men waxing sinewy and
colossal on the spud-celery diet. • They’re mere
diddling deceptions. Of course, if a person’s com-
missariat somersaults at the sight of steak, his
only commonsense course is to turn vegetarian.
But to start preaching it as a doctrine for universal
application is quite another thing.
PHILOSOPHER CHING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
None of these calamities would have happened had
we only been guarded by the precepts of Philosopher
Ching. —Pekin Gazette.
Why did we stray from the path that was straight,
Losing the ticket we held for the Gate?
Why did we marry the wrong sort of girl,
Getting our life (and our hair) out of curl?
Why did we drop into ways that were lax,
Wand’ring to Tophet by devious tracks?
Why did we sigh when existence should sing ?
Why did we turn a deaf ear unto Ching?
Where was he living and what was his caper?
When did he burn out his studious taper?
Was he in youth just a giddy young spark,
In for all mischief and out for a lark?
Did he make love in a voice that was fluty,
Swearing his almond eyes swam with her beauty?
Did he turn sage when he’d had a mad fling,
Or was he born Philosopher Ching?
Sooth, but I know not. Yet well had it been
Could we bring back the old man to the scene;
Cut off his pigtail (as China does now),
Gaze with respect on his deep-furrowed brow ;
Model our lives on the lines that he gave,
Worship our parents or weep on their grave ;
Drain from his chalice a draught of the spring
Whence came the wisdom that bubbled in Ching.
Had he been boss of the Council or Curia,
Ne’er had the White Devil gone with Manchuria ;
Had but the nous that was under his cap
Lighted our way, we had beaten the Jap ;
Had he but guided old Buddha Tzu-sche
Down her long journey, how happy were we!
Samisens joyful with ting-a-ling-ting
Ring out the praise of Philosopher Ching!
True that his precepts are shrouded in maziness ;
True, his career is beclouded in haziness;
Though he was reverenced so highly by most of ’em,
Reasons for worship we find not the ghost of ’em.
Still, had we lived by his “Counsels that Bless,”
What we had risen to no one can guess;
And you’ll admit that a doubt is a thing
No decent person could have about Ching!
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

TERRIBLE BURN ON PALM OF HAND.
“One night I struck a match and the whole box
went off in my hand, burning me across the palm,”
says Mr. J. R. Worseley, Margate (Tas.). “The pain
was awful. I went to the store and looked round for
something to relieve me. I saw Chamberlain’s Pain
Balm, and noticed the word ‘burns’ on the label.

“Bro”: Old Pathrick Maguire lay dying,
and friends and relatives were assembled at his bed-
side to speed his soul with prayer. In the midst of
the devotions the dying man gave one deep groan,
sighed, then lay back on his pillow. In the com-
motion that ensued, Pathrick, jun., collapsed into
the capable arms of widow Mclnerny. Someone
promptly rushed for the bottle of stimulants and
held it to the fainting man’s lips. Then a feeble
voice whispered from the bed: “Be sure, Pathrick,
and lave a little dhrop for th’ mornin’!”

“F. M. Clements”: The “pigface” (Me-
sembryanthemum /Equilaterale) referred to by your
“L. 8.” (since dead) and “Coola” (B. 6/3/’l3) is
the goonah of the Hottentots and the canagong of
the Australian aborigine. It is also eaten as a kind
of spinach by the Maoris. It has slightly astringent
properties, and some varieties —especially M. Crys-
tallimim —are rich in soda. As goonah it is largely
used by the South African natives as a remedy for
sore throats, insect bites, abrasions and inflammatory
conditions generally. Such therapeutic value as it
possesses is principally due to the alkaline consti-
tuents.

“Arm”: Of all the curious ways that
black and brown brother has of enticing fish into
the cupboard I have long ago concluded that lasso-
ing them is on its solitary. That’s how they catch
crayfish and prawns along the Kemp AVelch River,
Papua, using palm fibre for the lasso. It seems a
tedious and slow process, but if you saw a couple
of dozen youngsters fishing a pool you would be
surprised at the result. Another method is the use
of a creeper root to “poison” or stupefy the fish.
This root is crushed between stones and thrown into
the waterholes, where it emits a milky juice which,
mixing with the water, in an hour or so brings
every fish gasping to the surface, where the bre-
thren easily gather them in with a net not unlike
a butterfly-catcher, but made of spider's web instead
of cord.

“S.S.S.”: He was a wizened old canni-
bal, clad in a microscopic grass rami, and adorned
with strings of dogs’ teeth. Round his neck hung
a pendant that looked uncommonly like a man’s
hand. This son of Satan squatted on the plat-
form of an alligator-shaped rami, 60ft.
high and 150 ft. long, chewing betel and
occasionally licking his lime stick, and,
with insolent uninterestedness, scorning to
even glance at the white sinabada. The
dingy outer wall of the rami was over-
hung with hunting and fishing nets and
queer pig- and alligator-traps. In every
corner were heaps of bamboo knives and
bows and arrows. Groups of men and
youths, splendid in bird-of-paradise plumes
and tight, broad, bark belts, sat round
eating pig, stewed with bananas and sago,
from great wooden bowls passed up to
them through a hole in the floor by the
women underneath (for no native female
may enter a rami). Accidentally I brushed
against a fiendish-looking totum shield,
which fell with unnecessary noise and
struck the shoulder of the aged son of the
Devil’s wife. He glared at me over his
lime stick, and I caught the glitter of pre-
cious stones in its hilt. Through my
orderly boy I was permitted to inspect
this object. It was 12 inches of a human
thigh bone, the upper part carved to the
exquisite semblance of a beautiful woman’s
face, with emerald eyes (I wonder where
they got those emeralds?), and hundreds
of tiny hair plaits curled at the top, where
they passed through the bone and finished
off at the ends with minute rose-red shells.
The neck was faultlessly modelled and the
curve of the shoulders expressed complete
beauty. It was ivory-white at the face,
tinging to deep gold brown where the Son
of the Morning’s claws clasped it. I tried
unsuccessfully to trade that stick
for a brand-new tomahawk, a pair
of scissors, a 3ft. scrub knife, and, finally, all
three. The information was passed along that the
stick belonged to the ancient cannibal’s grandfather,
and if it lift the village the hill people would come
down and eat the tribe. It was a tough tribe, so
I pressed the matter no farther.

“F.H.R.”: It isn’t necessary for me to
see a snake to know there’s one about. Time after
time I have felt a queer warning and stopped-
once with my foot poised over a black snake in the
moonlight; another time within a yard of a “tiger”
that I had no chance of seeing or hearing (I was
running at the time). Recently I was so sure of a
snake in the barn that I waited and got him next
day. I left home Monday morning recently, and
said to the wife, “You look out for a snake here.”
I work in the bush, and on Tuesday, after dinner
I had a bad attack of nerves. At every bit of
brown bark I ■ jumped. At four o’clock tea I told
my mate, who remarked that I would be unlikely
to see one that day, as it was late and rather cold.
However, I killed a big “carpet” before five o’clock,
and three miles away the wife killed another. This
is gospel. I can’t explain it. I only ask if others
have got the same sort of internal snake-forecasting
barometer.
“Eddyson” : Johnny Noall was dead. The
hearse was at the door, and practically the whole
adult population of Cup Cup was assembled on the
widow’s verandah, or in the parlor or the kitchen,
celebrating the occasion with whisky and water
and corned beef sandwiches. There was beer for
those rugged inhabitants whose grief called for a
larger bath. Presently it was noticed that a
stranger had intruded upon their sorrow. The per-
son who had butted into the ceremony was a small,
wiry female, in a shabby black hat and wearing
ostentatious crepe. She had a tiny, hard, dark eye,
like the head of a tin tack, and a sharp nose that
went about the place quickly and inquisitively.
She was very suggestive of a sniffing terrier. She
sniffed the whisky before taking a big nip, she
sniffed the sandwiches after attacking a four-storey
helping; then she sniffed at everything in sight, in-
cluding the bereaved relict of J. Noall. After all
of which she took some more whisky and another
seven layers of corned beef sandwich. At this
stage a relative intervened on behalf of the pro-
prietors. “Excuse me, madam,” he said, “but may
I ask who you are?” “I’m yirs. Noall, that’s who
I am!” retorted the female with sudden energy.
The relative endeavored to point out the lady’s
strange error, and make it clear to her that the
real widow was the stout lady crying in the corner;
but the unexpected guest wouldn’t hear of it. “She
Johnny Noall’s widder!” piped the invader. “Not
a bit of it, she ain’t! Johnny Noall was married
to me afore ever he seen her.” And as an emphatic
assertion of her authority the claimant seized, an-
other whisky. Efforts were made by relatives and
neighbors to hush up the scandal, but the new Mrs.
Noall was not the kind of person it was easy to
hush up. She got on the parlor table and started
to state her claim. “I’m Nell Noall,” she pro-
tested, “Johnny Noall’s legal, lawful, wedded
widder, none other genuine.” She produced a docu-
ment to establish her position, but nobody would
look at it, and Nellie was hustled out. But when
the funeral was about' to move, Sweet Nell was
found ensconced in the 'mourning coach. She was
ejected after an unseemly struggle, and then tried
to climb into Clark’s buggy. Driven back from
that, she elected to walk, and trotted behind the
hearse all the way to the cemetery. At the grave-
side she started in again to dispute the right of the
supposed widow to the alleged remains of the said
J. Noall, and produced her document again. It was
forgery and corruption and burglary, she said, to
rob a decent widder lady of her lawful corpse, and
she was so persistent that the presiding parson
consented to look into her proofs. The papers were
certainly authentic. Nellie produced a marriage
certificate which established her claim to one John
Noall. “He ’ad twin warts on his chin,” cried
Nellie. The late Johnny had two warts on his
chin. “One was a blue one,” asserted Nell, triumph-
antly. One of the lamented Noall’s warts was cer-
tainly a turquoise blue. Doubts and suspicions
beset the burial party, and after a brief consulta-
tion it was decided to open the coffin and allow
claimant to identify the remains. The coffin was
opened; the expectant widow stepped forward and
gazed on the face of the dead. Then, uttering the
poignant cry of a baffled hen, she turned on the
mourners with just anger. “Why, this ain’t ’im!”
she said. “This ain’t my Johnny Noall.” She
shook her fist at them. “Watcher mean by it?’’
she said. She gazed on deceased once more. “Not
a bit like ’im.” she moaned. “The blighter—’e’s
dodged me again!” Then Nellie went away. But
too late—everybody agreed she had quite spoiled the
funeral.

Billy T. : D. McCool was, compara-
tively speaking, a hearty and happy man till his
wife started a boarding house. She might have
known what the result would be from Dan's be-
havior at harvesting time, when it was sometimes
necessary to lock him in the barn while the hands
were eating, so irritating was the spectacle to a
man of his frugal temperament. But the mines
started at Peewee, and Mrs. McCool saw what she
called “dollops” of money in it. She was right.
Within a month she had 30 boarders at a quid a
week, 33 per cent, of which was bunce. But the
sweet peace of mind of Daniel McCool was broken
for ever. The sight of 30 hungry men in full
action at table on Sunday drove him frantic. It was
bad enough on week days, when there were always
a number of men absent on day shift; but on Sun-
days the full strength of the company assailed the
victuals three times within 12 hours, and Dan's
powers of restraint were not equal to the occasion.
He would dance the length of the groaning board in
an ecstasy of apprehension, and eventually his agony
found expression. “For the love iv Hiven, Stacy,
have a little thought wid th’ mate, will yeh?”
“Davis, take some consideration fer th’ house, man.”
“Aisy wid th’ sugar, Squires!” “Anderson, lave
some duff for th’ weemin!” It might be supposed
that this sort of thing would drive boarders head-
long from the house; in reality, it spurred Mrs.
McCool’s paying guests to more desperate fights
with the viands. Mrs. McCool did them well, and
Dan’s acrobatics and mental agony became a much
appreciated comic-relief. The diners were disap-
pointed if he did not perform, and “Lave some for the
weemin!” was a cant term in all the mines at Pee-
wee. Mrs. Mac and her two stout daughters tried
their old device of locking Dan in the barn at din-
ner hours, but Dan performed like a ravening lion
behind his bars, and the boarders raised such strenu-
ous objection to the deprivation that McCool had
to be loosed again. He would stand at the head of
the table, pale and tremulous, and shout piteous ap-
peals or angry expostulations at the energetic feeders
as he saw the too, too solid flesh melt, and the piled
vegetables fade away, and “Lave some for the wee-
min! Mercy Gcd, lave some for the weemin!” was
the ever-recurring refrain of his intolerable lament.
It was a good joke while it lasted, but it did not
last long. The climax was sudden, dramatic and
full of action. It came along at Sunday’s dinner.
McCool had been bewailing and threatening with
even more than his customary vehemence. During
the pudding course he dashed from the room. When
he reappeared, a minute later, he had a gun.
“Dogs! dogs!” he screamed, “’atin’ a man out iv
house ’nd home!” and fired into the thick of the
startled feeders. The first shot broke Anderson’s
arm, the second smashed a sauce bowl. Then, roar-
ing like an animal, McCool got at them with the
butt of his gun. He gave Squires concussion of the
brain, knocked six of Hoadley’s teeth in, and dislo-
cated Trouson’s collar-bone. The survivors bore
poor Dan down, and secured him after a long fight
in which the tip of Davis’s finger was bitten off.
The police took McCool to Ballarat, and a lunatic
asylum swallowed him. Probably he is there yet.
His delusion was that he was being eaten alive by
wild boarders.

“Te Babarantiko”: The people of the
Pacific races have unlovely voices, but it is surpris-
ing what excellent “time” they keep. Practically
all of them are enthusiastic singers and dancers.
When the dancing mania is on him, which is nearly
always, the native squats in his house, chanting his
monotonous dance-song, keeping time with slaps on
chest, thighs, palms, and with gestures. Before they
can really speak the children do the same thing, and
the old crone chants on the brink of the grave. A
hundred men, and as many women, may take part
in a dance, and the slap with the flat of the hand
on the naked chest sounds as one crash, while the
stamp of 200 feet thunders like the stamp of a soli-
tary giant. But apart from this ear for “time,
the Pacific Islanders are not musical, or native-made
instruments would be more generally found. Where
they are found it is wise for the tourist to side-step.
THE ESPOUSAL OF THE CAVE WOMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To win as mine some hugely splendid woman,
Some woman breasted deeply, broadly thighed,
Moulded and mighty-limbed, of love defiant,
Large, savage-lipped, long-haired and fiery-eyed.
To steal upon her, bathing at the river,
Stalking her from my cover unawares,
Bellying along to tree and rock and hillock,
Knowing that all is lost me if she hears.
To close upon her till the waters, slipping
In silver tumult down her gleaming sides,
Show drop by drop distinctly.... then the onrush
Across the space that her and me divides.
Startled, she splashes through the water shallows,
Rustling the swaying reed-beds in her haste,
But swifter far, I leap upon and seize her
Around the curved delighting of her waist.
We fall and grapple in the ooze together,
Her teeth and nails are tearing at my heart,
But quick I lock my throttling, greedy fingers
Around her neck and force the jaws apart.
Now is she mine! And, struggling on my shoulder,
I bear her, till she struggles less and less ;
Then all her struggle ceases. And so I loose her.
She does not seek to flee my hot caress.
She licks my bloodied wounds, her soft tongue
stirring ;
Nor any boon I ask will be denied:
Her eyes are gentled as she looks upon me....
Up to my cave she goes, my happy bride.
N.s.w.
BERNARD INGLEBY.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
‘Abbey” Dangar, of Baroona, Singleton
(N.S. vv.), who died on Saturday, was the
fourth son of Henry Dangar, who came to
A. S. Wales in 1822, and was employed as
an assistant Government surveyor. This
original Dangar had five sons and two daugh-
ters. The sons were W. J. Dangar, who died
in 1890; Henry Carey Dangar, of Macleay-
street, who will be 83 on June 4; F. H.
Dangar, of London, who will be 82 in Octo-
ber ; A. A. Dangar, who died on Saturday
(he was born in 1840) ; and Francis Richard
Dangar, who died unmarried. The girls
married Walter Lamb, of Rooty Hill, and
G. F. Want, of Sydney. The four elder
brothers had 130,000 acres on the Gwydir.
A. A. Dangar, in addition to his share in
that estate, had 53,000 acres of his own in
Queensland. A part of the Baroona pro-
perty (A. A. Dangar’s own) is Castle Forbes,
owned in the _ ’3o’s by the notorious Major
Mudie, an aristocrat of his day and gener-
ation, and about the most strenuous convict-
flogger of the period.
“Dahn” Fisher (otherwise the Hon. F. M.
B. Fisher, Minister of Customs, Marine and
in Charge of the Government Printing Office
of Maoriland, at present a visitor to Aus-
tralia) is a son of that great personal force
in Wellington’s life, “Our George”—comp.,
reporter, mayor, M.P. and Cabinet Minister
under “Stockwhip” Atkinson. “Dahn”
Fisher, whose people came from Ireland, had
no fewer than three uncles who were comps ;
one of these (in Australia) and his father
being “whips” at the type-setter’s trade,
loung Fisher is keen to emulate the
achievement of his father, by tackling
a revision of the tariff. He has been
breaking himself in during the past
three months by assiduously inspecting fac-
tories up and down M.L. and meeting groups
of manufacturers. He and the boot people
found each other a tough proposition. There
was Smith, the manufacturer, fighting for a
high tariff to protect his trade, and there
was the same Smith, the importer, urging
Fisher to grant freetrade in footwear. It is
reported that “Dahn,” with his dazzling
smile, satisfied both Smiths.
The External Affairs Department recently
informed Hop Poon Gooey (the Chow who
has been so much in the public eye for the
last two years) that in a fortnight from date
of writing the time would be ripe for his
frequently-interesting missus to leave this
country. Whereupon the Argus announced
that “Mr. Poon Gooey was upset at the
hurried intimation of the Department, and
the short notice allowed to make all arrange-
ments for the departure of his wife.” As
an example of “hide” this takes the biscuit.
Mrs. Gooey was allowed to visit this count-
try in 1910 under a six-months’ exemption.
On the ground of ill-health an extension of
time was granted, whereupon the lady
promptly became “interesting.” Heeding
the blither of the Argus and other barrack-
ers for a piebald Australia, Josiah Thomas
extended the time allowance by another
three months. The next gag was that,
owing to the Chow revolution, Mrs. Gooey’s
life would be in danger if she returned to the
home of her fathers. So Thomas granted
yet another six months’ grace. Before this
was up the industrious lady succeeded in
becoming “interesting” some more. This
time Thomas summoned up all his determin-
ation (which doesn’t amount to much) and
said that Mrs. Gooey must go as soon as a
doctor certified that she was fit to travel.
The medico did this in February last, and
Gooey was informed by the Department that
the date of his wife’s departure had been
fixed for April 10. At the end of March,
when asked what arrangements he had made
for the voyage, the Mongolian intimated,
with a smile that was child-like and bland,
that he was horribly upset at the “hurried
intimation” and “short notice.” Also, he
procured a medical certificate to the effect
that his elder infant was unfit to travel ;
but Josiah’s ultimatum gives no heed to this
final strategic move.
An atmosphere of gloom and disapproval
brooded over the High Court last week when
Justice Powers took his seat there. The bar
stayed away with enthusiasm and unan-
imity, and no congratulations were forthcom-
ing from anywhere. The new Judge must
have felt something like Oscar Wilde’s “Sir
Mathew Ileid. President of the Royal Col-
lege of Physicians,” on the latter’s first visit
as a member to the Buckingham Club. Sir
Mathew was elected “in mistake for some-
body else; a contretemps that so enraged the
committee that when the real man came up
they blackballed him unanimously.”
Once more “ Jeames” :
The most notable feature of the late Pierpont
Morgan was his nose. It somewhat resembled the
proboscis which Coquelin used to build on to his
tace in the r6le of Cyrano de Bergerac. The chief
difference lay in the fact that Pierpont’s was more
purple and crenellated, and generally outrageous-
looking. It bulged like a small ballooji at the end,
and gave to his naturally small eyes a curiously pig-
like, expression. I used to see him every morning in
1 aris, years ago, buying a button-hole in a shop in
the Rue de la Paix. In those days it was said that
he was prepared to give half a million sterling to
anyone who would make his nose like that of
a normal man. The yarn may have been a lie.
Nevertheless, hundreds of people squandered hours
brooding over ways and means to repair the great
financier’s countenance. A doctor once said to me,
‘Why, I could fix him up with a few apnlications
of radium. in no time.” I maintained a civil silence,
but disbelieved him. There are some things that no
human being will endure if it is in the power of the
medical profession to free him from them, and one
is the sort of nose with which Morgan was afflicted.
CASEY,
CHAIRMAN
V. R. C.
“11. H.” : In a way the passing out of Pier-
pont Morgan, the great American capitalist,
was pathetic. It was a case of a multi-
millionaire dying of hunger. He had enough
money to buy half the food on earth, but he
hadn t anywhere about his clothes, or his
banks, or his trust companies, or his safe de-
posits, the price of a new stomach. And the
solitary old stomach had been so neglected
in the process of money-making that it had
lost its punch. Consequently its owner lost
his paunch. Morgan would probably have
given the value of a small kingdom for two
or three pounds weight of fresh innards, but
they were almost the only things he couldn’t
buy.
