Neil Stewart, alleged to be the oldest in-
habitant of Parramattta, Australia’s oldest
settlement outside Sydney, died the other
day, aged 92. In the first roaring J so’s he
was a gold commissioner at Bathurst, a place
which roared a good deal at that period.
John Lee, who for 66 years had lived in
the Maitland (N.S.W.) district, died a few
days ago. aged 79 years. He was the son of
an old Peninsular warrior who settled and
died at Parramatta. The warrior’s eldest
son, Benjamin, is now climbing up the ’Bo’s.
Ben was member for Maitland at one time,
and is remembered for a, famous scrap on the
/mi 1 " the House with another member.
(These were the days when big members
picked up small ones and threw them bodily
at the Speaker.) He was afterwards P.M.
at Bathurst, and for a while S.M. at the
Water Police Court, Sydney. Dismal Lee,
ex-Minister for Works, is a younger brother
New Chief Rabbi Hertz, head of English-
speaking Jewry, is only 41. He is a Hun-
garian, but was taken to America in his
12th year. After becoming a rabbi there he
transferred to Johannesburg, whence he was
expelled by Kruger. That got him the ear
of Milner, who is credited with a share in
making him Chief Rabbi.
Sometimes the centenarian business is
overdone. Thus Richard Blood, of Jack-
son’s Swamp, near Bathurst (N.S.W.), died
last Saturday at the age of 110. It only re-
quired the addition of another cypher and
R. Blood would have clearly remembered
how Egbert won the Dattle of IJengestdown.
And with another cypher he would have
remembered Chaos.
Everyone has his secret ambition. N.S.W.
Attorney-General Holman’s is not to be
Agent-General or Premier of the State, but
to shine in the British Parliament. Holman
was born a Londoner, and will probably die
one.. He acquired his Socialistic views as a
disciple of William Morris, who used to lec-
ture at street corners. His parents, both of
whom were members of the Brough-Bouci-
cault comedy co.. brought him to Australia
in his precocious teens.

a lot of things into his life. In the davs
when lie was a whirling figure in Vic. politics
—though he even then aimed at being some-
thing of a fashion-plate—there was a gag
to the effect that he had been a policeman
in America. In his first years in Victoria,
at the time of the gold rushes, he helped
Angus Mackay to own the Bendigo Adver-
tiser ; and from that he climbed into Par-
liament, and was Minister for Justice in
McCulloch’s second team, with Gorge
Higinbotham for a colleague. He was also
a vigorous Lands Minister under Francis—-
with his partner, Angus Mackay, at the
Lands Department. Then, pushed out of
Parliament, and hankering for a stiddy job,
he engineered himself on to the County
Court bench in 1884, and what he didn’t
know of law he made up for in his know-
ledge of men and things. Retiring about
10 years ago, he spent his old age mostly in
samnling the food of different countries. No
man knew more of the prog of the universe
than Casey.
A. A. (“Abbey”) Dangar, the N.S.W.
squatter who died last week, was one of the
little group of Singleton people whom the
big Cobar mine enriched. The business
started with the two Longworth brothers,
who worked a little local coal-mine and made
coke. They couldn’t get anybody to buv the
coke, so they decided to try to get hold of a
mine themselves and use their own coke in
smelting the ore. Cobar was abandoned, and
they got hold of that. Gould, the local soli-
citor, was in the business with them. When
they wanted more money, they went to
Dangar, the local rich man, and Dr. Read ;
and this lot constituted the Cobar Syndicate
which made big profits for 12 years and then
sold the whole concern for about half a
million.
THE BETRAYAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Millionaire Pierpont Morgan died at Rome from
acute indigestion. —News item.
The rich man owned a princely paunch,
In keeping with his place;
He nourished it and held it dear ;
It grew in beauty year by year,
And with his wealth kept pace!
A symbol of his sov’reignty,
The hall-mark of his caste,
He treated it with tender care,
Yea, lined it well with viands rare,
And loved it to the last.
Its sleekness and its rounded grace
He conned with lofty pride.
But in the end the pampered thing
Attacked its kind, indulgent king—-
And then the rich man died!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.
Octogenarian Smythe.
A Bulletin man who knows him well
speaks a piece:—
R. S. Smythe, whose score now stands at 80 (not
out), is probably the oldest journalist and impres-
ario in Australasia. Certainly, he is the most
widely-known public identity who has figured in
both capacities. As a professional penman he closed
his notebook when he returned to Melbourne from
“The Much - Travelled” (R. S. SMYTHE).
his first visit to America in 1876 (after acting as
special correspondent for four or five newspapers at
t- Philadelphia Centennial Exposition), but he re-
mained in evidence as a manager of “stars” up to

lectured in Australia.

living on the press. Five years latm.

R. S. SMYTHE.
(An old Phil May sketch.)
a Deniliquin weekly, the Southern Courier, in op-
position to a scandalously democratic rag, called the
Pastoral Times, their cnoice of editor fell upon young
R.S.S. Those bloated capitalists paid him £l2 a
week to check the country’s headlong descent to the
D- 1, and for nearly a year Smythe earned his
salary. Then he broke away again and resumed the
managerial burden. His travels and troubles began.
He took Dr. Lynn, the conjurer, and a small concert
company for a five years’ tour in the ’6o’s of last
century, visiting countries where the hoof of white
showman had never yet trod. Japan, the treaty
ports of China, Straits Settlements, Ceylon, Mauri-
tius, India from end to end ; and South Africa sent
forth audiences to welcome the invading show
Smythe included even St. Helena in that record-
breaking five years’ experience, from which he de-
rived his title of “The Much Travelled.” He was
the first manager to cross the Orange River in
S Africa with a professional co, Smythe tells a
story of how, standing at the inner door of the local
hall, which he had hired for his ungodly enterprise,
he held out his hand to receive the ticket or cash
of a tall, hairy Dopper, who had come late to the
show. Brother Boer, under the impression that it
was a revival meeting, and that all were welcome,
shook Smythe’s outstretched hand with Christian
cordiality, and passed himself into the back seats.
The Much Travelled returned to London, and made
himself acquainted with Europe, He was still a
journalist when he was not engaged in money-
making. In 1868 he contributed to the Pall Mall
Gazette, and wrote an article or two for the Corn-
hill, and for some time afterwards he supplied some
of the Occasional Notes for that magazine. No show-
man had a wider knowledge of newspaper offices. It
was once said of R. S. Smythe that he was person-
ally known to every editor of any importance from
i okohama to Capetown. To his capacity for writing
smart paragraphs and articles in advertisement of
his business and for getting them readily published,
a lot of his success was due. He was his own press
agent, and the best of press agents at that. Thus,
he was able to establish the “lecture platform” in
this country, and to make a speciality of importing
stars’’ from Europe or America. His name became
identified with the business that is now carried on
in a milder form, by his son, Carlisle Smythe.
Robert Heller, a clever conjuror and remarkably
fine pianist, was one of his earliest importations.
Arabella Goddard, the hardest and loudest woman
pianist of her period, was an early concert “star,”
an “ o Up c to tbe tllne of . Santley’s visit, and later,
R.S.S. figured as the principal concert-runner here-
abouts. He was the first to engage Paderewski, but
file contract was broken, and the long-haired Pole
eventually came to Australia under other manage-
ment. The first lecturer whom Smythe boomed into
notoriety was the Melbourne parson, Charles Clarke.
Lecturing was popularised by the silver-haired Rev.
Charles, and after he had returned to England on
his own, he came back to Australia several times
under engagement to his original manager. But the
most remarkable of Smythe’s early successes with a
one-man entertainment, was the' tour of Richard
Proctor, the astronomer, who talked about the firma-
ment to crowded audiences wherever he went. A
showman who could work up a hysteria of intelli-
gent public interest in the lectures of a scientist,
must have been a master of his craft. His manner
of exploiting Proctor gave Smvthe the first call on
celebrities, who could be tempted to make the long
trip to Australia. Some of these had a magnetic
name, and were comparatively easv to work. Archi-
ed J° rbes > Geor £ e Augustus Sala, H. M. Stanley
(the discoverer” of Livingstone), Max O’Rell, Mark
twain and Talmage (the Yankee preacher) came to
a c ' olm * r ' V that wanted to see and hear them for
their notoriety’s sake. Such less-known persons as
Rev. Haweis (the apostle of music), Harry Furniss
(the London Punch artist), Miss Von Finkelstein
(the 6ft. ladv who yarned about the Holy Land)
and Rev. Haskett Smith (one of the most interesting
and educational of Smvthe’s long line of platform
talkers) were not celebrities outside of literary circles.
They needed all the local notoriety that ' the old
hand could attach to them in order to make them
. e mana ?' er ’ s name was a guarantee of bright
>" their lectures. They were accepted on the
R.S.S. label, and the tiublic never complained of the
goods. R S. Smythe’s Reminiscences of the Remarkable
People he has piloted around Australasia, and
the others with whom he has foregathered whilst at-
tempting to get into closer business relations with
them, would make an attractive volume. He has
outlived every one of his biggest attractions. Their
celebrity is now so clouded o’er with “memories of
later years,” that the announcement of Smythe’s
80th birthday came to middle-aged readers as a
sudden reminder of the many famous names the
clever old showman has handled in his time. As
for the younger generation, the lecture platform can
go to rot for all it cares. It knows not Smythe, nor
is m the least respected by him. At the age of four
score years he has earned the right to think that
public taste,. in forms of entertainment, is losing
its last vestige of craving for education.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
N. S. Wales finances get madder and
madder. In the first nine months of the last finan-
cial year the expenditure exceeded the revenue by
£368,201; in the first nine months of this financial
year the excess is £1,731,756. Every week or two
Cann, who is understood to be Treasurer, announces
that he has put the peg in; but while there is a
penny to be had his spendthrift colleagues are going
to have it, and Cann doesn’t count. This is how the
whole matter stands:—
Revenue £11,407,156
Expenditure—-
Revenue account . . £13,598,976
Works Fund . . . . 685,931
Closer Settlement Fund 46,729
Loan account . . . . 6,889,696
21,221,332
Excess of Expenditure over Income .. £9,814,176
That is for nine months. Of every £lOO spent dur-
ing that period, no less than £46 6s. has been raised
on the State’s I 0 U’s; and we shall have to pay
those I O U’s some day—and the chances are that the
payment will come due when we are far more hard-
up than we are at this moment. Meantime, we shall
have to pay as much as £4 7s. p.c. interest for the
money. Money, in fact, is so scarce and dear that
private people with business instincts and training
are borrowing no more than they are forced to; they
know that for many years past there hasn’t been a
more unfavorable time to borrow. But the fools or
rogues who are running N. S. Wales and ruining the
Labor cause at the same time, branding the party’s
administration as the wildest and most extravagant
on record, pay no heed to such considerations.
And we are undoubtedly in the backwash already.
The N.S.W. March revenue was certainly £19,982
ahead of last year’s; but there was £40,563 less
from taxation, and the £60,545 more which the
business undertakings brought in is simply due to
the boom trade that results from the current squan-
dering of millions of borrowed money. And, anyhow,
there is another £350,000 or so of interest to be
earned—or owed—this year. Victoria shows pre-
cisely the same sagging in the middle. Its nine
months’ revenue has been £6,904,716, as against
£7,025,312 in the corresponding three quarters of
last year—a shrinkage of £120,596. Loan and
other expenditure figures from that quarter are not
available. S. Aus. has got in £3,190,475, as against
£3,166,755 ; but the Broken Hill trouble will send
the figures for the current quarter to pieces. Loan
figures are never mentioned in S. Aus. ; in fact, the
average S. Australian, who has been told that his
surpluses go to reduce the public debt, has a vague
idea that he doesn’t owe anything worth mentioning.
What he does owe is. about £9O per head—twice as
much as the Victorian, and more than any other
State in the Commonwealth ; more, indeed, than any
other discoverable State in the world.
Queensland collected £4,417,570, or £193,534
more than in the same three-quarters of the previous
year; but the expenditure was £4,173,350, or
£413,141 more; and that shows the same old wild
rush of the Gadarene swine down the steep place.
On the other hand, Loan expenditure, though still
far too large, was £567,927 less; the nine months’
total was £1,868,957. Westralia continues to suffer
from the common microbe: Revenue, £3,099,143;
expenditure, £3,374,527. Certainly, Westralia has
to charge itself with various sinking-fund contribu-
tions. In other words, it is borrowing to pay either
its sinking fund or its interest, and if ever a confi-
dence trick was played on Cohen that is one. Loan
expenditure is also never mentioned in the West—
except when the Auditor-General throws his report
on the table, and casually mentions that the meals
bought for immigrants on arrival are included among
the permanent and reproductive works charged to
this account. Finally, Tasmania, thanks mainly to
the special Commonwealth subsidy and the boom in
the metal markets, has shown up better with a
revenue of £882,773, as against £795,422 in the
first three quarters of the previous year ; and when
an improvement is due to a Commonwealth dole and
a sky-rocketing rise in the metal markets, it isn’t
much to give three cheers about.
* * * *
An important development is likely to
occur shortly in connection with the floating of loans
by the Australasian States. Hitherto it has been
the practice for the Agent-General of a State to
initiate all financial negotiations with the stock-
broking firm which for years has represented the
Australian States and Maoriland on the London
Exchange. A few years ago this issuing house,
Robert Nivison and Co., took into partnership Robert
Smith, who was official stockbroker to the Govern-
ment of India. Then an arrangement was made
whereby Nivison and Co. acted as financial agents
for the Dominion of Canada and its brokers. Thus
the greater portion of the debenture issues of Canada,
India, Australia and Maoriland, and most of the
loans of the Crown colonies and dependencies, are
now floated by one firm, having a close connection
not only with the recognised underwriters on the
London Stock Exchange, but also with the numerous
banks, discount cos., bill brokers and other private
cos., firms and individuals that habituallv indulge
in underwriting as a pleasant and profitable pastime
when the securities are doubly gilt-edsred. The accu-
mulated wealth behind this congeries of underwriters
is vast. Every loan recommended by R. Nivison and
Co. goes through whether the public comes in or
not. The public . nowadays rarely applies direct
for much of a colonial loan. In London nearly every-
thing in the nature of investment of capital is done
through one’s banker rather than through one’s
broker. The banker collects the anplications from
his clients and passes them on to the bank’s stock-
brokers, and they divide the brokerage. The bank,
being usually an underwriter, is quite prepared to
take up its underwritten portion of the issue. It has
daily applications from its customers for sound securi-
ties, and .it . thus, peddles off the new loan. Now.
however, it is quite likely that the experiment will
be made of handing a couple of Australian loans to
two London-Australian stockbroking firms, who have
so far been closely identified with Australian mining
scrip. Should these firms, whose rise to wealth has
been sensational, decide to become issuing houses,
they will compete for the honor, and incidentally the
profit, of floating all Australian and M.L. loans ; and
it can scarcely do harm, and most likely will work
for the public benefit, if these two Melbourne firms
co-operate to create a new underwriting group in
London. There will be only one drawback to success.
None of them speak Scotch, and their noses are only
of moderate size.

The capital is £40,000 in 7 p.c. cumulative pref.
shares and £122,591 in ordinaries. Of the pref.
shares 27,500 went to the pref. holders in the original
private co., 10,000 were subsequently issued at a
premium and 2500 were allotted to the emplovees'
provident fund. Goodwill accounts for no less than
£65,702 of the capital:—
Original Wunderlich business .. .. £38,50j0
Rooke and Co.’s business (Melb.) .. 20,’000
Adelaide and other purchases .. .. 7,202
Total . . . . £65,702
Reserves amount to £61,620, so there are now
tangible assets to represent practically all the capi-
tal. In a year or two the balance-sheet should be
cleaned up by wiping off the paper item entirely. A
few other things might be wiped off at the same
time. “Catalogues, stationery, prepayments, invest-
ments, etc., £3414,” isn’t, for instance, presentable
enough for the company it is in. Besides the visible
goodwill item there is another of a sort represented
by a payment on profits to be made to the three
“Wunderlich brothers who constitute the head office
Board. As managing or permanent directors they are
entitled to 5 p.c. of the net profits after the prefs.
have taken out 7 p.c. and the ordinaries 6 p.c., with
something more on the profits in excess of £lO,OOO.
This bonus amounts to more than the pref. dividend.
The pref. div. is, however, small. If the co.’s profit
was cut down by one-half, there would still be
enough, after feeding the prefs., to pay the ordinaries
10 p.c. and lay a fairly respectable amount by.
Whichever way you approach it the thing looks very
good indeed. There is a cumulative pref. div., cer-
tainly; but it can call for only £2BOO a year. There
is a cut by the managing directors; but it doesn’t
start till the ordinaries have had 6 p.c. There is a
big goodwill item; but just about enough surplus
profits have been accumulated in five years to wipe it
out. The trade has evidently grown very rapidly,
yet there are no liabilities whatever; on the other
hand, £60,564 of the capital (total, £162,591) is
in freehold lands and buildings and £41,287 is in
book debts (less reserve) and cash. And though
there has been a building boom of late, and though
under normal conditions the co. would get neither so
much business nor such prices, profits, as mentioned
before, could fall 50 p.c. and still show 7 p.c. to
prefs. and 10 p.c. to ordinaries. That they are quite
capable of falling with a nasty thud seems to be sug-
gested by the earlier figures in the table, though in
running the eye down the Profit column it should be
noted that until 1910 the co. made up its accounts
half-yearly.

The object of the management seems to be to make
one part at least of its accounts as unintelligible as
possible. It succeeds admirably. Six months ago,
after paying the 6 p.c. div. on" pref. shares, £3308
was appropriated in a lump to pay “div. at 10 p.c.
p.a. on ordinary shares and bonus to present holders
of ord. and pref. shares of Is. in the £ on dividends
paid during 1911.” Possibly the object of the
bonus (which, after all, amounted to less than p.c.
all round) was to provide shareholders with their in-
come tax; but it was a slummocky wav of doing it.
Now, after paying 6 p.c. p.a. on pref. shares and 10
p.c. p.a. on ordinaries, a bonus of 20 p.c. is to be paid
this month on ordinaries; the money, or some of it,
to be applied to the payment of calls on shares not
fully paid. The notice runs this way: “Where a
shareholder holds fully-paid ordinary as well as con-
tributing shares, such bonus shall be credited as
capital on his or her contributing shares to the
extent of 125.. on each share.” There are 60,000
fully-paid ordinaries and 20,000 ordinaries paid
to 2s. Apparently the directors mean that, out
of any sum payable to a shareholder, there may be
applied to capital so much as shall pay the now
partly-paid shares up to a maximum of 125., any bal-
ance to be paid to the shareholder in cash. The re-
sult, seemingly, will be that contributing shares will
be paid up to all sorts of amounts —which will be
more untidiness.
According to the Profit and Loss account, the
net profits are smaller than those shown in the table.
The balance is made up of amounts dropped into the
reserve fund. This shows how:—-
Profit (as per Unexplained
P. & L. Additions to
Account). Reserve Fund.

That reserve fund now amounts to £34,000; and it
is out of it that the bonus is to be paid. The fund
is, presumably, a reserve to protect the big asset
which consists of time-payment debts. All the assets
are raked into two heaps:—
Property, Stock, Sundry
Fittings, etc. Debtors.
June, 1909.. .. £7,206 £41391
Dec., 1909.. .. 6,944 50,230
June, 1910.. .. 8,577 53 880

June, 1911.. .. 58,485 74,684
Dec., 1911.. .. 69,889 90,630
June, 1912.. .. 75,454 89,148
Dec., 1912.. .. 80,995 103’491
The sundry debtors—presumably time-payment debts
—are growing fast, and, though the capital has been
gradually increased until it now stands at £81,963,
the co. has had to get accommodation to the extent
of £24,664, besides the £34,350 owing on mort-
gage. Prima facie, the person who buys on time-
payment hasn’t the available resources to pay cash,
and it would look like risky business to supply him’
But the strength of a concern of this kind lies in its
means of finding out the bad marks beforehand ; also
the debt is being constantly reduced, and the further it
is reduced the less likely is a “hire-purchaser” to
renege and lose the goods and his instalments into
the bargain. This brand of trading, if the manage-
ment is good, therefore produces far fewer bad debts
than might be expected. But if there were a sudden
slump, the co. would no doubt be landed with a
heap of returned second-hand goods; also, trade
wouid certainty go to pieces. For it is a weird
tact that, whether it be Government or individual,
the tendency is to borrow harder and go more furi-
ously into debt in good times when earnings are high,
than in bad times when work is scarce and wages
poor.

The agent of the big co. picks up side-lines as he
goes along; and, since the big co. doing numerous
lines can afford to paste its agents all over the coun-
try, the little co. doing only the odd jobs finds its
row getting harder and harder. Hence, a little over
12 months ago this co.- —which bought out a still
smaller one some years back—decided to go into
more general business, and set its agents talking
fires and accidents and burglars, as well as horses
and other cattle. The capital at that time was only
£6414, in 25,658 shares fully paid to ‘ss. Another
24,342 were offered and taken, Is. apiece being paid
on them. During the year nearly £lOOO has been
spent in organising the new departments ; hence the
diminished profit. So far there is nothing much to
show for the outlay in the shape of increased pre-
miums; the income from policies in 1911 was
£12,504, and in 1912 £12,694. It is too early
yet, though, to draw conclusions. The capital isn’t
imposing enough to hit the average insurer very
hard; but the funds are quite large enough to carry
the present volume of business and a bit more. And
there is no doubt about the quality of those funds ;
with the exception of a trifle of furniture and sun-
dries and £648 of secured advances, the whole lot is
in deposits and cash.

To temper the Polar blast to the closely-cropped
lambs, the dreadfully eloquent directors promise that
in about six months, when the co.’s new premises are
opened, “a return to profitability should be experi-
enced.” A few years back the directors (there are
nine of them) found sales so sick that they decided
that, unless a member spent at least £l2 per annum
at the stores, £1 of his capital would be deducted.
That explains the “forfeited capital.” The visible
result has been to nominally reduce the deficit; pro-
bably another result has been to embitter a crowd
of small customers who weren’t large customers
simply because, instead of nutting the available cash
into stock, the directors dashed it into out-of-the-way
city property. The directors’ report, anyhow, still
makes sad reading: “The sales at head stores fell
from £50,029 for the half-year to January, ’l2, to
£40,412 for the half-year to January, T 3. The loss
at the bakery (£275) The loss on the sale of
drapery stock ( £300) . . . . law costs in appeal case
(£300)....” And a promise of further losses next
half-year is helc( out. During the past six months
the before-mentioned out-of-the-way head stores were
sold for £28,000 (just under book value), and ten-
ders have been called for the erection of more cen-
tral premises, where the directors hope to chase the
nimble shilling with more success. The co. in the
previous half-year also got rid of its drapery stock
for £6269, the loss ( £300) being debited to this
half-year. Although the head store’s sales shrank,
both the Williamstown branch and the bakery show
increases; but only the Williamstown store shows a
profit ( £54). Higher wages, increased price of
flour, etc., are blamed for the bakery’s failure. For
the six months to June, 1912, the sales were £11,508
and the profit £344. To January, 1913, the sales
had risen to £14,885, yet a loss of £275 is shown.
As a matter of fact, the whole bakery enterprise
was ill-conceived from a business point of view. The
bakery appealed to hardly anybody outside the
society; and the society’s customers were scattered
throughout practically all the suburbs—one here and
another three streets further on. Successful trading
under those circumstances was just about impos-
sible.
The co.’s liabilities are heavy: Debentures,
£11,894 ; mortgage, £13,000; bank overdraft,
£4002; outstanding cheques, £7744 ; sundry credi-
tors, £1073; total, £37,713. There is, however,
£26,000 to come off this on account of the sale of
the Flinders-street property; how much is to go
back to debit on account of the new premises isn't
mentioned. At present various premises stand at
£45,574, while stock on hand is only £24,371.
Until the position is reversed, this co. is likely to
remain the shocking example among Australian co-
operative stores. The share canital now stands at
£ 52,823.

Thus in three months short of eight years the co.
has (1) paid a dividend of from 10 to 15 p.c. p.a.;
(2) paid for all new plant and other purchases; and
(3) put by £145,750. As for the dividend, it will

ferred to capital during the past few years; so the
present 10 p.c. is only a little under 15 p.c. on the
actually-paid capital ( £280,000). It wasn’t always
like that, though. Formed in 1881. the co. had a
hard struggle for many years, and it wasn’t until it
had built up a fine bunkering trade that there was
any glitter on the results. Even a 6 p.c. div. didn’t
arrive till the middle ’9o’s. Nowadavs the business
i-s very steady, with a slight tendency upwards. This

A few years ago it was calculated that the coal
reserves would last about 40 years—27 years on
from now. A sinking fund was established to wipe
out the capital cost of the undertaking within that
period. With its own intertest-earnings that fund
now amounts to £51,282. Since the date of that
original estimate, large discoveries of mineral have
been made, and the life of the property has been
extended, provided no catastrophe happens. Never-
theless the original basis of the sinking fund has
been adhered to by a management whose long-
headedness strikes this paper most favorably. There
isn’t indeed another Australasian coal-mining con-
cern which seems to so thoroughly realise that its
principal asset is wasting all the time and must
presently disappear altogether.

A select Melbourne crowd has made a lot of money
out of wrecks during the last few years, and this
hasn’t been the least profitable of them. After the
Freehold Assets Realisation Co. had sent its divers
down and explored the ruins, the management re-
ported that there certainly wouldn’t be anything
for the shareholders in the old “bank,” while, as for
the depositors, they would be lucky if they got
much more than 6s. Bd. in the £. The old business
was accordingly wound up and this new co. was
started. Shareholders were regarded as dead ; credi-
tors got nominally 7s. 7s. in the £—3s. in shares
and 4s. in 4 p.c. debentures, and 7d. was passed on
as a nest-egg in the new co.’s reserve fund. That
was in 19C7. The proceeds of a few extra-good
harvests were just then beginning to pile up; the
States’ borrowing boom was starting; and between
these two things, with a little help from others,
values began to kick up their heels again. This
co., being in the liquidation business, naturally
grabbed a buyer when it found him ; and the grab
has been pretty steady, though not violent. When
sales have been completed, the surplus over the
written-down value has been reckoned as profit;
while expected profit on sales made on terms has been
passed to a contingency fund, which is now £19,673
in credit. This latter item isn’t included with the
reserves in the table. Of the past year’s profit,
£4262 (less some expenses probably) has come
from sales. As the co. can’t go on selling the same
property, it is obvious that, unless it gets new
business, the profits must shrink presently. Recog-
nising this, and finding that the business of buying
wrecks is much to its liking, the Freehold Assets
Co. lately bought 140,500 out of a total of 145,700
shares in the Land Mortgage Bank of Victoria —a con-
cern which struck the same storm and found the
same rock as the Freehold Investment and Banking
Co. The effect of this deal is to put up the asset
“Shares in other cos. at cost” from £15.851, as at
December, 1911, to £102,686. as at the end of
1912. The purchase has, however, been easily
financed. Liabilities have moved up only from
£74,364 to £98,985; the balance of the purchase
money has come out of liquid assets and from reali-
sations. Of the liabilities, £70.949 is on account
of the debentures issued when the co. was formed.
Twelve months ago there was 31s. per 20s. share
visible for shareholders, assuming that the proper-
ties in hand bring only their book-value; and,_ as
remarked before, properties so far have been bring-
ing a good deal more than that. But the Land Mort-
gage deal completely alters the look of things. The
new asset represents almost one-half of the total
assets, and there is no data yet to show how it is

Of the co.’s reserves as at June, 1912, £13,716 con-
sisted of share premiums. The extremely doubtful
course has now been pursued of transferring £12,500
of this fund to capital, and the still more doubtful
course has been followed of transferring the balance
(£1216) to Dividend Reserve. Presumably this
latter reserve is a fund for the equalisation of divi-
dends, so we may presently have the spectacle of a
reputedly respectable co. paying a dividend out of
premium capital. Market-riggers could play a nice
game this way. Suppose a co. in which they were
interested wanted more capital. With tongue in
cheek they could boost things by issuing shares to
themselves at, say, 30s. apiece, that being 2s. or 3s.
above market value ; then presently they could further
boost things by declaring an utterly fictitious divi-
dend to absorb the premium they had previously
collected from themselves. The law may allow this
sort of business; but even if the law acquiesces, a
Stock Exchange with a proper regard for the invest-
ing public would jump hard on a co. which plays
any tricks with premium capital. Apart from this,
the accounts tell little of any value. Profit for the
half-year is alleged to be £3348, but how much or
how little has been written off against depreciation
there is no way of finding out. It is a matter of
trusting the management—the management which
transfers premium capital to a dividend reserve. The
capital stands at £62,258; and liabilities total
£14,093. On the other side the principal items
are —skip properties, £45,776; freeholds, wharves,
etc., £32,619; Pearl Bav estate (less amount
received from purchaser), £3177. The co. is gradu-
ally getting rid of the Harbor foreshore properties
into which it plunged some time ago, before it
found out that there wasn’t as much in the picnic-
ground business as it thought; and the proceeds are
going into new ferries, presumably to replace the
venerable arks with which the co. has been carrying
on its rather precarious business along the upper and
mostly tram-served reaches of the Harbor.

“Gargoyle” : The more I see of company
directors the less I think they are necessary. Par-
ticularly superfluous is the director of the little
mining co. and his brother of the bank or life assur-
ance office. When four or five of the little ones
bustle into the fortnightly meeting of a little gold
mining co., which survives on threepenny monthly
calls, they usually find the business on the agenda
paper cut and dried lor them to adopt, and all the
cheques neatly filled up for the two best writers to
sign. Sometimes there is no agenda paper; always
there is a cheque for directors’ fees. The legal
manager makes sure of that cheque—and of his
own. Often there are minutes to hear read and to
confirm. Just as frequently there are no minutes of
previous meetings. One director, usually the chair-
man, does what real work there is to do after the
mine manager and the legal manager have ordered
the goods and engaged the miners. There is no
question of policy for the other directors to he!])
decide; they are mere surplusage, never ornamental
and rarely useful. In fact, most of these extra
directors know nothing of mining, and only about
2 p.c. of them have ever been down a mine. The
other 98 p.c. are afraid to venture in the cage. The
case against the director who is not wanted is as
strong in the instance of a bank or insurance co.
The bank director turns up on Wednesdays for one
hour to hear the general manager read the con-
fidential statement as to the amount of cash on hand,
and the quantity of bills discounted or overdrafts
granted by himself and the country managers; but
the Board rarely sees either the bills-discounted lists
or the overdraft lists. Everything is done by the per-
manent officials, and the only time a director does
any active work is when he calls to ask general
manager to look kindly on his friend Billkin’s appli-
cation for a loan. To the bank directors who thrust
advances on unwilling managers during the Mel-
bourne land boom, was nearly entirely due the
resultant smash. If the chief officers had been left
alone, the Scotch deposit money would not have
been thrown to three or four groups of allied land
speculators. And with stronger reason is the director
of a mutual life assurance society a useless ap-
pendage. He does not introduce business; he can-
not investigate loans or mortgages; he does not
seek to shape a policy he would spoil if he did; and
he, too, is a mere decoration to a newspaper adver-
tisement. When a director of this class of co. in-
terferes in its administration it is usually to get
an appointment for a relative or do a job for a
friend. In the United States and in Germany the
director, as we know him, is unknown. In Aus-
tralia he is a valueless excrescence, and should be
scraped off.
“The best possible” scheme of reconstruc-
tion put forward by the receiver of the Common-
wealth Oil Corporation makes cold reading. He
proposed to deal with things this way:—
(1) Issue £350,000 of 6 p.c. prior lien deben-
tures, giving £175,000 in 5 p.c. shares as a bonus
to subscribers.
(2) To induce the holders of the existing
£150,000 of 5/ p.c. first mortgage debentures to
agree, offer them 6 p.c. and pay their arrears of
interest in cash.
(3) Exchange the existing £400,000 second de-
bentures for £240,000 debentures (not secured) ;
interest 6 p.c. Interest arrears on the old deben-
tures not to be paid.
(4) Sundry creditors for £lOO,OOO to get £50,000
in unsecured 6 p.c. debentures.
(5) Shareholders (£725,007) to get £175,000
ordinary shares.
r l his means that the old shareholders would lose
£550,000 and the old debenture-holders and credi-
tors £230,000, plus arrears of interest. The new
capital paid up would be £350,000, out of which
interest arrears would be taken. The scheme, ac-
cording to latest advices, had little hope of going
through; and it would probably be ineffective if it
did go through. The wasted capital proposed to be
written off was quite inadequate, and the money
proposed to be put up was inadequate, too.
* * * *
The Queen Bee (N.S.W.) many months
ago was spreading her wings and generally getting
busy. The weather was propitious, and the unusu-
ally knowing ones predicted that a flight was about
to be made. That was at the beginning of the
copper boom. But somehow the ascent never took
place, and now there has been a nasty drop instead,
on account of the warden’s refusal to grant a six-
months’ suspension of labor conditions. The next
move belongs to the co. Mismanagement is what’s
the matter with the Bee.
* * * *
Bodangora (N.S.W.), after putting up
a lengthy struggle on its own account, is to be
turned over to the tender mercies of tributors,
whose job it will be to gouge out the ounce stone
said to have been developed. If the report as to the
existence of the stuff be true, it doesn’t look as if
the mine is altogether to blame, even though its very
flat dip does make mining expensive. The mine is
only a few miles from the railway at Wellington and
is well situated in many respects. Gold has been
proved over a wonderful length, and there seems to
be plenty about. The co. retains the right to the
tailings. Next door, or on the property itself, there
is a cyanide plant putting through accumulated
tailings—apparently just for the fun of the thing,
as there are no visible signs of any profit.
* * * *
Pulganbar, the alleged mercury (with a
dash of copper) show outside Grafton (N.S.W.), is
once again heard from; but this time it is in a
comparatively chastened mood. During the interval
of silence the oil engine (the chief asset brought
into the amalgamation by the old co.) was replaced
by a steam engine, and a new pump was erected,
having six times the capacity of the old one. The
mercury furnace j,s reported to have given a highly
satisfactory test, the recovery being perfect. What
there will be to perfectly recover in the future is
the interesting question. Meanwhile the plant is
billed to start at once.
* ♦ * *
Chairman A. Bowler had the sense to put
the acid on the man who got up and talked hifalutin
at the recent meeting of the Australian Diamond
Mines. Bowler has the cold-blood microbe in his
veins, and so is content to merely repeat that the
pipes on the co.’s lease at Delegate (N.S.W.) resemble
the Kimberlite matrix in S’African mines. Yet, as
Bowler truthfully says about the myriads of garnets
found, that “means nothing” so far. The little
white octagon has to be got before a tiara can
adorn the brow of the Australian Diamond Mines or
a motor be added to the directors’ backyard.
* * * *
When Hawkins Hill (N.S.W.) fails to
come forward with news about the discovery of a
virgin reef, it may be taken that it is in a bad
way. That, however, is not yet, as the latest to
hand relates to a new untried reef found on the
surface near the Exhibition shaft. Coarse gold, “in
specks weighing pennyweights,” is reported, with
prospects up to lOoz. per ton. Samples are hurrying
citywards. But the trouble is never with the sam-
ples. It is always with the reefs that the samples
come from.
Prom Vic. mining engineer W. A. Rain:
The reference to the Murray-Rain crowd in connec-
tion with the Ajax Central (B. 27/3/T3) is mis-
leading. I am not a director of the Ajax Central,
and have nothing whatever to do with its control,
either directly or indirectly. As regards the sugges-
tion that I am inclined to withhold information in
regard to the extent and value of a mine’s ore re-
serves, let me say that I know of no Victorian mine
which declares the tonnage and value of its ore
reserves, and it seems to me unfair to single me out
for not being the one exception to this order (?) of
things. It is also unfair to mention specifically any
one Victorian mine, and condemn its Board for not
doing what no other mine in the State is doing.
The question of declaring the tonnage and value of
the ore reserves of Victorian mines is one either for
the suffering shareholders of any individual mine, or,
taking the mines in toto, a matter for Government
intervention. Many men, inexperienced in mine
sampling, but experienced in the management and
control of Victorian mines, have expressed to me
their opinion that it is not possible to estimate the
value of a Victorian mine by sampling. I consider
this opinion erroneous, but I wish here to emphasise
the importance of the personal equation. In other
words, the right man must supervise the work and
make the estimate, and grave differences of opinion
are likely to arise as to who is the right man. I
believe, however, that no proper idea of the value of
any mine, more especially of a small mine, can be
conveyed by a cold statement in figures of estimated
tonnage and average value. In every case the state-
ment requires to be amplified more or less by de-
scriptive writing, which will necessarily be colored
by the engineer’s views regarding future develop-
ment —not forgetting that the real development of a
mine is done in its stones, the drives, crosscuts, rises
and winzes being simply “highly suggestive.”
* * * *
A legend has run for years that a deep
lead exists in the Ovens Valley (Vie.) below where
the dredgers are turning the surface into a gravel
mound. A lively little Welshman has been check-
ing data, and there would be as much talk over his
boring as over ■some of the best work in the Cathcart
Central, if people had the time to listen. Anyway,
a paddock of 3000 acres is roped in, and 1000 acres
are to go in a float for 15,000 paid-ups and £lOOO
cash in a co. of 60,000 shares. The private work
done by the syndicate is said to exactly bear out the
results of the old State bores, and gold has been
actually found. This is more satisfying than the
deduction that, because gold was got in the adjoin-
ing hills, it must be found in the deep lead. With-
out damping the syndicate’s spirits, the old advice
has to be repeated—bore the lead well before starting
the mine. Then, if the bores are good enough, don’t
skimp the sinking, for no one knows what drainage
from the mountains goes underground in the Ovens
Valley system. Also, don’t dream that a call or two
will put the claim on gold. At Creswick the average
outlay was about £lOO,OOO per shaft. At other
leads of any size the outgo was £30,000 upwards;
and there is no evidence that the cost will be less in
the Ovens Valley.
* * * *
Ivanhoe (W.A.) has at last confessed that
it can’t maintain present profits any longer. This
time the monthly surplus is to be reduced to
,£14,000, and the drop will take effect at once. The
general manager’s latest estimate of ore in sight is
1,080,850 tons of a gross value of £2,196,897,
while that of the consulting engineers (8., M. and
Co.) is 1,089,595 tons containing £2,201,890.
Allowing 30s. per ton to cover all exes, and loss in
residues, these figures give a profit of 575. 6d. and
565. 9d. per share respectively.
Associated Northern (W.A.) is shoving
down its shaft at Ora Banda as quickly as possible,
and hopes to open out at No. 5 level in a month or
so. It will, however, take two or three months
longer to reach the point where the rich shoot is
expected to be cut. Meantime, the market has
added to the value of the stock a good deal more
than the new property warrants.
* * * *
An official circular announces that the
Great Boulder directors have surrendered the option
on the Great Victoria, development work there
having given disappointing results. It is added,
however, that the Board has “other important busi-
ness in hand of very considerable promise.” Some
admirers of the show reckon that Great Victoria
was turned down without a fair trial. But this
is certain: as the shafts and winzes were sunk ore
values dropped, and to have acquired the property
in the face of this would have earned for the man-
agement a shower of bricks.
Victorian alluvial expert Briant has been
engaged by Dr. Laver and others to report on the
leads at Kanowna. Hamilton, of the Great Boulder,
and Yeo, who works in with Lionel Robinson and
party, have also decided to import another Vic-
torian to prospect the same locality on their behalf.
There are tons and tons of gold still in the ground
at Kanowna, but the problem of profitably extract-
ing it isn’t easy to solve.
Another change of management is pend-
ing at the Gwalia (W.A.). Some months ago_ tne
then superintendent, Jack McDermott, had a differ-
ence with the general bosses (8., M. and Co.), and
resigned. A. Wauchope was brought down from
Yuanmi to do things that it was thought McDermott
couldn’t do, and after a short innings he has re-
tired. Several queer things have taken place on
this mine during the past 12 months, and an in-
vestigation into some of them wouldn’t do any
harm. # *
Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) is reported
to be looking “very healthy” in the upper levels,
but in the lower levels it still appears to be off-
color. An average grade of about 6dwt. is being
milled, and with a bit of luck the management
hopes to maintain this for some time to come.
* * * *
Corinthian North, near Bullfinch (W.A.),
has its plant nearly ready, and expects to start
crushing this month. The ore to be treated is
estimated at 30s. per ton, and as working expendi-
ture should not average more than 12s. per ton, a
monthly surplus of £2OOO on 2500 tons crushed is
anticipated. * As the co. wanted nearly all the
money it could lay its hand on for plant and equip-
ment, very little development has been done during
the past six months; but directly revenue is derived
from ore treatment this matter will receive atten-
tion. It is proposed to deepen the shaft in order to
obtain sufficient water for milling purposes. For
the present, the mine will be worked mostly on the
open-cut system.
* * * *
8., M. and Co. have thrown up their
option on Perkins’s lease at Gwalia (W.A.). A few
hundred feet of development was done on the shoot
above water-level, but nothing to warrant a deal
was met with. Locally the show is believed to have
the continuation of the Sons of Gwalia lode. Pos-
sibly it has; but as far as 8., M. and Co. could see,
the gold isn’t there.
When last heard of, Boulder No. 1
(W.A.) had some tributors breaking ore in various
parts of its Kalgoorlie property, and dust-storms
were interfering with its slime-treatment works at
Lennonville. Nothing short of a miracle will put
the co. on its feet again.
* * * *
Queensland mines last year produced
£4 175,355, of which copper gave £1,698,280 and
gold £1,477,979. Mount Morgan, easily the biggest
mine in the State, gave nearly equal values of the
red and yellow metals —copper, £641,887; gold,
£583 682. At Cloncurry copper preponderates—
£76l,7ss out of the total of £842,267 from all
sources. On that field Mount Elliott led; its
54,631 tons returned £540,266. The gold output
of ’the State shows a decreasing yield, and the slump
would have been much worse but for the metallur-
gical works, which got great help from the boom in
industrial metals. Of quartz mines, the Scottish
Gvmpie was the most consistent; it produced
86,700 tons averaging s.7dwts., and paid £16,500
in dividends. _
Said that Lionel Robinson, Clark and Co.,
are nibbling at the 300,000 Mount Morgan shares m
the late Walter Russell Hall’s estate. The price
mentioned is 60s. If the big deal is concluded, the
shares will go to London, where the firm will see
to some preliminary booming. This, more than any
changes at the mine, has been responsible for the
recent firming of the stock in the market.
* * *
A start has been made with the Chillagoe
Co.’s railway to the Mulligan coalfield. Prospectors
will doubtless be attracted in that direction for the
country adjacent is highly mineralised. Chillagoe s
most important mine, the Einasleigh, is looking up a
bit. The new lode struck in deepening the main
shaft is low-grade silicious ore, but is a big, solid-
looking body which may give better assays in other
parts. . , *
The Marble Island Iron and Limestone Co.
(0 ) registered in 1904, has had a merry life. It
started with 200 £lO shares. For the four years
1907-1910 it paid divs. of £43 per share, thanks to
flux purchases bv Mt. Morgan and Great Fitzroy.
last *vear the directors were negotiating for the
sale of ore for three years, which sale would have
paid another £35 per share, but at the last moment
business fell through. Things, therefore, are dull
now and the directors are thinking of selling the
whole outfit at anything over £5O per share Some
shareholders, however, want to hang on, and there
are threats of Law in consequence.
Thus a Melbourne Ahgus financial cable:
“Mount Elliotts, 106/3—loB/9. The sales of Mount
Elliott are due to a French pool being in difficul-
ties.” Any excuse is good enough; seemingly, the
shutting down of the mine has nothing to do with
the slump. Facts are, this stock was puffed to a
ridiculous figure, and Alphonse was landed with most
of the shares at the top of the market. The alleged
creep in Mount Elliott itself may be taken with some
salt. The real trouble is the short supply of ore
of the old high grade. Certainly, there was a
fire in the Hampden Consols —a very opportune fire.
How much timber was there in the Consols, any-
way?

97280 z. better than the previous best (May, 1912).
A return of 1,000,0000 z. a month is, according to
some optimists, likely to be recorded before long.

WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, April 7, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
On Saturday afternoon the Rose Bay goff
house was full of the scent of orris root and
hot scones. Four hundred or so of our most
Expensive Persons jerked their tightly-
swathed limbs out of cars, cabs and three-
penny trams, and ambled up the flower-
edged strip of asphalt to the eternal tea-
tables. The German Consul’s lady gave one
large Pekoe party, and Point Piper rallied
round another dame who consistently feeds
the hollow interiors of innumerable acquaint-
ances on the fatted cream puff.
Mr. Ted Simpson, captain of the Golf
Club, showed Governor Strickland and his
blue-eyed daughter the sights, and tennis
raged furiously on the lower lawms where
Mrs. Ted Antill and C. F. Stowe won a
mighty tourney (mixed doubles) and van-
quished Mr. and Mrs. Hooper in the finals.
The opening day brought all the best golfers
from their summer hiding places. Dr. and
Mrs. Kelty topped the score in mixed four-
somes. The Hugh Macneils were lower down
in the count, Miss Williamson and J. P.
Doyle hurrying along ahead. The Rose Bay
caddie resumed his grasshopper flight in
search of lost balls, and the deluge oblig-
ingly postponed its arrival till Saturday
night.
The Prince Alfred Hospital, a vast net-
work of wards, will hold its annual meeting
on April 14. Governor Strickland takes the
chair, and will divide his attention between
the balance sheet, and the nurses who flutter
to the front to get their priceless rewards
of merit. No priceless reward is worth less
than 2d.
The legal ladies of the High Court big-
wigs haven’t hitherto been remarkable for
anything but Stodge of the purely suburban
type, and a jealous clutch at their own un-
important Importance. Now a picturesque
personality, the brand new Justice Rich’s
handsome wife, will toss her plumes amongst
the dull dames of horsehair circles. Mrs.
Rich used to be a Miss Bowker. She is a
daughter of the late medico of that name,
and won the breathless admiration of ad-
miring friends by the capable manner in
which she mothered, as a slip of a girl, a
generous helping of brothers and sisters.
Without an ounce of folly or incapacity in
her mental make-up, the level-headed Mrs.
Rich is a charming addition to the veteran
fleet of legal lady cruisers in these parts.
Ex-Bush Brother Feetham, the long, lean
bishop-elect of North Queensland, was fare-
welled in Dubbo wflth an address and a
cheque. As Parson Feetham is going to
take on a pretty tough billet, with the dis-
tinction of being about the worst paid bishop
in the Anglican fold, he’ll find the cheque
very handy. The address might make an
emergency meal for his nag in time of
drought. When a North Queensland cleric
pays for his own oats, there’s supposed to be
mighty little left over for the necessary gee-
gee.
That cherubic young parson, Saumarez
Smith, and his wife are leaving for Eng-
land in the immediate future. The Saum-
marez Smiths didn’t make any great blaze
on these shores. The late Archbishop of
Sydney was a man of peace, with a generous
waist-line, and no hankering to split skulls
re the Boyne River accident of long, long
ago. But the “Bob” Smith cab fare epi-
sode stuck to him like wax, though his ex-
planation had a grain of truth in it. Only
a woman with an iron iaw would ever dare
to give cabby his legal fare. His late Legs
of Sydney argued that in handing out the
exact fare (one shilling), he was fighting for
a principle. But the weak-kneed will always
over-pay, and the mean will invariably give
less than the just fare, if they can, so the
principle wasn t worth the “Bob” nickname.
Suffragette Mrs. Pankhurst, who has just
been served with a sentence of three years’
plain, cold English gaol for being an uncom-
mon nuisance, has babbled politics all her
life. Her father was a red-hot Radical, so,
naturally, when she was sent to Paris to
school, she chummed up with Rochefort’s
daughter, and after that she was no end of
a red Republican. When she- married it
was to another political—a mild, scholarly
“an who was a leading member of .T. S
Mill s Women’s Suffrage Society. Pa Pank-
hurst had one victory; he drafted the
Married Women's Property Bill, which be-
came law. I hen he died, and the widow
had to battle for her own crust —which she
did for some years as registrar of births and
deaths in Manchester or thereabouts.

the awe-inspiring name
of Mrs Ruffey Hill has come from Westralia
to shake up the morals of this port in the

Frank Thompson, a fine young Cornstalk,
much admired by the girls hereabouts, is off
to Europe for six months. He’s something
in the Lands Office by way of earning a
crust, and spends most of his Sundays as a
church organist, instead of cracking oysters
up the dizzy reaches of Middle Harl^r.
The Walter Blundells are over from Well-
ington (M.L.) for a few weeks. He follows
the pleasing occupation of owning, or part-
owning, that snug property, the Evening
Post. It thunders forewords of political
omen once a day in the Windy City, and
Editor Lukin turns the handle of the
thunder.
A week or two ago, the far-off M.L. town
of Invercargill landed an Austrian woman
journalist. She was promptly interviewed.
Alice Schalek gives the impression of being
a happy little Bohemian knocking about in
odd ends of the earth on behalf of a Vien-
nese paper—a billet every pen-driver with
the wander-thirst in her veins yearns for,
though it wears out boots and takes your
front hair out of curl. The scribbler un-
folded about a column of matter re
the Austria-Bulgarian-Servian triangular
tangle. Servia, it appears, has only one
market for pigs—Austria. But the haughty
Austrian won’t look at Servian hog, hence
the pursuit of a new market in Adrianople.
The latter town, however, is rather over-
crowded with ruins just now to be much
good to pig of any nationality. The Austrian
lady left Invercargill for a scamper through
the top half of the Dominion. Later, she
skims this continent in search of copy. In-
vercargill must sue her for damages, if she
forgets to mention that it grows the most
southern lamp-post in the world. Of course
it doesn’t, but it likes to think that it does.
Saturday was private view day at the
Royal Art Society’s .room. The atmosphere
of Rip Van Winkle fell heavily on two view-
ers who slumbered on the long settee that
faced the oil section. No surging throng
shook their sub-conscience lethargy. They
turned heavily in their sleep as the cat-like
tread of the occasional critic or patron fled
past the worst offences on the opposite wall.
The throng that might have attended the art
show, but didn’t, was mostly at Randwick, or
on the Rose Bay golf links. In the Chamber
of Sleep one ecstatic snore woke the startled
echoes, and the startled echoes snored back.
Alfred Coffey values an effort in oils caUed
“White Satin” at 25 of the obsolete coins
called guineas, which means £25 and 25
twentieths of a pound. There are no guineas
in circulation now, but some people will talk
in the dead coin. You really might as well
price your picture in talents, or shekels, or
rose nobles, or fix the value at 10 lakhs of
rupees and one anna and a few piastres and
a franc and a boliviano. The guinea vaguely
recalls the story of the hen and a half that
laid an egg and a half in a day and a half,
about which time the clock stopped so that
no one knew how many minutes and a half
it was after half-past something.
To return to the 25-guinea pic-
ture, the lady ’inside this frock must
have been rushed at a bargain sale
if she gave more than 4M. a yard for
the material, which looks like glazed calico
—the really cheap brand you give the prim-
eval savage for a cocoanut isle, if you get
ahead of the local missionary in the deal.
The “White Satin” lady wears a bleak air
of stony disappointment, so I suppose she
hates her shroud as cordially as does the
spectator. Mabel Barling still stalks hun-
gry looking lions, and sketches them on fast
days for her Zoo collection.
Mosman ought to turn out to its
last man and its final dog to see
what it looks like in a water carnival.
The artist’s name is Beer. Arthur J. W.
Burgess is a delightful person who casts a
glamor over the grey hulks of modern
cruisers. His wonder-patch of Reckitt’s blue
water flecked with foam, “Pacific Shores,” is
the illustration every Louis Becke story is
waiting for. You can almost see the brown
siren behind the slender poles of the palm-
trees, putting the last touch to her toilet of
hibiscus flowers (one behind each ear). Bur-
gess forgot to put her in the background, but
you know the minx is there just the same.
Women artists are few in the water-color
section at the R.A.S. show, but Bernie Pring
shows them the way in soft shades of peri-
winkle blue labelled “Mount Dundas”
(Tassy’s Isle).
A few weeks ago the Bondi Surf Club
selected two teams to compete for the Roth
Challenge Shield. The chosen only amounted
to about eight or so out of the 80 or more
members of the club which wrestles with
the business end of a breaker, and is poured
off the top of a big wave like something
of no importance out of a dish. So a
huge and healthy Bondi surfer named
Ormsby, who also belongs to the club of life-
savers, conspired with other mermen tocom-
bete rr / or / .rt le shield- They were dubbed the
U.I.T. (Unofficial Inefficient Team). But
when the day of reckoning came with the
serried hosts of fish-men, the U.I.T. snatched
the trophy from all-comers. The shield,
however, mysteriously vanished for a day or
two, pending an academic debate as to
whether the U.1.T., being chosen only of
itself, could officially win anything. A
crowded meeting, after a debate that would
paralyse the best efforts of a fully-fledged
politician in the act of raising his own
salary, decided by a majority of one, that it
would take the U.I.T. back to the official
cave. So all aspersions are withdrawn, and
the marine Potts now associates with the
sea Slurk on terms of amity. If the amity
isn’t quite real, it is at least a fair imita-
tion.
Zanelli, who came here with the “Butter-
fly” opera co. as a mild young baritone, has
developed a fine dramatic tenor. Some day
he may uplift it in a thanksgiving song to
the Australian climate, when he handles big
fees in opera or marries an heiress. (N.B. —•
Any good-looking tenor with an ounce of
pluck can carry off a moneyed maiden if he
wears the right clothes and has large melan-
choly eyes.) Zanelli’s farewell concert was
a tumult of enthusiasm. He goes first to
place the tenor he found in Sydney under
the noted Perilli in Naples. Later, he will
warble operatic rbles under Jean de Reszke
in Paris. Jean used to be a baritone him-
self before he climbed into a tenor, by the
way. To quote the singer’s own words:
“My father sent for me to return and finish
my studies on the Continent. I remained
in Sydney with a purpose. Soon after com-
ing to Australia I noticed that my voice was
changed. It is the climate —I am sure of it.
So now I have the stuff —the tenor voice—
I go to place it under Perilli.” Just here
the office cynic inquires whether Zanelli
phre can bribe the Australian tenor voice
not to turn into a Neapolitan bass or a
French baritone or a German sausage.
The friends of musician Hellemann, who’s
going to wave his baton over the Welling-
ton (M.L.) choral society, are gathering
themselves together in one place as this
meets your eye. A purse of sovereigns will
be forced into the Hellemann tail-pocket as
a token of farewell. The whole reason of
the tail-pocket is that when you wear it
your friends can crawl up behind you like
pale backbiters in the night, and shove
money upon you unbeknownst. Then,
as soon as they are gone, you take it out
and shove it into your front pocket lest the
Artful Dodger should get at it.
The Sydney Phil, had the luck to capture
Mary Conly for two festival concerts, the
rather dull “Elijah” and the gorgeous “Cre-
ation.” Hadyn’s poetic dream of the begin-
ning of things is an improvement on facts.
The first man was a savage who probably
argued with a club and knocked his lady on
the head if the fig soup wasn’t up to the
mark. In oratorio, Adam addresses Eve in
the language of a vegetarian Chesterfield,
“Graceful consort, by thy side, softly fly the
golden hours.”
The Town Hall was packed with con-
firmed Conlyites for the “Creation.” Mary’s
beautiful voice is evidently as carefully shep-
herded by the owner as is the Melba' organ
by the only Nellie. The Conly soprano
keeps the bird-like purity of a youthful
warble. Allied to brilliant technique, it
soared high and sweet above the growls of
the ’cellos or the combined voices of the huge
Phil, chorus. “On mighty pens” and “With
verdure clad” were vocal triumphs with
which large selections of flappers from local
schools slaked their musical thirst. Regi-
nald Gooud, the hundred-fathoms-deep or sea-
bottom basso of the oratorio, curled his mas-
sive voice round “furious storms’ tempestu-
ous rage,” and descended to lower G or
thereabouts to earmark the birth of the first
worm. It was the most bottomless worm
on record. Even the deepest and most un-
profitable mine at Bendigo doesn’t compare
with it. Tenor Higginbotham, beautiful to
behold in immaculate and priceless swallow-
tails, was the mild Uriel of the solo cast.
Artist Frances Hodgkins, whose head-
quarters are in Paris, will give a one-woman
show here within the coming week. On
April 15 the public will be welcomed in a
select corner of Anthony Hordern’s art show
rooms to view the Hodgkins water-colors.
The Maorilander, who left her native geysers
for Europe about 10 years ag- was the' first
and only petticoat ever elected as teacher of
water-colors in the classic Academy Colla-
rossi, in Paris. Later, she set up a small
academy of her own in the city of boule-
vards. Miss Hodgkins gets steep prices for
her work. She returns to her French pupils
at the beginning of the Paris winter term,
which is October. At present she is linger-
ing picturesquely with her cousin. Mrs
George Rich (the lady whose husband has
hed the High Court wig fitted to
his judicial cranium), at Darling Point.
A slim youth, who started from his native
Hawera (M.L.) for London as a veteran of
Lf or Id summers, blew into this port from
the Dominion last week. His name is Hut-
chins, and his business is music. Pader-
ewski, who heard the infant prodigy six
years or so ago, advised that he should be
packed up and sent to the Royal Academy
forthwith. This was done, and the Hawera
boy pianist waded through a stiff course of
exams., emerging from the ruck as a sub-
professor before he put an edge on his first
razor. Hutchins, who has attained the lofty
eminence of —1 years, has been giving re-

Madge Temple, the Tivoli goddess of
gowns, is a tall, handsome young woman
with a surprising soprano voice and 14 times
her fair and reasonable share of ostrich
feathers. Her tailor-built style reflects the
last gleam of fashion in perpendicular lines
of crystal buttons. She is an old friend of
Grace I'alotta, and has caught the Viennese
trick of putting a distracting touch of black
into some portion of her attire —half an inch
of black silk, or a thin strip of belting on a
rose coat, or a semblance of black velvet
under a white hat. The effect is It. But-
tons, I imagine, after a respectful interview
with a dozen or so Temple costumes, are
next to cut and color, the climax to a per-
fect outfit. The new, long, flat crystal but-
ton is like a slit almond made out of a slice
of decanter-stopper, colored with a flick of
absinthe.
The Madge Temple voice was trained by
Marie Duma (a Covent Garden authority)
for grand opera, but it descended to musical
comedy as a more auriferous profession. The
gold specks in opera are for the few. It is
odd to think of Ethel Irving in the trivial
jingle of comic opera, with Madge Temple
as her youthful understudy— yet that hap-
pened. But Ethel kept well, and didn’t give
her understudy a hope. The Voice was
afterwards raised in Stoll’s extracts of grand
opera at the Coliseum, and was commanded
to the _ presence of the Spanish King’s
mother-in-law, the Battenberg matron, who
is a very small royalty and the widow of a
royalty who was so small as to be almost
imperceptible. She paid the warbler in com-
pliments, while vaudeville pays in cash.
Hence, the Temple hats at the Tivoli. Yards
of ostrich tail trail round these temptations
(isn’t the Devil supposed to chuckle when
he sees a woman trying on a new hat?), for
which humble Eves sigh in vain.
Australia seems to be looking up as a
good address. Have you noticed that the
American Tittell-Brune is starred in the
Thames village as “the great Australian
actress”? /
The suffragette germ must have floated
round the King’s Ilall the other night, when
Prof. MacCallum addressed the Sydney Uni.
Women’s Union. A small, mild man, like
the literary MacCallum, seldom meets the
lady “objector” in her warpaint, since poli-
tics generally claim her undivided orations.
The burning subject of the next Senate elec-
tions, however, got the interjector and the
case for the prosecution into fighting line.
The little Prof, found his wind after being
asked whether he was present by his own
invitation, or something of the kind, and
threw a ton or two of hard mouth material
at the lady hecklers. Then he landed a
neat uppercut somewhere on the official per-
son of the Minister for Education. Figura-
tively, of course, for the Person wasn’t pre-
sent. The only information of any account
that the non-academic mind can drag out of
the recent uproar is the fact that the learned
Louisa Macdonald, M.A., is first favorite
in the female rush for a perch in the Senate.
This year the Royal Art Society proposes
to spread itself over an artists’ ball, which
is to be quite 200 degrees higher than all
previous efforts. The idea is a fancy hop.
illustrating the History of Australia from
the arrival of the first Spanish and Dutch
voyagers, up to 1860. Why 1860? The date
will prevent anyone appearing, either as the
Kelly Gang or as the bank smash of 1893.
Bushrangers and aboriginals will be wel-
comed. The. outlaw who designed the pre-
sent Australian stamp ought also to be im-
pounded. His costume should consist of a
few hundred Commonwealth stamps, in-
sufficiently gummed to his body. A general
rally of those interested will occur in the
society’s rooms on April 14, at S p.m. E. H.
Oxnard Smith is lion. sec.
The Alliance Francaise will mix its irre-
gular verbs with light refreshments on the
evening of April 22. St. James’s Hall is
the place.
