MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 7, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Alf. Vincent, The Bulletin’s esteemed
Melbourne artist, announced in a letter
dated April 4, that he was to be married on
April 5 to “the finest girl in the
world.” As a matter of fact, the
event really happened at St. James’s
Church on the date mentioned. Vin-
cent has hitherto been a chronic bachelor, but
he intimates that if he has fallen in love
later in life than some men, he has done it
badly in order to make up for lost time. The
lady is Miss Phyllis Potter, late of Sydney,
and everybody who knows her congratulates
Vincent. Also, everybody who knows Vincent
congratulates her, so things are about level.
The wedding presents included ten writs
for libel damages aggregating £50,000 —ten
writs at£sooo each. They were served by
ten of Vincent’s colleagues on The Bulle-
tin, including the general manager, the editor
and the associate editor. Each of them is
already married to the finest girl in the
world, and consequently the Melbourne ar-
tist’s statement is actionable. Eminent
counsel and other animals have been re-
tained. Meanwhile the financially ruined
pair are honeymooning at Healesville.
A 26-year-old woman of unremarkable
appearance led her fourth husband to a Mel-
bourne altar the other day. “Not No. 1, or
No. 2, or No. 3,” as the deluded valet used
to say in “Ma Mie Rosette,” “but, hang it!
No. 4.” The bride must have started her
matrimony at an early age. Pier first hus-
band lived four years after marriage. His
immediate successor lasted 15 months in holy
bonds, and the next one bade the world good-
night after nine months of wedded bliss.
That’s how the lady came to be marrying
her fourth in her 27th year. It sounds'like
a record—one of several Australia has put
up lately in the matter of marrying. Knibbs
told us the other day of a sprightly damsel
who had been divorced and remarried before
she was 18.
Lord Whitburgh, a burly but blas6-looking
young man in the late ’3o’s, was here for an
all too short stay. Our eligible girls had
heard that his peerage was a new “creation,”
and that his forbears were “colonial mer-
chants,” so, putting two and two together,
they reasoned that he was good enough,
matrimonially speaking. However, his get-
away was accomplished too decisively to
allow of any business being done. He plays
golf, and did a little in that line at the Mel-
bourne Club. A mother and sister are look-
ing after him.
Senators Tudor and Frazer laid them-
selves out to entertain F. M. B. Fisher,
Maoriland Minister for Marine and Customs,
who is here to have a look at the Dominion’s
new battleship. They invited a score of
people to meet him at lunch at the Oriental
Hotel. Mr. Fisher has brought along his
sister, Mrs. Mac Ewan. She graced the seat
of honor with big mustard-colored plumes
drooping over a particularly well-tailored
shoulder. Maorilander Manson and his lady
and Joshua of commerce were among the
guests.
Maud Washington Smith, a girl of high
social tonnage who divorced her medico hus-
band, Downey, some time ago, took unto
herself another spouse last week at
old St. James’s Church. The second experi-
ment is one Bryce, a big man with an English
engineering fame to match. Sister Mrs. Doc-
tor Downey (the two sisters Smith married
two brothers Downey, both doctors) shed the
light of her full approval on the wedding—a
smartly fixed-up affair, of which many of the
bride’s women-friends knew nothing until
after the event. She and hubby put up at
Menzies for a few days before leaving for
England. In that interval, she figured at the
Hug-Duckett wedding, and received heaps of
congratulations in a black velvet snood and a
marvellous cloak of pale pink chiffon velvet.
Edward Goll, Bohemian, who married the
young widow Julie O’Brien, has returned to
these parts because his wife is too good an
Australian to stay away. He comes covered
thick with London honors for his piano work,
and _is going to give Melbourne recitals on
April 26 and 28. Sister-in-law Mrs. Tom
Hogan, of Killegran, and all the other big
Society powers who dwell along the Balwyn
White Horse-road, are rousing Hawthorn
and Toorak to the call, and the Melbourne
Town Hall promises to be packed with the
Very Best chiffons.
Keith Maxwell, an eligible Sydney-sider,
has come and captured an English widow.
Mrs. Sayers. The lady had arranged to man-
age the Oriental Hotel, but after five weeks
she succumbed to matrimony, and departed
for Sydney with a grey chiffon bridal dress
and a pink feathered hat in her Saratoga.
Keith Maxwell has a station in Bananaland,
and is related to various well-known N. S.
Walers. A sister married the eldest son of
G. E. Pilcher, K.C. The new little bride is
plumping for Sydney as a dwelling place.
The better-half of the Thonemanns is lying
quietly in wait at a swagger suburban guest-
ery for her decree nisi, and intends to go
abrawd as soon as legal matters are arranged.
Mrs. Fred Thonemann, a daughter of James
Service, _is so rich that .she will be able to
indulge in extended wanderings.
Mrs. William 11. Higgins and her three
daughters left by the Osterley for Europe,
taking William H. along with them.
Col. Chauvel and his wife —a slim piece of
elegance from Bananaland—have taken a
residence in Toorak. They furnish the nur-
sery with a brace of small boys, beautiful
enough to win prizes at a baby show.
The officers of the battleship New Zealand
were billed for an afternoon tea-party at the
Oriental last week. Two hundred invitations
were out. Expectation cantered high. The
excitement in many a gentle female breast
was almost intolerable. And then, with a
sickening thud, comparable in its dreary
finality to the sound of a dead Chinaman
falling out of a third-floor window on to a
wet pavement, came the news that the fes-
tivity was off. The officers “regretted,” but
“they were coaling.” Fancy those divine
creatures shovelling coal!
Vida Goldstein and a number of other fair
self-advertisers are putting on a street scene
borrowed from poor old Suffragette-harassed
London. The performers stand in a rigid,
carefully-calculated pose, and hold out ban-
ners bearing the device “Votes for
Women.” Their elbows are primly tucked
in toward the waist-line, and a correctly-
gloved forefinger and thumb adhere genteelly
to the top of the placard. It is an attitude
that makes even a curvesome and right-hug-
able Suffragette look angular and forbidding.
The old Melbournians are giving a lawn
party at the Church of England Grammar
School on April 12. The delights that are to
be heaped on visitors are a sports pro-
gramme, tea and (hold tight) music in the
class-rooms.
The whole of the commissioned sea-lions
belonging to the battleships at present
making black smoke in our bay were asked
to dine with the vice-regal Denmans on
Friday night, and Lady Denman invited a
choice selection of girls from the Most Ex-
pensive circles to dance with them after
they had done feeding. About 40 cars, full
of fluffiness and impatience, arrived on the
scene too soon, and were kept in a queue in
the drive, severely cut off from the sacred
portico until the lengthy dinner terminated.
When the youthful Battenburg prince got
to the stage of dropping sugar in his black
coffee, somebody gave the signal and the
dead queue of motor wakened up. Men
chucked their cloaks through a sort
of mouse-hole in the outer hall, and
the women were herded in a narrow
cloak depository, lined with pigeon-holes
and garnished with one lonely and in-
adequate Venetian mirror. Even the solid
gold toilet fixings that backed it up, couldn’t
compensate for one mirror. Girls got out of
fur motor mufflings and stared with anxious
criticism at each other, for this was the sort
of hop that calls for every ounce of ammuni-
tion. Clothes simply shrieked in a deter-
mined effort after effect. One damsel hung
cerise over purple and another had petunia
panniers, like donkey’s packs, on either side
of a black and red brocade sheath. A fair
girl put on blue and white stripes as wide as
railway lines—broad gauge. A dear little
girl, just emerging from flapperdom, tilted
her nose in conscious superiority. She got
her original note by having all her pretty,
white, crisp tulle frills turned upside down
on the white sheath frock. She looked like
an adorable chicken very much ruffled. A
girl with maize-colored hair stood a flat pink
feather, quite a foot high, on top of her
head and drooped otherwise in flesh pink
fixings. The officers had something to rouse
their attention when these damsels came
through the swing doors, guarded by pink-
purple flunkeys. A vividly pretty young
matron had clothed herself in chiffon bro-
cade, the color of a tender young lemon,
while Mama appropriated the tint of the
ripe lemon for her magnificent gown. A
fine effect was achieved. A dame just
arrived from London and Paris might have
brought her dress from Timbuctoo. She
wore her feathers Red Indian fashion and
put her blazing diamond tiara on as a bul-
wark. A little new bride was demurely
subdued in black, and a tall member of a
wool family affected a purple cloak like a
Roman toga. Dress was all staccato or rag-
time, and the uniform of the officers com-
pleted the presto impassionata of the
theme. The ballroom. like many of
the dancers, was one half carpeted and one
half nude. The guests de luxe fed from gold
plate. Lady Denman, in black, had half a
sack of diamonds and pearls scattered over
her. She and her lord received the after-
dinner guests in the drawing-room, standing
with their backs against the fortress of the
bic. white marble fireplace, which usually
holds signed portraits of Royalty on its
chilly shelf.
Mayoress Hennessy gave a party to fare-
well three social Importants, who are going
abroad for a time. Ex-Mayoress Davey, one
of the departees, will plunge into London
Society under the wing of the local Lord
Mayor, who is a nephew of her husband, and
is enjoying a second term of office in the
Mansion House. She said her good-byes look-
ing buxom in black and white. Mrs. Moreil
was also farewelled, and so was Dame Gillotr.
wife °f the Sam who was first Gaud Mayor of
Melbourne. His predecessors didn’t have the
Gaud attachment. Mayoress Hennessy wore
a clingsome leaf-green garb. Mayor Hennes-
sey, the only man present, contrived to seem
quite cheerful under the circumstances. It is
the duty of Gaud Mayors to smile even at a
tea-party labelled “for women only.” The
heavy aldermanic air was absent from the
party. Mrs. Geoff. Syme added a frivolous
note to the dressing in a Frenchy pink frock
trimmed with ermine. Mrs. J. G. Barrett,
just back from London with her eye-doctor,
was a magnificence in old-gold silk. She
brought a daughter, who was nicely tucked
into a sealskin coat. Mrs. Godfrey spread
several yards of petunia silk with fur garnish-;
ings. Bands played cheerfully in the blue
rooms and the corridors, while the serious tea
business of the afternoon happened in the cosy
council room.
Sydney man Macintosh, who has come over
to manage the Oriental, has instituted a
Guest Night dinner that will make a world of
difference to hostesses worried by the Nary
Mary problem. While you dine a concert
proceeds in the Juliet balconies that jut out
over the turquoise winter-garden lounge. On
Friday night Arthur Andrews and Fred Col-
lier warbled sentimental ditties, and an
orchestra murmured classical scraps of comic
opera to the diners in the big oak hall, with
scarlet electrics to show up pretty frocks.
University Commencement on Saturday
was richly decorated by dons in gorgeous
robes and presided over by the Waxcellent
Chancellor in a flowing frock of honey-color
and black. Governor Fuller, an interested
spectator, was a mere cipher in the doings,
and sat in subdued civilian dress, at the right
hand of the enthroned Madden. Arts Prof.
Tucker, tall and correct, opened negotiations
with the Chancellor for the admittance of his
bunch of graduates, including sweet bachelor
girls to the number of 10. They swung their
rabbit-skin and colored hoods jauntily, and
favored the admiring eye of the Chancellor
with lovely blushes. Skeats, dark and debon-
air, squired the Science graduates. He wore
scarlet and the loveliest new-fashioned beef-
eater hat imaginable. There were girls after
Law and Medicine and Surgery and Teeth
and Music. Only the departments of the
Yets, and the Engineers escaped the feminine
invasion. Among the visitors on the plat-
form was horse-doctor Gilruth, Official Guard-
ian of Australia’s Front Door, looking like
a picture of Abraham Lincoln just after he
heard of Lee’s surrender.
The wives of the professors sat at the foot
of the platform, and Sassiety, in its prettiest
frock, yawned politely to the farthermost
limits of Wilson Hall. The admission of 132
new graduates might be inspiring from a
patriotic point of view, but the ceremony of
initiation becomes tedious. The solemn pro-
cession of the robed from the platform
savored of church on a Big day, and then
came a flurried break-up for tea. For this
last rite the building, which was once the
Museum, and after that was a music college,
and still later was transformed into an under-
grad.’s luncheon room, had been decorated
with the festive palm and laurel wreath. And
it calls for some extra-decoration, for some-
body has painted it in a crude color like
skinned rabbit. The wives of the professors
had charge of the tea-pots. Mrs. Peterson
was a dainty vision in lime color, Mrs. Allen
wore white, Mrs. Ewart came out into the
sunshine from a sick bed for an hour.
The Chancellor’s wife was gaily dressed
in pale blue, and Mrs. Professor Masson made
an energetic hostess in a soft black gown and
a charming manner.
Preparations for the winter balls are be-
ginning. _ The P’ran Mayoral hop—an
annual incident of importance—is billed to
happen on June 4. These coming events
cast terribly long shadows before, don’t
they?
Cards are out for a farewell mayoral spree
to three important dames —ex-Mayoress T.
J. Davey, ex-ex-Mayoress Gillott and Mrs.
S. J. Morell. The outburst will happen
early in the month.
The patient orchestra of the Theatre Royal
is boxed again in “Milestones,” and the
purring of the music comes from beneath
waterlilies and a green muslin pond. This
fashion of concealing the bald heads, pers-
piring faces and deplorable clothes of the
makers of music is growing. “Kismet” shut
them behind latticed screens in stage boxes,
and “The Blue Bird” buried them, like the
“Babes in the Wood,” under an avalanche of
leaves. Some of us wouldn’t complain if
they were buried beneath an avalanche of
blue-metal.
Two St. Kilda girls, Ruth and Evelyn
Sainton-Dolby (by the way, one of them has
just become Mrs. Somebody), who have
Voices and recurrent attacks of stageitis, are
feeling that “Milestones” is giving an ances-
tress a nice little boost every night. Gertrude
Rhead, the progressive maiden in the 1860
act, demurs at singing “Juanita” because she
“heard Madame Sainton-Dolby sing it last
week,” and her own performance would seem
to her so shocking by comparison.»

A Chinese restaurant has opened near the Trades
Hall. The chief characteristic of a Chinese restau
rant is that the food has the charm and dignity of
Age. You can buy eggs at a properly conducted
establishment of this sort that were laid when Con
fucius was a mere lad—and at a reasonable figure
Miss Doris Jones, of Fullarton, has placed a novel
with Cassell and Co., of London. It will be pub-
lished in a month or so.
The dressing at Morphetville races on Saturday
was what one might term involved. Some wore
chiffons, some furs, others a dash of both. These
discrepancies will happen, my dear, when summer
declines to go, and winter refuses to definitely arrive
Mrs. Arthur Cudmore, Mrs. S. J. Jacobs, Mrs. David
Wilkie, Mrs. O’Leary Mrs. Crozier and the Misses Lewis
were among the throng. The surprise of the day was
Katingawa’s win in the Handicap Hurdles- He gave
his backers the cheery return of £53 18s. for £1
which quite put in the shade the £l5 16s. paid by
Myrica in the Breeders’ Handicap.
From Paris comes the news that Miss Gladys Rey-
nell has had a picture accepted by the Salon. This
good-looking young woman has been studying in
that festive centre for some years.
The H. C. Caves and the Edwin Barritts are to
make Ruthven Mansions their headquarters for the
winter. The Alan McFarlqnes have taken a house at
Marryatville.
The Seaton Golf Club has issued its annual report.
The good old Wolf of Want has not only been beaten
off the doorstep of the club house, but likewise
kicked to death, and its carcase has been sold. To
such good purpose did the committee put the boot in
during the preceding 12 months, that the membership
now totals 326, and the income from members’ sub-
scriptions is £163. Shrieks of applause.
Mr. Purnell, who has jpst taken charge of our
Public Library, was on the staff of the famous Bod-
leian, at Oxford, for six years, from 1897. He then
went to the Library of Queen’s College, Oxford.
Afterwards he undertook work in connection with
various public and private libraries, and was given
charge of the Central Lending Library at Croydon,
England. He has also cast a critical eye over some of
the chief Continental libraries. Do you blame this cul-
tured city if it reposes an almost childlike trust in
its new acquisition?
Miss Howard, of Fullarton, left for England in the
Osterley on Friday.
Dr. Jefferis met his eightieth birthday with a
cheery smile on Friday last. He has just put in a
strenuous week with the Congregational Assembly.
The medical and legal professions were at logger-
heads in the Civil Court on Monday, when Mr. F. V.
Smith was cross-examining Dr. Kelmar, who is at war
with the Adelaide United Friendly Societies’ Dispen-
sary, or some such fearsome thing. Mr. Smith was
not inclined to swallow too readily some of the
medico’s technicalities, and said so ; but he got no
help from Judge Murray, who had “understood for
years” facts which the lawyer regarded with a
dubious eye—at which point the smile on the face of
the doctor was expansive.
The curates of the Outer Harbor met on Friday to
say good-bye to the departing Bishop Thomases. A
breakfast was consumed in honor of the fleeing
bishop, and, according to the local church paper, it
was a hilarious affair. Now, can a clerical breakfast
be hilarious?
Mr. T. J. Mitchell, who died at Woodville on
Thursday, was a colonist of over 73 years. He came
to Port Adelaide in the Ganges in ’39, and got a
billet in the Bank of South Australia, to which con-
cern he clung for 47 years. His original home was
a wattle-and-dab structure .near the west end of
Hindley-street, but he had lived at Woodville for about
33 vears. He was an unusuallv good classical
scholar, and could prattle cheerfully in Greek and
Latin. Also, he was a personage of some tonnage in
the Methodist world.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.
“Rawlet” tells how the wind blows: —
Before polling-day there is going to be trouble in
the Liberal camp, and if Joe Cook wishes to quell a
rising rebellion against his “popular” leadership, he
ought to come to Victoria at once and kick his “fol-
lowers” into line. I attended a “Liberal” gathering
at Carlton the other night. Speakers: Sam Mauger
and F. O’Neill, billed as “Selected Liberal Candi-
dates.” Mauger was asked to mention the party he
belonged to. He answered that he was not tied to
any party; as for a leader he had none, and as for
pledges, he gave none, save his platform utterances.
O’Neill was next verbally inspected. Certainly, he
was njt a member of the Fusion party. He had no
leader either; “every candidate was a leader in him-
self.” Also, “Mr. Cook was only elected till after
the campaign, and when the elections were over they
would have another meeting to decide the leader-
ship.” Joe Cookism has ruined whatever chances
the Opposition ever had in Victoria. Alleged Liberal
candidates are afraid to mention their chief’s name
these days.

and entirely to your liking.

to Melbourne complete without patronising the Savoy—a Cafe
renowned for its cuisine, competent supervision, courtesy, choice-
ness of eatables and all-round excellence.
Under the capable personal supervision of L. R. HOSSEN-
LOFF (Diplome d’ Honneur, 1889-90) the Savoy has rapidly
increased in favor, and to-day is recognised as THE Cafe u par
excellence” for all that is best in Luncheons, Dinners and

Other Notable Features are its Beautiful Appointments,

Up-to-Date System of Ventilation provides even on the hottest

POLITICAL
POINTS
A profoundly depressed British blue book
announces that Bull’s little voluntary army
is now 84.800 men short of its nominal
strength. So if the long-threatened Franco-
German scrap breaks out this year, Britain
will have to help its French ally by sending
over two old women and a dog.
STRIKE-BREAKING UP-TO-DATE.
In New South Wales, under the Union Jack, we
are supposed to have constitutional government....
What we do find is a weak-kneed Government going
cap in hand to these evil-doers.... This is the kind
of Government we have got—a Government untrue
to its oath, and untrue to the people as
a whole, instead of a strong, firm Govern-
ment, everybody under the Union Jack has
the right to look for; a Government that will at
once set to work to punish evil-doers.... If a firm,
strong, capable leader were only to come forward
with the old flag in his hand, right side up, it
would be found that a vast majority of the people
would follow him, and nail the flag and its tradi-
tions to the mast. etc.
Sydney TELEGRAPH correspondent.
The good Fairy waves her magic wand thrice ,
and the demons of evil melt away like mists
before the wind.—Scene from a threatened poli-
tical pantomime.
“Eheu Fugaces,” his Lament: —
As a Conservative of the old school —one who has
never ceased to deplore the cessation of government
by the Upper Classes, and whose face has been set
steadily from the beginning against unionism, one
man one vote, and Radicalism generally—l would
like to draw the attention of The Bulletin to a
cable message of last week. It ran as follows:
“Mr. Lloyd-George, Chancellor of the Exchequer in
the Asquith Government, giving evidence before the
Marconi Commission, said: ‘I am a comparatively
poor man. The position of a Cabinet Minister is
precarious, and the glory is transient. Parties come
and go, and only the judicious make provision for
the future by investments. The whole of my in-
vestments have returned only £ ItOO in a year. I have
only one house, that in Wales, for which the con-
tract price was £2OOO. Surely a man of 50 can
have one house that he might call his own.”
Is ary further evidence needed of the rotten state
of affairs that prevails when the administration of
a great nation falls into the hands of Socialists?
England has come to a pretty pass when her Mini-
sters are driven to admit, on oath, that their pri-
vate fortunes are such as many a petty shopkeeper
would be dissatisfied with after a decade of toil. It
was otherwise in the brave individualistic days when
gentlemen ruled the land and Labor Parties were
unknown. Consider the position of a British Minis-
ter in, say, George lll.’s reign. Had he remained
satisfied for a moment with investments returning
£4OO a year he would have been regarded as marl.
In those days, as Sir George Trevelyan remarks:
“Everybody who had influence in Parliament used
it for the express and avowed purpose of making
or repairing his fortune.” This was a period when
“it was more profitable to be a member of a
Cabinet than the partner in a brewery.” Henry
Fox, the father of the great Charles James, did not
hold office for very long. But while he did he was
in a position analogous, more or less, to that of the
miserable Lloyd-George—he was Paymaster of the
Forces. Starting, in a financial manner of speak-
ing, almost from scratch, he rapidly put himself
and his family beyond the reach of want. In the
words of the historian: “Every new regiment that
was mustered, every fresh ship that was m com-
mission, every additional ally who applied for a
subsidy, every captured province or colony which
had to be provided with a staff of salaried adminis-
trators brought grist to the mill of the Paymaster.”
And what was the result? We find that bv his will
he was enabled, after living luxuriously for years,
to leave his wife £2OOO a year, Holland House and
Government securities to the amount of £120,000.
His son, Stephen, received between four and five
thousand a year in land. Charles got a property
in Kent and £9OO a year, and Henry an estate in
the North and £5OO a year. The sons also got
£50,000 in cash between them, and, in addition,
the debts of the two eldest, amounting to over
£200,000, were paid by their father. And all out
of the public purse! Can our statesmen do this
sort of thing nowadays? Certainly not! At the
first faint sign or hint of anything of the sort the
so-called democracy is up in arms, the gutter press
is shrieking like an inebriated fish wife, and the
loot has to be abandoned. The result is, we have
the Cabinet Ministers of the greatest nation on
earth living in villa houses and dying poor. If any
better proof is available of the sordid process that
is the ideal of the modern Laborite or Socialist—■
an ideal that leaves no scope whatever for sturdy
individual effort —I should very much like to be
supplied with it.
Thus Joseph Cook, leader of the Federal
Opposition, at a “rally” in Sydney the other
night:—•
Australia is a land of hope and glory, and will
remain as such until amateur politicians can make
it otherwise.
Yet the “professional politician” was, until
yesterday or thereabouts, the subject of so
much cursing by the papers which support
Cook that the smoke of their language was
like a burnt offering on Mount Carmel. Why
this change? Is Cook himself a professional
politician nowadays?
The wobbling Adelaide Advertiser pours
much grease over the S. Aus. Government
for abandoning the late Yerran Ministry’s
proposal to establish State brick works and
concluding a deal with a
private firm for a year’s
supply. The price is 10s.
per 1000 less than the cur-
rent quotation; and “there
is nothing whatever to pre-
vent a number of builders
pooling their probable re-
quirements with equally sat-
isfactory results. Of course
there isn’t. Yet when Ver-
ran proposed something
wholesale in the way of
pooling—in short, when he
proposed that the whole
State should co-operate and
produce its own bricks —the
Advertiser was horrified
at the wickedness of the
idea It was a most deplor-
able thing for 10,000 people
who want bricks to pool
tlieir probable requirements ;
but it is a most sensible thing for “a num-
P er (( £ f builders” to play the same game. One
is Socialism” ; the other—well, the other is
business.
It is a cold day now when N.S.W. doesn’t
float a loan in good old London. Its latest
effort has been to raise £3,000,000 at 4 p.c.,
i epaj able in 10 years. Each £lOO security
sells at £9B, which makes the interest some-
thing over £4 4s. A little scrap of extra in-
terest is thrown in, and there is the Bull
stamp duty and the underwriters’ commis-
sion and the sundries. So one way and
another, the Ma Prodigal is borrowing at a
rate that even a tenth-rate bank wouldn’t
dream of offering to its wildest depositor In
tact, there is many a faded building society
living up a back street which would think it
reckless to cadge at the price.
JOES EASY TRIUMPH.
Mr. Tudor (Minister for Customs) blamed the
Fusionists for having frustrated his attempts to ex-
punge the anomalies from the tariff. When he in-
troduced his Anomalies Bill....the Opposition whip
(Mr. W. E. Johnson) said: “So that there may not
be a misapprehension in the minds of the members
of the committee, or of my constituents, I wish at
once to dissociate myself from any approval of this
attempt to re-open the tariff and impose higher
duties.” This was the sort of talk the official whip
of the Fusionists indulged in over tariff reform.

THE LANGUID ONE: “’E wouldn't let me .”
The real trouble at the bottom of the
Sydney goods porters’ strike is the sudden
realisation that, though they do exactly the
same sort of work as wharf-laborers, they
are paid about half the price per hour,
being on weekly wages. Of course, the
wharf-laborer replies that his work is not
constant, and he has a lot of broken time ;
whereas the railway porter draws his wage
every fortnight whether he has lost time or
not.
There comes to hand the glad information
that the N.S.W. Government proposes to
“throw open” for settlement 84,000 acres of
land near Hay—as if the 84,000 acres were
a door or a bazaar or an oyster. It is a
grand idea, but so long as Hay is only
allowed to export its wheat and wool via a
port that is nearly 500 miles awav, and the
ports that are only about 200 miles away are
rigorously blocked under the Sydney conges-
tion policy, there isn’t much prospect for the
settlers in the 84,000 acres. If Twofold Bay
or Melbourne or Portland happened to be
“thrown open” to Hay the place might begin
to have a show, for it is really almost a
coastal area, but the State Frights policy
makes it almost as remote as the middle of
Siberia or the back yard of Timbuctoo.
Judging from his Parramatta speech, Joe
Cook utterly disbelieves in Protection. He
regards it as a ruinous policy for the coun-
try. But he doesn’t propose to try and save
the country in spite of itself by educating it
to a proper tariff view. Instead, he will
refer the matter to a referendum, and if the
country prefers, in its ignorance and blind-
ness, to go to the fiscal devil, he will cheer-
fully let it devil. In fact, there is nothing
Joseph won’t do for the 30 pieces of silver
that are otherwise known as office.

The Queensland Railway Department fell
heavily on its officers over the Murphy's
Creek disaster. Usually in these cases it is
some insignificant person who gets the boot,
but, apparently, in Queensland they do
things differently, and a General Traffic
Manager and a District Traffic Manager are
in the soup. Both have been reduced in
rank, and one fined £lOO, to be deducted
from his salary at the rate of £5 per month.
The other is reduced £lOO per annum in
salary and fined £5O, to be collected at the
rate of £3 per month. This will be a sort
of epoch-marking case, and is probably about
the first on record in Australia where such
high officers have been dealt with so drasti-
cally. Still, the accident was a very bad
one —the sort of affair that no railway de-
partment could do otherwise than make
trouble over. And Queensland rose to the
occasion.

Parson Woodful], at Victorian Methodist
conference: —
If men are to be trained for mutilation and
slaughter, then it is best to go back to barbarism.

The S. M. Herald (1/4/T3) told Prime
Minister Fisher what he ought to do: —
There is no hint that Labor may be defeated in
the coming Federal elections. The Prime Minister
does not admit for a moment that the failure of the
Referenda will be the greatest possible note of cen-
sure that his Government could receive, and that
under ordinary conditions of straightforward British
politics it would have to resign.
Two days later it told the electors what they
ought to do :
The voter will have to choose the body to whom
he is prepared to entrust the Government of Aus-
tralia, which is a matter of party politics: he will
also have to answer the questions of the Referenda,
and from these the element of party should be com-
pletely absent ....
When did the llerald know a Government,
acting “under ordinary conditions of straight-
forward British politics,” to resign because
a purely non-party proposition had been
turned down?
A SONG OF CHEAP DEFENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Strict economy has been shown by the Reform
Government in matters of defence. The cost during
Mr. Massey’s term of office has been, proportionally,
little more than half that of the Australian system.
—Glad shout from Maoriland.
The gun was cheap we bought to guard
Our backyard fence against the foe,
For powder we had much regard,
And bullets were but kept for show;
We would not waste our caps to fire
In practice for the coming fray,
Because it was our great desire
To make the country’s business pay.
And yet our lads were smart enough,
They held themselves with pride elate,
We felt they were the proper stuff
To smash the foeman in the gate;
But boots and uniforms were dear,
And we must keep expenses down,
And so we exercised them near
The quiet places of the town.
The angry scrub might tear their dress,
The rugged hills their soles destroy,
A very little carelessness
Makes an expensive soldier boy ;
We ran the whole Department cheap,
Economy was still our cry,
Our term of office would not keep
Until the shots began to fly.
The people’s votes had turned us down
Before the wrathful foeman came,
And then we had the fair renown
And our opponents got the blame.
The roll of death was rather large,
The landing place was hard to hold,
But those who perished in the charge
Forgot to mention they were sold.
And now with frightful haste and pain
The people mend the damage done,
And rush to man the breach again
Beside their new, expensive gun;
Which gives us an excuse to say,
To those who hold the seats we lost,
We made the country’s business pay
And you forget to count the cost.
For thus the game of party goes
In pleasing spells of turn about,
And we must use our country’s foes
To get the other fellows out.
A little blood perchance 7nay flow,
But we shall praise the men who die;
We claim we ran the cheapest show
Before the shots began to fly!
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
City Tattersall’s Club distributed £2200
added money among the horse-owners at Randwick
on Saturday last, which was about £BOO more than
was passed out last year. It was a grey, still after-
noon, with rain hovering horribly along the horizon,
but the club had a bumper crowd, which probably
yielded it a record gate. The course, notwithstand-
ing the wear and tear of three days’ A.J.C. racing,
was generally voted good. The public got a bitter
smite in the Hurdle Race, with which the meeting
oper-ed. The field was five, and Gorlias, the aged
son of Gaulus, was loaded with public money till
he started at 10 to nine “on.” His rival right through
the race was the Maorilander, Merrie Laddie, who
went out at sixes, and they kept together till after
the last jump. Then the favorite was evidently
done, and the Maorilander ran home with 10 lengths
to spare, the favorite filling second place, with
Honey Bag third and away to glory. Seventeen
started for the Denman Stakes. Valido was the
popular fancy, and went out at sixes; but Patrick
O’Judas was in a generous mood, and did business
on some others at eight. The result was a bolt
from the blue. The winner, Malt Mary, appeared
suddenly from nowhere at the distance, and at half
distant:; ran through and won by half a length
from Lady Denman, with Mersina, who also carried
some public money, third. Lady Denman started
at the same price as the dubiously named Malt
Mary. Presumably if Malt Mary has a daughter
she will be called Beer Polly. The chief event of the
afternoon was, of course, the City Tatt.’s Cup. a
valuable utensil worth 50 guineas as well as £lOOO
added money. All this cash, and the sweepstakes,
with the exception of £2OO for the second and £lOO
for the third, is piled into the mug. Sixteen went
out to battle for it. The public rushed Hallowmas,
and he hardened to seven to two against. Cagou
was next at fives and Bernard at sevens; so it was
evident there were few on Bernard. Hallowmas
was first away, but Bernard quickly passed him,
with Cagou not far away. These three seemed just
then to include the winner of the Cup. At the dis-
tance Belove, winner of the Hobart Cup, took charge,
but Waltz, the imported daughter of Yorkshire Lad,
came with a rush. Bernard and Hallowmas fell
back, and the two mares fought a desperate race
with Offa, the gelding, straining lengths behind
them. It was a fierce finish, and Waltz won by part
of a head. Belove had started at eights, but Waltz
was soft at l‘Ve. It was a race after O’Judas's own
heart. Altogether Patrick had a pleasant afternoon’s
outing.
“0.K.”: Seems to me that “F.C.” (B.
27/3/’l3) libels himself when he describes his Flem-
ington Flat remarks in reply to mine of 20/3/’l3, as
“insulting.” They are too absurd to be in-
sulting. My paragraph was suggested by the
outpourings of a Footscray councillor and others,
who claimed for the public the right to enjoy a Mel-
bourne Cup Day picnic gratis on Flemington Flat.
I said that the Flemington racecourse show was an
expensive entertainment, provided by people who paid
to go in, and that nobody had a right to go in with-
out paying. “F.C.” says: “Now, will ‘O.K.’ tell us
what entertainment a Flatite sees? Has ‘O.K.’ ever
been on Flemington Flat? Absolutely the only en-
tertainment the Flatites get is what they provide
themselves. Most of them go there to bet,” etc.
To which I reply that the entertainment a Flatite
gets is a fairly close, or quite close, view of the
race at any point he may choose, whereas the paying
public gets practically no view of the race, except
when the horses are in the straight. A Flatite’s
view of jumping races is closer than the paying
public gets. I have been on the Flat, and I know.
Flatites go to the Flat to bet and see the races—for
nix. Standites and Hillites pay for going to the re-
serves, where they bet. Or they go for an outing—•
.to see the crowds. Well, the other people’s money
provides the show that draws the crowds. But for
the gate-money and subscriptions there would be no
races, no crowds, no sight worth seeing. As for
“F.C.’s” contention that Flemington should have
been under control of trustees like the Melbourne
Cricket Ground, I merely say that the M.C.C. ground
is a show-place where everybody has to pay for ad-
mission. The M.C.C. has never admitted the public
free to its flat on test match days, whereas the
V.R.C. has done so for 40 years on Cup days. Had
the club stewards been called trustees, they would,
presumably, have charged a bob for admission from
the verv outset.
* * * *
Boyo Driscoll, the Welsh fighter, made
his first appearance in Sydney Stadium on Saturday
night against Charles Simpson. If he fights a
thousand he will never meet a pluckier, more fight-
hungry bull-pup than that sterling little Victorian
who was about a head shorter and half a stone
lighter. Simpson was naturally deficient in reach.
And yet what a fight he did put up. It was magnifi-
cent in its courage, and in the tenth Chapter it was
tremendous in its effectiveness. But excepting in
that tenth chapter he never had a real hope, for
Driscoll was his master all the time. He has a
beautiful left which he times perfectly, and moves
with all the piston-like grace which characterised
the left of Peter Felix in the days when Peter was
really Felix. He has a head-guard that is well-nigh
invincible, and that left is the soundest defence of
all. It is always in the place where it ought to be,
and Simpson literally dashed himself to pieces
against it on Saturday ni&ht. By the 13th he was
done. It would have been madness to let so plucky
a fighter get further knocked about in a hopeless
battle, so the Victorian towel fluttered in. Driscoll’s
left, if it keeps on as it has started, will certainly
become famous.
The two latest importations of American
boxers by Snowy Baker for the Svdney Stadium
bear out his promise that only superior men are to
be brought out. It would be hird to find two more
gentlemanly youths than Stone and Thomas. They
were welcomed at a supper on Friday night, and
prove to be both men of parts. Stone is married,
and his third child is a wee daughter who arrived on
the planet after her father’s departure for Australia.
He has achieved fame outside the ring, and wears a
gold medal given in recognition of his bravery in
saving the life of a child at Savannah fU.S.A.).
His most famous opponent was Abe Attell, whom
he fought when that brilliant young Hebrew was
featherweight champion of the world. Thomas was
present at the 1908 Olympiad, where Mr. Baker saw
him win the world’s bantam-weight championship.
Although he has also met Abe Attell. and fought men
like “Knock-out” Brown, of New York, and other
big guns, his only defeat was at the mauleys of Jack
White, who won on points after 20 rounds. Both
will shortly be seen in battle array at the Rush-
cutter’s Bay home of the pugs.
The fact that Bill Lang is returning to
the ring ih Sydney to risk the loveliness of his
countenance in a combat with the English heavy-
V'-eiglit, Petty Officer Curran, is not a matter for joy.
In fact, it is a matter for a day of prayer and inter-
cession and general grief. This tendency of the pug
who has retired to come back and have one more bout
is not only harmful to his own countenance but to
the face of the country. Usually the Come-Back gets
an unholy walloping and the credit of the nation
suffers. And N.S.W.’s credit is already so bad that
the last loan cost nearly ii per cent.
Writer has heard of marriage as a remedy
for neurasthenia, but matrimony as an aid to pugil-
ism is a new thing. It was generally understood
that the sturdy Danish fighter, Battling Nelson, was
through with his ring career. Then he got married,
and departed for his honeymoon. Next he jumped
into a ring at Racine, Wisconsin, and attacked a
husky fighter named Sorenson for 10 rounds of a pair
of minutes each. Sorenson had an awful experience,
being knocked down four times and slugged almost
into insensibility. Nelson worked with all his old-
time skill and seemed faster than ever. Most of his
blows were landed on the body, which is an old
game with Nelson. He prefers to hit the body, as
he says the blows are just as effective on the recipi-
ent, and not so hard on the hands of the giver.
Nelson has always held it is more blessed to give
than to receive, and he has always taken great care
of his hands —a fact which is standing him yeoman
service in his attempt to come back.
* * * *
American fight fans are not altogether
devoid of sentiment, it seems, and gave Leon
Ketchell, brother of the late Stanley of that ilk, a
rousing reception at the Olympic Club, New York,
on February 10. It was Leon’s first appearance in
the professional ring, and he seems to have swum
at once into popular favor. He is said to be a minia-
ture edition of the late middleweight champion, and
to have all his dash and skill. He got through his
initial 10-round mill with credit, and save for a
couple of eyes in deep mourning, was unmarked.
* * * *
When the mail left New York, Tahmmy
Burns, a Canadian boxer who once visited this coun-
try and fought a forgotten nigger named Johnson,
was having a new kind of conflict. Tammy is running
a Stadium at the Canadian town of Calgary. The
Rev. Di. Marshall was trying to close it up, and
was going strong on the subject from the pulpit.
Tahmmy was going strong in the press. Marshall
said the fights at Burns’s Stadium were brutal, and
Burns retorted that they were not half so brutal as
the amateur scraps indulged in by the boys in the
doctor’s church gymnasium. Then Marshall made an
attack on Burns personally, and Burns got in a
reply. He challenged the clergyman to publish a
complete list of all his sins, and he would do like-
wise, and let the public judge between them. Just
then the mail left. It’s a weird scrap. Talking
about the ferocity of amateur boxers, there is cer-
tainly more gore and hair and fury in an amateur
tournament than in a year’s professional fighting.
The amateurs are over eager, and fight all they
know, whereas half the skill of the professional is
devoted to taking care of himself. Abe Attell is
evidently of the same opinion as Tahmmy Burns, for
when he opened a stoush saloon in New York he
announced to the public that he would stage only
fighters who were just coming on, struggling for a
place inside the ropes. Stars, he declared, and he
was a star long enough to know, are always stallers
who, nine times out of 10, impose on the public.
It’s a pretty revelation, but it hits the alleged
brutality of the professional ring right on the point
and the solar plexus simultaneously.

A sporting event called a ‘ swim through
Adelaide,” was inaugurated in chilly weather last
week. The course was from Frome-road bridge (by
the Zoo) down to the weir, measured as one mile
575 yards, and Moon did it in 40min. But, this
being the first time of holding, the time required
had been altogether over-estimated, and sundry com-
petitors, with nearly half an hour’s start, were” finish-
in& when Moon was about at Morphett-street bridge,
a field of 20-odd, two ladies started—and finished,
* * * *
Saturday saw the commencement of the
13th and second last round of Sydney First Grade
cricket. Interest was somewhat dimmed by the fact
that the Rugby League started its practice matches
and those who' desired to watch form from the jump
threw cricket overboard. Moreover, all but three
clubs are right out of the hunt for the premiership
which fact has decreased the attractiveness of the
bulk of the matches. The clubs that matter are
North Sydney, with 17 points, and Redfern and Uni-
versity, which, at time of writing, are each 14
North Sydney met Paddington at' Hampden Oval'
and Paddington went to the sticks, where 163 was
knocked up laboriously. North Sydney had no
time to show its mettle, and when stumps were
drawn had smote two without losing any "timber
Paddington’s highest score was 39, accumulated by
Noble. His only good backer was Munn, who hit
35; so these two, with Sundries, were responsible
for about half the score. North Sydney is feeling
good. North’s best bowler was Middleton, who got
five sticks for 19 runs. Redfern and University met
at the former’s oval, where University coruscated
by heaping up 431 for the loss of nine wickets. The
boss scorer of the day was Barbour, who was reminded
by a section of the crowd of his doings in the gas
strike. He biffed 143, so the heckling did not seem
to disconcert him. Roy Minnett made 68 and Gregg
64. A shocking feature of liedfern’s play was the
existence of 32 sundries. Redfern’s captain should
buy anew pair of boots, and use them hard on some
of his men. The fact that Middle Harbor made
199 against Gordon, reflects little credit on the rank
and file, seeing that 120 were hit up by the not-out
man Gee, and after him the highest score was 17.
Gordon hadn’t much time, but it made good use of
it, and by the end of the light had accumulated 132
for the loss of one wicket. Trumper made 44, and
then fell to Randall, and Macartney carried his bat
for 67. Although Sydney’s highest individual score
against Petersham was Coffey’s 49, the rest of the
team lifted the tally to 213 with the help of 11
sundries. Burwood called on Waverley, and being
invited to make itself at home, bashed its host’s
bowling for 306. Waverley allowed the reply to
stand over till next Saturday. Glebe met Central
Cumberland at No. 2 Cricket Ground, and with the
help of Bardsley’s 114, put together 277, after
which Central Cumberland started with 19 for no
wickets. Parramatta’s best bowler was Cranney,
who took three Glebe wickets for 31. #
Sydney Grammar School XI gathered the cricket
premiership of N.S.W. Great Public Schools after an
exceptionally triumphant season. Of 32 matches
played, Only one was lost and seven were drawn.
The other 24 were occasions to whoop and indulge
in catcalls. The schoolboys’ averages revealed a
couple of shining lights in W. McLean and G. A.
Street. Mac piled up 1143 runs at an average of
45.72, and Street gathered 120 wickets at an aver-
age of 8.72 per wicket., #
With the hoofballers practising on most
Melbourne grounds, the locals at Fitzroy began their
semi-final battle against South for the Cricket Prem-
iership. Winning the toss, ’Roy went in and made
a promising start, notching 150 for a pair of wic-
kets ; but a large and anaemic tail failed miserably,
and when stumps were drawn the side was out for
225 If the weather holds fine South has now its
last chance and a "ood one. Simultaneously, on
the rugged ’Varsity oval, the Students, winning the
toss, sent ’W'ood to the wickets, and got the 11 out
for 176. That distinctly moderate score leaves the
Students with the thick end of the argument; but
neither the pitch nor the weather are things one
can swear by, so the finish next Saturday promises
excitement. _ _ _
While some 5000 spectators viewed each of the
above episodes gratis, an equal number paid . a
“sprat” apiece to see if Middle Park could raise
278 in its second innings scuffle with ’Roy foot-
ballers for the Junior Cricket Premiership. Park
couldn’t. One batsman made 23, another 10 ; the
rest laboriously compiled 32 among them. So ’Roy
won handsomely. Here endeth the Junior Lesson
for the 1912-13 season.
* * * *
This writer isn’t quite sure whether it is
evidence of the increasing gentility of the great winter
game or of lovely woman’s tendency to push her-
self into the strenuous lifa, or whether it is merely
a pleasing specimen of feminine sarcasm, but in any
case he views with alarm, as Fat puts it, the fact
that a young damsel of Yarraville has applied to
the Yie. Football Ass. for an umpire’s license. She
states that she is athletic and hefty, understands the
game and has a good command of language. But
as there is sufficient “language” already on and out-
side the hoofball grounds, and as permission to
allow a woman umpire to exchange “remarks” with
players and barrackers might mean much exhilara-
tion, but would certainly mean very little football,
the Ass. turned the application down.
* * * *
The Metropolitan Rugby Union, which con-
trols the electorate side of Rugby football in Sydney
as far as the true blue amateur is concerned, has
decided not to add to the clubs which battled for the
glory last year. The Y.M.O.A. recently clasped its
knees and begged to be allowed upstairs among the
great ones, but the M.R.U. coldly bade the Good
You-ng Men be content in that station of football to
which it had pleased God and the M.R.U. to call
them—namely the second grade. The M.R.U.’s
trouble is that it hasn’t got grounds on which to
play, the taller offers of the more heavily gilded
League having driven it forth from the places where
it once reigned supreme. The council has now re-
commended the purchase of new grounds, either by a
syndicate or by the Union. If a syndicate can be
found, well and good. But at present the general
impression about the Union is that it is absolutely,
stony, motherless broke. The struggling Australian
League managed to finance the purchase of a couple
of grounds, but it looks as though the M.R.U. will
have to confine itself to unoccupied spaces and dodge
the law by playing in George-street when the police
aren’t looking. It’s a grievous case of the glory
having gone up the flue.
The whole of the Rugby League clubs were
in the field on Saturday, and adopted the wise plan
of putting on two separate NIII’s for the first and
second halves. This was to give the young talent
a show to reveal what it was made of, and it turned
out a good idea. Also, the young referees were
given an opportunity, and General Oninion remarked
afterwards that they all did very well. The Horder
family, which is playing for South Sydney, showed
up well in the scoring, the three brothers scoring
six tries among them. Which shows that it is a
fine thing to be strong in the legs. The Rugby
Union clubs and the Soccer teams were also out, so
there is no longer any excuse for imagining that
summer is with us still. The God of Winter is
abroad. * * »
The wind was almost dead on Sydney Har-
bor on Saturday afternoon, and as the grey, unin-
teresting afternoon wore on it became beautifully
less. The big yachts managed to finish the Prince
Alfred Yacht Club’s Ladies’ race, Sayonara, Cul-
wulla and Yuelba finishing in that order. But the
Skiff Club’s General Handicap, for which 13 started,
had to be called off. The wind got so faint that the
problem of how to get home suddenly became
urgent. The Skiff Club hadn’t brought its bed with
it, and it didn’t want to sleep on the bosom of the
harbor.
The Paddon-Arnst match for the sculling
championship of Australia is right off. Paddon would
row only on the Richmond River, while Arnst would
scull only on the Parramatta. So in the end Arnst
lifted his deposit and let the matter go. What
people are wondering is whether Paddon is feeling
glad or sorry. There are grave doubts on the sub-
ject.
» * ♦ *
Although 15 championships were listed on
the programme of the recent N.S.W. Amateur Ath-
letic* kick-up, only one record was equalled, and
that not a world record. It was the 100vds., in
which E. R. Cox covered himself with glory by
devouring the distance in 10 sec. This placed him
level with those demigods Nigel Barker, Stan Row
ley, W. T. Macpherson and Duffey, so far as their
Australian performances were concerned. But it
did not attain to the dazzling eminence of the
9 3-ssec. put up by Duffey at New York in 190“> and
since then equalled by J. Donaldson, E. S. Donovan
and Dan Kelly.
* * * *
Sydney bowling enthusiasts were not en-
thusiastic on Saturday afternoon. The weather was
against enthusiasm in any shape or form, except that
which might have accelerated a hurried departure
to some sunnier clime. Still the Pennant semi-finals
provided some good games, especially that in which
Lowlands (Newcastle) was defeated by Ashfield bv
the narrow margin of 103 to 101. City and Chats-
wood was a close thing, the bloated Metropolis win-
ning by 104 to 101.
* * * *
When 11. Grandon dropped the job of strok-
ing the N.S.W. Eight Oar Crew for Adelaide, the
Ma State found itself in danger of being left. The
crew must be in Adelaide by the end of the month,
and there was no time to find anew stroke. Ma
was saved from humiliation by single sculls cham-
pion Harry Green, who threw up the almost certain
chance of winning the single sculls and offered to
stroke the crew. As he is a stroke of parts the
selectors were delighted, and Ma will be safely re-
presented in the Eights, but not in the single sculls.
Is it possible that rowing is in such a parlous state
in N.S.W. that it has only two men capable of strok-
ing an eight-oar crew?
* * * *
The sport, business, or recreation of fire-
fighting in Greater New York is being conducted
strenuously. Motor traction is being introduced, and
while the old system is still allowed to go on its
smoky and erratic way, 45 new stations are being
created, 20 of which will be ready for occupation
early this year. Motor vehicles to the number of 85
have been ordered at a cost of about £140,000. They
include 31 combined chemical and hose waggons, 28
motor-drawn steam pumps, and 26 escapes of various
types. Nothing now remains but to organise a ser-
vice of aeroplanes to fly at the throat of any sky-
scraper which may dare to burst into flame in the
upper storeys.
* * * *
Henry Hughes, the veteran handicapper
to the S.A. Jockey Club, Adelaide Racing Club, and
practically all allied bodies in S.A., and at Broken
Hill, too, has given notice of his retirement at the
end of the present season. He has been at the racing
game for half a century, has filled every possible
official position on the course, was starter at the first
races ever held at Morphettville, has seen every Birth-
day Cup and every Adelaide Cup (except the one
that was run in Melbourne), has been a professional
handicapper for nearly 40 years, and for 20 years
has made horse his sole occupation.

“Hori” meets the newest Prophet:—
Have just returned from Waimana (M.L.), up
on the borders of the Uriwera country, which was
the last refuge of Henare Maori when the pakeha
Government confiscated the rich coast lands in the old
war time. Across the river from the pakeha settle-
ment is Henare’s stronghold—the Waimana pa,
where Rua, the Kaiser Bill and Moses of the Uri-
wera, has his establishment. Rua is an unofficial
king and the very latest thing in prophets—nickel-
plated and geared for high speed. He can tell more
highly variegated tales of the rewards for good
Maoris than the most enthusiastic missionary would
dare to, and is, therefore, a very successful prophet.
It is a sight to draw tears of joy from a Mormon
elder to see brown brother drive through the
pakeha section of Waimana, with his train of
seraglio beauties, gentlemen-in-waiting, rough-
tailed ponies and pariah dogs. The great man him-
self leads the way, ensconced in a smart butterfly
gig, with the latest addition to the harem at his
side. Next follows the rest of the skirt-collection,
driven in a high-wheeled four-in-hand by the heir-
apparent. Behind the beauties come the nobles of
the court, with their spouses, in every variety of
vehicle from an open drag to the humble dray.
The costumes of the entourage, leader included, are
of the latest and most fashionable cut, and six-
guinea suits and silk gowns are in striking contrast
with the get-up of the humble cow cocky, who, in
dungarees and bluchers, trudges slowly past in the
dust thrown by the flying wheels of the procession.
Yet in six months’ time, when the proceeds of the
latest land sale have evaporated, those same haughty
nobles, gay courtiers and radiant damosels will be
glad to pick up from the billiard-room floor the
cigarette butt discarded by the rouseabout employed
by that identical despised cow-pakeha, who happens
to have the knowledge, denied to them, of the uses
to which a banking account may be put.

“IWAN” Post-Hole Diggers
build firmer fences.
Because the “IWAN” bores the hole just big enough for the post
—thus you have practically no ramming, and the fence is firm
right from the start.
On the other hand, digging post-holes with crowbar and spade,
you must dig a hole at least 2ft. in diameter to get the required
depth. Consequently, there’s a heap of earth to shovel back and
ram; the post takes a long while to set, too.
Now, the “IWAN” digs post-holes just three times faster than
a crowbar and spade; is easier to work and easier to buy.
Where you only put in chains of
posts with bar and spade, you can do
18 chains with the Iwan Digger.
Empties with a shake, and by fitting
' extra pipe to the upright bar, the Iwan
bores to 35ft. No man wants to rack

The Law has a long arm occasionally.
Five years ago Carmody, of the Victorian
Agricultural Department, was walking home
when a party of three gentlemen smote him
at North Fitzroy. Two of them were
gathered in and did a “stretch” each. The
third man fell into the hands of the police
the other day, was tried, found guilty, and
remanded for sentence. One of the two who
had done a “stretch” appeared as a witness
and swore the third man had nothing to do
with it, the witness having been the sole
assaulter. Unfortunately for the prisoner
there was outside evidence, which had not
done a ‘“stretch,” to the effect that the third
man had taken an active part in the
assault; and it looks as though the name of
of the third man is Slush. When a five-
year-old crime suddenly rises up like this
and catches a bloke on the chin, it produces
a lack of confidence among the battery
classes which is liable to have a bad effect
on their branch of industry.

is designed for
Crushing and pulverising limestone, lime, coke, ore,
brickbats, sewer pipe, terracotta,. glass, quartz, phos-
phate rock, sandstone, gravel, tile crockery, flint,
manganese ore, oyster shells, stove lining, shale and
all other refradtory materials.
Made in Six Sizes.
The various sizes are made with a view of meeting
the requirements of the small, medium and large
consumer.
Crushing and Pulverising to Fines.
Those materials of a refradtory nature can be fed to
the machine m 3m. to 6m. cubes and smallei.
The several degrees of fines are secured by installing Grate Bars containing interstices of various widths. A measured
decree of fines is secured by running the materials over a given mesh screen, returning the rejedtions to the Pulveriser. The
coarser the grind the greater the tonnage capacity, while the finer the grind the smaller the tonnage capacity. The more
dry the material is made, the faster and finer the machine will pulverise.
Strength and Durability.
Careful observations, based upon adtual experience, have resulted in adding to and making extremely strong all parts
of the machine. Extended research and grinding tests year in and year out have brought about the use of Manganese Steel,
a metal that will best resist abrasion and withstand the greatest impadl.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Vic.”: Mackay, our Government forest conservator,
read a trenchant paper to the A.N.A. conference on
the subject that fills his days. Of all continents, Aus-
tralia has the smallest forest area. According to the
statists, the total is about 100,000,000 acres, or 5 p.c.;
but Mackay insists it is under 4 p.c. The U.S. of
America took 250 years to bring their forests as close
to the verge of destruction as Australians have gone
in 70 years. The great Victorian Otway and Strz-
lecki forests were virtually destroyed in 30 years.
Nor are,we keeping a tight hand on what is left. Of
Queensland’s forest area, under 3,250,000 acres are
set aside for timber supply, and no portion even of
that is inalienable. In N.S.W., with forests esti-
mated at 15,000,000 acres, settlement is steadily
biting into, and destroying, large areas. Victoria’s
forests are commonly given as 11,800,000 acres; but
Mackay, who ought to know best, declares that the
true area is less than 7,000,000 acres, and that much
of it is greatly damaged by fire. Plantations of pine
and fir will soon reach 20,000 acres; but this is not
a tenth part of what there ought to be, because Vic-
toria pays annually about £750,000 for imported
wood. Tasmania has about 65 p.c. (11,000,000
acres) under forest. South Aus. has a very small
area, with less than 147,000 acres reserved. Wes-
tralia has 20,000,000 acres; but this, again, is subject
to extensive timber-cutting licenses. The annual ex-
port amounts to £1,000,000, from which Govern-
ment receives a revenue of less than £45,000. The
export cannot last beyond 12 or 15 years. And
then— ?
Maori land has grown so dissatisfied with London
as a distributing port for the frozen jumbuck that a
determined effort is being made to have all the pro-
duce of the Shaky Islands sent to Bristol. And, of
course, Bristol is willing. Indeed the western Brit-
ish port, which was once the second in the island,
and ha'd nearly all the ‘long-distance trade, has been
so_ stirring itself dp lately that it has spent several
millions on its wharves and river, and is now reach-
ing out both hands to Maoriland in the name of
Empire and commission and petty cash.
* * * *
“8.N.F.”: Everybody wondered what ex-Minister
Nielsen. (N.S.W.) was doing to earn his “expenses”
while in America. A recent report throws some
light on the subject. From that and the illustra-
tions reprinted in a weekly paper, I gather that the
peregrinator was inspecting ferry services. There is
a nice little 'picture with a long description of San
Francisco’s ferries. The' picture shows a very old,
beam-engine boat, belonging to the Southern Pacific
Railway Co., whose craft are the joke of San Fran-
cisco. They are. used bi' nobody who can get on the
Key Route service. The latter has up-to-date boats
with a screw at each end, and they can pass the
S.P. boats any old time, besides which they are sup-
plied with modern conveniences. Compared with
even the Sydney ferries, the Southern Pacific boats
are modern only so far as their fittings go.
* * * #
Judge Williams, of Maoriland, has said his say
on the important Brighton land case, and his deci-
sion is one to bring temporary cheer to Premier
Massey. Brighton, for his payment of £47, cannot
acquire the £13,000 worth of coal below the sur-
face, but only the surface rights granted by his
original lease. Yet Massey’s Act of last year ex-
pressly stated that Brighton could obtain the fee
simple of his land. Ward’s Act had previously
laid it down that he could have the freehold of
the top layer only, with all mineral rights reserved
to the Crown. Now, if Massey’s fee simple only
meant the same thing as Ward’s top layer with
reservations, what in the name of Cohen’s mortgage
did Massey mean by altering the old Act at all?
The debate which raged over the question was, on
Judge Williams’s decision, only so much hot air,
for which the country had to pay.
* * * *
The Australian Government’s decision to replace
the copper penny and halfpenny with smaller nickel
coins threatens disaster to certain representatives of
Private Enterprise and some small expense to the
Post Office. The “brown” at present in use is an
unnecessarily massive article, and when a man has
to transport a dozen or so he becomes temporarily
lopsided, and walks as though he had a boil on his
spare rib or thereabouts. The new coins will be an
advantage and a blessing to weak-kneed persons,
but they won’t be any good to the slot machines.
There are quite a number of these contraptions
about Australia’s various cities; and the larger gas
companies own a lot of them —the big Melbourne co.
as many as 23,000. The proprietors of the weighing
and cigarette and lolly machines are in worse case,
for they don’t make the huge profits of the gas co.;
but so far there has been no wail from them nor any
lament about “capital being driven out of the
country.”
* * * *
“Star”: The American Musical Burlesque Co., in
eruption at Melbourne Bijou, makes an obtrusive
display of one number, a song and chorus, laboriously
extolling a coming “World’s Fair” in a Yankee town.
It doesn’t belong where it is, and is obviously a
large advt., for which the authorities provide ade-
quate remuneration, no -doubt. Three or four years
ago I advised Australian Governments, rabid to ad-
vertise their States, to subsidise Australian plays,
and send them “abroad” with laudatory statistics
judiciously mingled with the heroine’s woe and the
wicked man’s villainy. It is plain that America
has taken kindly to the proposition, and this yap
about the exhibition is the first -instalment of a
long series of ads. designed to decoy dollars from
Australia to the land of Sam.
* * C * K
“Ricardo”: Margarine is again seriously threaten-
ing the Australian butter trade. The import to
Britain in 1912 was 1,352,427 cwt.; of butter (from all
sources), 4,005,159 cwt. Very nearly as' much mar-
garine as Australian butter was landed on J. Bull’s
docks, and far more margarine than butter is made
in England itself. Altogether, the consumption of
the mess is estimated at one-third that of butter.
Worse still for us, Danish butter does not fear
margarine; it is the Australian and Siberian imports
that suffer, because of their poorer keeping quality.
As to price, margarine is retailed at Bd. to lOd. the
lb., reliable butter can hardly'go under Is. 2d. Nor
can a stone be cast at the wholesomeness of the stuff;
in fact, the Glasgow corporation chemist states that
the use of vegetable oils, chiefly cocoanut, brings
margarine well within the range of pure butter as a
food, while as for taste only an expert can distin-
guish—-and he often has to rely on texture as well.
WRITTEN ON MY INCOME TAX FORM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In pursuance of the Acts, and the regulations
thereunder, every person in receipt of income within
the meaning of the said Acts is required to fill up
the following return, and deliver the same at, or
forward it by post to, the office of the Commission-
ers of Taxation. —Horrible official warning.
The Old Year’s gone with its joys and sorrows;
The cash it brought me has also fled ;
The young New Year with its blank To-Morrows—
Mere calendar leaves—hangs overhead;
The week days black and the Sundays red
Look down upon me like great birds waiting;
These kites and vultures must 4 all be fed ;
Their endless appetites I am sating.
Into the sunset the last flock wavered
On weary pinions that slowly flapped;
Below a hideous curse I quavered;
In bills receipted my wounds I wrapped.
Oh, but my anguish with ease was capped
When out of the east came fresh ones winging i
Their hungry beaks on the trees they snapped.
(Their raucous croaks in my ears are ringing! 1
Butcher and grocer, milk-o and baker,
They’re waiting all for their pound of gold!
The watchful shades of the moneymaker
That follow—follow till he grows cold 1
No coins are left in the purse’s fold ;
My gains are nil for the long year’s raking.
Yet my lost income must here be told,
While this year’s vultures their meed are taking!
N.s.w.
E. O’F.

No sport is more exciting—more pleasurable—than
keenly-contested horse racing.

Course.

contested, and the ground appointments thoroughly up
to date.
No detail tending to increase the comfort or enjoy-
ment of patrons has been overlooked.

BRISBANE.
IN BANANA LAND
On present appearances Bananaland
Assembly will house another member of the
Bell family. Wienliolt, having quitted Fassi-
tern in order to win the affections of Wide
f?ay, by boxing two rounds with all comers,
there is a rush of “Liberals” for the State
seat; but E. T. Bell, of Coochin-Coochin
station, looks like an easy winner from the
crowd. The Bells’ reputation for scatter-
ing money freely will tell in a purblind dis-
tnct which clung to Wienholt because that
wealthy young man handed over his Parlia-
mentary remuneration to the local hospital.
Brisbane Oddfellows are up against a
bi am-racking problem. Women are rapidly
pushing their way into the most recondite
mysteries of Oddfellowship, and now the
awful question has arisen, “Can a woman
be a Noble Grand?” Up to date, the Bris-
bane conference has said “No,” but it has
said it to a sex -that read the story of the
importunate woman when it went to Sun-
day school, and hasn’t forgotten the lesson.
Now that the millionth male Eureka
otockader has gone to his reward the
women are coming along with vivid stories
of their part in that famous riot. Two old
ladies have just written to the Courier
giving their experiences. One of them pro-
duces a flattened bullet which whistled bv
her head during the battle. And, even if
anyone were inclined to doubt a lady’s word
there s no disputing the bullet, of course.
Queensland’s new Liquor Act got to busi-
ness on April 1, and Bung has fallen upon
evil days. While the Law is looking, lie
cannot now serve any person under 21 with
liquor; blackfellows, lunatics and Kanakas
may get no drink; women are prohibited
customers. The whole grog business must
henceforth be conducted strictly on a cash
basis, no debt being recoverable by law.
Lambing-down is henceforth an impossi-
yi~ ty , ( so it is alleged), for when Jimmy
W ire l ence . Pl an k s his cheque he must
get the change immediately—no putting it
j? the till and . telling Jimmy in 48 hours
that his deposit is exhausted, and that it
is time for him to take the track with a
bottle to comfort him.
“McNorth,” from Thursday Island :
Here the banging of hammers and plug-plug of
oil engines driving circular-saws in the Jap boat-
slips, mingle nicely with the tones of the Sabbath
church bells. The law that was passed to prevent
sweating and kindred evils is flouted in the most
flagrant manner by our brown brethren. If Bill
Smith mere white man, wants to build a fowlhouse
da^A e n ha ? t 0 obtain a Permit. No one
interferes with the Jap; apparently, he can do as
he pleases, and he goes on systematically doing it
The consequence is that the Jap has got the whole
ot the boat-building industry in his hands. White
carpenters cannot compete with his long hours and
short wages; they are objects of derision if they
even attempt it. A walk along Thursday’s main
street at night will reveal more instances of colored
brothers indifference to the voice of authority. Jap
tailors! shops will be seen to be illuminated, and sit-
tmg in full view of passers-by are the workmen mak-
ISlpClothes for white residents who profess to be
White Australians. The Chow does much the same. Some
time ago the Early Closing Act was enforced, and
Ah Sin was informed that he would in future have
to close his stores and workshops at 6 p.m. . instead
ot 8 p.m. He blandly agreed—but opened two
hours earlier in the morning. So far nobody has
lemonstrated, and it looks very much as if nobodv
cares to..
How are the mighty fallen! Brisbane,
once the home of Brunton Stephens and
minor bards innumerable, couldn’t raise a
solitary poem worthy of the prize set apart
for a lyrical effusion at the local Eistedd-
fodd.. Nine sets of verses were sent in, but
the judges looked at the nine—the tuneful
Nine— and said “We pass !”
A retiring president of the Methodist con-
ference haughtily told a Bananaland audi-
ence the.other day that the pastor&and mem-
bers of his show could ao without Fisher’s
baby bonus plasters.” Considering that
Methodist parsons get £8 a year extra for
every child they own, and that 99 p.c. of
them eagerly snare it, the saintly potentate’s
jest was, to put it mildly, out of place.
Though the Liberals nowadays make some
pretence of enthusiasm in matters of De-
fence, at heart a big section of them are
just the old let-it-sliders. Thus, Finlayson,
M.H.R., told Ipswich the other day
I believe there is no nation in the world to-day
that wotjld invade Australia.
Presumably, every unprepared nation that
ever was invaded, fooled itself with those
words. Korea was certainly saying such
things only a few years ago—and where is
Korea now?
QUEENSBURY RULES.
PEACE-MAKING PUBLICAN : “Don't -take no notice. He's drunk, and he’s a big Jellow
to boot.”
STRANGER: “Dam nit, man! D’ye think I kick when I fIGHT?"
Arnold Wienholt, Liberalism’s black hone
against Andy Fisher, is of German extrac-
tion. His father misrepresented the West-
ern Downs electorate with Robert Ramsay
in 1871. He married a daughter of Dan
William* who built the first section of the
Toowoomba-Warwick railway at the close of
the ’6o’s. Recent mention of Arnold’s pugil-
istic efforts recalls that he is a daisy at
electioneering. He beat Jenkinson out of
sight for Fassifern by promising his salary
to the local hospitals, going around rigged
up _as a cowboy and yelling “Hoch der
Kaiser” in his spare moments. The latter
stfint told in the frequent centres where Pots-
dam was a mor£ familiar name than Bris-
bane.
Having had a few months’ trial of things
sinc£ the old C. D. Act was repealed,
Queensland now finds that some action is
necessary; wherefore it is provided by Order
in Council that if the Commissioner suspects
any perspn of venereal disease he may re-
quire individual to submit to special
clinical or bacteriological examination. The
Order particularly specifies that men are
included. Properly and pluckily adminis-
tered, the Order (which is based on the
Health Act) should do an immense amount
of good—or, anyway, stop an immense
amount of harm.
Avignon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Twas in an old brown book I read it,
A heavy book with a golden clasp,
1 here were blurs of damp on the ragged
cover,
A stain of green on the broken hasp.
Avignon !” How the soft word haunts me
T , twittering pipes in the summer air,
Uelmate winds in the feathery grasses,
\\ hen light as a leaf the white moth passes
And dreams are everywhere.
Avignon! Was it hill or forest
Windy eyot or bough-blind way;
Cow white walls of a tree-girt city,
-Lhe word has followed me all the day!
a . dust y road in a hilless country
t •"'V 1 trunks stricken with fire and drought
1 aow a woman’s lips could say it
Avignon,” when the stars are out!
And I turn in fancy the pages over
(The sun blood spotting the haze’s grey).
And dream the name as a twilight lover
Dreams the end of a burning day.
And over the creak of the saddle leather,
And over the red spur’s driving on.
Comes the croon of winds in the feathery
grasses,
As when the ghost of an old love passes
With a broken whisper of “Avignon !’-
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 5, 1913.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
His Ex. in a frock-coat (etc.), Lady
Macgregor in mauve, and the Vision in a de-
licious dove-grey frock, adorned the General
Hospital meeting on Thursday. Everybody
in the room, excepting the two official ladies,
spruiked. Her Excellency, however, filled a
gap by ladling out the medals won by the
useful nurses. Grey haired Nurse McDiar-
mid —the angel of the hospital, as she is
usually called—scooped a gold medal for
highest marks in her year.
The Indian Government, having issued a
serious warning to white Australian women
against marrying Afghans or Pathans, I’d
like t" know what the Australian Govern-
ment intends to do about it. At Mount
Gravatt, a few miles out of Brisbane, an
Afgharn hawker bought a few acres of land
opposite the State School, married a white
woman, and settled there. Then he cut his
estate up into small allotments "nd sold
them to his fellow-heathens. These followed
his example with regard to white wives, and
now a half-caste settlement with a Moham-
medan mosque in its midst, has sprung up.
There are too many palpable leaks in this
White Australia policy,, to say nothing of
the horrors that await our lost white sisters
when, as mostly happens, they are taken to
the Land of the Flowing Shirt-tail. Does
the whiteness of Australia end when we
block the landing of a percentage of Chinese
stowaways V
Fred B. Smith and Raymond Robins have
been straining -the ear-drums of the occasion-
ally-faithful in this city with a shriek en-
titled, “Men and Religion.” It seems a
hard, dry, weevily sort of exhortation, still
1 suppose it has its advantages from the
point of view of the eternally-peregrinating
plate. The show was opened by a sort of
banquet, saddened by the absence of the
cup that hiccups, but officially brightened by
the presence of George V.’s plenipotentiary,
who was supported by a subdued private
secretary. The holy Smith testified against
the indecency” of the Brisbane women’s
dress, and we feel so awed now that “trans-
parencies” arp off till the next fine day
tVo e ft? wh - 1 i e ’ i We ' re h V nting out grandma’s
elastic-side boots, poke bonnet and shawl.

AUSTRALIA JANE.
Queensland s enervating climate received
a painful showing up the other dav when the
annual report of the Ispwich Technical Col-
rm passed round. Referring to the
that some of the State’s school-
teacher students had to surmount in their
efforts to attend the Saturday morning
classes at the college, it was stated that one
pedagogue left his far-out home at half-past
midnight, walked eight miles to the main
.no c . au Sbt a freight train and
l eached his destination about breakfast time.
But, with the weakening effects of the cli-
mate and the hardships of reaching the
centre of study, the best he could do was to-
head the list of Queensland’s Technical Col-
lege students in the subjects he took up. It
is all very disheartening. ,
F x '?VK r i ntend ® nt O’Callaghan got am
