apparent.
The \ ictorian Board of Health has unani-
mously recommended that in the Health Bill
which is to be introduced next session by the
'\att-YVeary Government, a clause should be
included making it compulsory for those
about to marry to produce a clean bill of
health. The proposal has, however, been re-
cmved by John Weary with bleak and heavy
disdain, and the Argus has become morbidly
facetious about it. Yet thinking citizens know
that there is something illogical in health
laws which profess to prevent the importa-
tion of the unfit from without, while permit-
ting their wholesale propagation within. The
Argus tells the Board of Health “that, to'
avoid being made ridiculous, it should
devote itself to the objects for which
it was appointed, and not waste its
time in discussing and deciding upon:
matters which it does not understand,
ihe Tory sheet evidently never heard
ot that expert in these matters Havelock
Ellis, who remarked, “It is the task of
Social hygiene not only to make sewers, but
to re-make love.”

EQUIPMENT.
WHEELBASE—IIO inches.

3i-inch bore, 5-inch stroke. Two-bearing
• crank shaft. Timing gears and valves en-
closed. Three-point suspension.
STEERING—RIGHT SIDE. Irreversible worm
gear, 16-inch steering wheel. Throttle con-
trol on stering column.
CONTROL—CENTRE LEVER operated through
H-plate, integral with universal joint hous-
ing just below. Hand-lever emergency
brake at driver’s left. Foot accelerator in
connection with hand throttle.
SPRINGS —Front, semi-elliptic; rear, full ellip-
tic and mounted on swivel seats.
FRAME—Pressed steel channel.
AXLES-—Front, I-beam, drop-forged ; rear,
semi-floating type.
TRANSMISSION—3 speeds forward and reverse;
sliding gear, selective type.
CONSTRUCTION—Drop-forgings wherever prac-
ticable ; chrome nicked steel used throughout
all shafts and gears in the transmission and
rear axle; high carbon manganese steel in
all parts requiring special stiffness.
BODY—FuII 5-passenger English type; extra
wide seats.

B.S.A. TWO SPEED FREE ENGINE HUB.
Owing to its extreme simplicity there is little to get out
of order in the B.S.A. Two-Speed Free Engine Hub.
The three positions—Top Speed, Free Engine and Low
Speed—are each taken up with silent smoothness through
the movement of a single clutch, operated by a con-
veniently placed foot pedal. The single train of gearing
is always in mesh and, therefore, free from risk of
damage. Its proved reliability in town traffic and on
hills make it indispensable to every motor cyclist.

For the Country Horseman
The saddle here illustrated is expressly designed
for country use, for stock riding, etc., and is built
of the best materials throughout. If any of our
friends are requiring such a saddle they will find
this a genuine bargain.
It has a hog-skin seat, a plated bar-tree, extra
long flaps, with points to buckle below. An economi-
cal feature is the skirt, which extends all round
and thus adds years of wear to the saddle by pro-
tecting its interior from the weather. It has a
deep, “sweepy” seat, ' 4in. kneepads, nickel-plated
iron stirrups, and double buckle leather girth. The
price complete is £3/15/- Mention of The
Bulletin when ordering will ensure your having a
leather surcingle given in free, and the saddle will
be forwarded freight paid. It is to be obtained
from J. P. Talty, 388 Sussex Street, Sydney.

Government Notices.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, March 20, 1913.
TENDERS FOR PILE SHOES AND
PILE RINGS.
rjIENDERS will be received, subject to the
conditions of tendering, until 12 NOON
on TUESDAY, APRIL 29, 1913, for the
supply of Pile Shoes and Pile Rings for
Flinders Naval Base.
The approximate requirements are :
PILE SHOES .. 1020
PILE RINGS .. 24
Specifications can be obtained and draw-
ings inspected at the office of the Director of
Navy Contracts, Navy Office, Melbourne ; and
at the Naval Staff Office, Sydney, Brisbane,
Largs Bay (S.A.), Fremantle and Hobart.
.Tenders to be endorsed “Tender for Pile
Shoes and Pile Rings” on the top left-hand
corner of the envelope, and addressed (if by
post, prepaid) to the Director of Navy Con-
tracts, Navy Office, Melbourne; or to the
Director of Navy Contracts, care of District
Naval Officer, Sydney, Brisbane, Largs Bay
(S.A.), Fremantle and Hobart.
The lowest or any tender will not neces-
sarily be accepted.
Tenders to be in all cases on printed form,
which can be obtained as above.
G. F. PEARCE,
Minister of State for Defence.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 28th March, 19j.3.
Royal Australian Navy.
Tenders for Blankets.
TENDERS will be received, subject to the
conditions, of tendering, until 12 noon
on Monday, 28th April, 1913, for the supply
of One Thousand (1000) Blankets for the
Royal Australian Navy.
Sealed samples may be seen at the office
of the Director of Navy Contracts, Navy
Office, Melbourne, at the Naval Dep6t, Wil-
liamstown, and at the Naval Staff Office,
Sydney, Brisbane, Largs Bay (S.A.), Fre-
mantle and Hobart.
Tenders to be endorsed “Tender for Blan-
kets” in the top left-hand corner of envelope
and addressed (if by post, prepaid) to the
Director of Navy Contracts, Navy Office,
Melbourne, or to the Director of Navy Con-
tracts, care of District Naval Officer, Naval
Staff Office, Sydney, Brisbane, , Largs Bay
(S.A.), Fremantle or Hobart, as the case
may be.
The lowest or any tender will not neces-
sarily be accepted.
Tenders to be in all cases on the printed
forms which can be obtained as above.
G. F. PEARCE,
Minister of State for Defence.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking thie strain off them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
"f you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the W’ilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
Ihe Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you _ wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
liven a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

How’s This f ! r Genuine Cure?
This little girl whose picture is given below is the child of Mrs. M. Doogan,
No. 1 Ann Street, Surry Hills. The child had a most terrible burn on her back,
which it did not seem possible to cure. On February 3, l.Jlo, Mrs. Doogam wrote
us the letter printed below to tell us how thankful she was to think she had at last
found a cure for her poor little child who
had suffered so much pain.
You will see from the letter that she had
her child at the hospital for seven weeks,
and the doctors tried skin-grafting and gave
the little one every attention. However, it
seemed impossible to cure the sore and cause
the skin to grow again, and it was only as a
last resource that she tried Rexona, the
Rapid Healer, which has cured the burn and
caused the skin to grow in a most wonderful
manner.
That the burn was a terrible one can be
seen in the photograph of the scar left on the
child’s back, and everyone will agree that a
remedy which will so quickly cure so bad a
burn as this, a burn which had resisted
every other known remedy, certainly merits
the term “The Rapid Healer,” by which
Rexona is so widely known.
Since writing this letter Mrs. Doogan has
informed us that the child is absolutely
cured, and that healthy skin has grown en-
tirely over the sore. We are sure that all
mothers will read her letter with great. in-
terest, and agree with her that Rexona is a
necessity in every household.
MRS. DOOGAN’S LITTLE DAUGHTER.
(from a photo.)
REXONA
THE FAMILY
FRIEND.
February 3, 1913.
The Manager, Rexona.
Dear Sir, —I cannot describe to you on
paper or in words how grateful I am to your
wonderful ointment, Rexona, for what it has
done for my little girl. She was burnt eight
months ago. She was in the hospital three
times for the burn, and skin grafted once
seven weeks ago. She was sent out of the
hospital as an out-patient. I used to take her
every second day to have it dressed; but I
was never let see the wound, as the bandages
were sealed on her. When I would ask how
it was, they would tell me it was getting
better. After attending for four weeks as an
out-patient, one morning I undid the band-
age, and I was horrified to find a big mattery
sore. I took her that morning back to the
hospital and asked for the doctor. A nurse
told me the doctor said if I wanted it to heal
I would have to put her in, and let her be
skin-grafted again. I was broken-hearted at
this after all she had suffered. I made up my
mind I would not let her go back any more.
A friend of mine asked me to give Rexona a
trial, so I went and bought a tin, and after I
had cleaned the discharge from the sore with
hot fomentations I applied the ointment. The
first week it made a wonderful difference, and
each day it kept getting smaller, until it is

“Shaver.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HIS declaration that his name was
Edward Saunders is the one thing
to attest that Shaver had been ad-
mitted to the Christian fold. No one that
ever I could find knew more about him than
himself. And when he became one of the
group at Whylungela he was not old enough
to be an authority, even concerning himself.
There was a waterliole out in a back pad-
dock of the run —a bad paddock, too, rocky,
hilly, scrubby, useless, worse than useless,
for when sheep wandered into it they per-
ished ; wherefore we had fenced it off from
the rest of the station. It was seldom visi-
ted ; but it happened that one of my boun-
dary-riders was out in the Dead Finish pad-
dock, as we called it, in search of strayed
horses, and returned to tell me a sick travel-
ler with a little “kid” was camped there.
Next day I rode out with Bill, and found
the poor beggar who had halted in that
hungry desert. I was not doctor enough to
know his ailment or to help him much; but
he was very ill, no doubt about that. Per-
haps, if I had been a regular medico, he
would not have suffered my treatment; for
a more sulky patient I never met.
A wretched camp it was for a sick man —■
just a few bushes propped against the stump
of a dead tree. He lay wrapped in a single
blanket; a swag for his pillow. Outside the
mi-mi the accoutrements of a couple of
horses littered the ground ; at the fire were
a pair of tin pots. Food seemed scarce. All
this was not surprising to me; we see all
sorts and conditions of tramps, sick and
well and malingering, out our way, and the
pick of them don’t travel with much baggage.
The queer thing was that such a chap
should have a little boy with him, a young-
ster from eight to 10, I should say, a picture
of misery, too, poor little wretch. He was
as silent as the man ; I can’t say if he was
fond or afraid of him, but he never left the
sick man’s side. He was dressed in some of
the man’s old clothes —not so much dressed
as lost in them. His boots alone seemed to
belong to his size. I wanted him to come
with me to the station, but he refused. So
I left Bill with what I had brought for the
sick man, and directions to look after him.
I had a heavy post to dispose of next
morning, so that it was late in the afternoon
before I was able to revisit the camp. The
man was visibly near his end. He was
hardly conscious, and while we waited he
died, as silent as he had been all along. We
wrapped him in his blankets and protected
the body from interference; then, taking
with us the stranger’s swags, and mounting
the boy before me upon my horse, we went
back home. Next day Bill and another man
went out and buried the traveller, and
brought in his horses.
The swag contained no clue to his iden-
tity ; the boy knew him by a Christian name
and said: “Th’ ol’ man an’ me we wus work-
in’ our way down f’om Billylaringa, but Tom
fell sick, an’ got worse, an’ we ’ad to camp.”
I wrote to the manager of Billylaringa; he
knew nothing of the pair as I met them. I
questioned every manager for a hundred
miles round; but no one seemed to know
either the boy or his companion. Then I
sold the dead man’s horses ; and the money,
with a small sum found in the swag, was
put aside for the youngster.
What to do with the boy was the next
thing to decide. On this point there was
agreement at the station. Our cook and
laundress, Mrs. Honorah O’Connor, said:
“Sure, an’ ye c’u’dn’ do ut, to sind a b’y
awa-ay to th’ p’lice; ’tis a harrd toime ’e’d
have wid ’em, and turrn out bad. Ye’ll not
miss the bit ’e ates, an’ Oi’ll say to s clo es.
Bill’s wife, down in the hut, was of the
same opinion. Shaver shook his head when
I suggested he should go in to the troopers
at the township, and asked: “What for? I’d
rather stop here.” So it was agreed he
should stay on with us.
The two women fed and clothed and cared
for him, till they gave him something of the
air and appearance of a boy at his age. He
was tall, but strangely emaciated in face and
body, partly because of hard living. When
he was stripped, it was painful to see how
tightly the skin stretched over his ribs
seemingly their only covering, for flesh there
was none; to see the throbbing of the heart
and the fallen arch of the abdomen. Poor
little chap ! His lines had fallen in far from
easy places. Nor was he in countenance
the kind of youth who takes the eye with the
charm of healthy and 'contented boyhood.
His usual expression was neither wistful nor
melancholy, but self-contained. Frown or
laugh seldom crossed his face; his shrewd
eyes were watchful and steadfast; and he
had a notable talent for keeping his thin
lips closed.
About his past he never willingly spoke.
We ceased to question him when we found
he could not, or would not, tell much. The
men on the station called him “Shaver,” and
the name stuck, till he himself adopted it.
His allegiance was divided between Graham,
the young storekeeper, and myself, Alfred
Hurst, manager of Whylungera. Shaver was
too old to be interested in the toddlers down
at the hut, though now and again he condes-
cended to nurse and care for them at the
mother’s request. He did not like the job,
but he was grateful to his friends and kind
to everything. In short, Shaver soon be-
came a privileged person among u§. He took
his meals generally with Mrs. Honorah in
the kitchen, and he slept somewhere in her
kingdom.
As months went by, Shaver did credit
to the care of his friends. Young as he was,
he grew an accomplished horseman, and he
had a quick eye for the features of the bush.
He stood on good terms with the men, and
was accounted by them “a dashed handy use-
ful, good-natured little beggar, worth his
tucker, all of it.” Company of his own age
there was none on the station, so that
Shaver had to share the grown-up folks'
life. My fellows were very decent chaps,
from whom the boy could learn nothing
worse than a good deal of jocose experience
of the world, a few commonplace oaths, and
the tags and refrains of some music-hall
ditties five years old or more.
Other things indeed he learned. The men
taught him all that concerned the run, its
boundaries, extent, neighbors, roads, tracks,
paddocks, tanks, timber, and a world of that
close knowledge, based upon endless observa-
tion, which is the bushman’s stock-in-trade.
They taught him during the long evenings of
winter to play dominoes and draughts, crib-
bage and euchre and “nap.” He caught
their tricks of pronunciation, varied now and
then by Graham’s accent or mine. He
adopted their idea of music, and could lift
up a thin but steady stave on occasion, with-
out shyness as without forwardness. Step-
ping out to us if we were on the verandah of
a summer night, or to the hearth, indoors,
with the firelight upon his face, if it was
winter, he would drone his way through the
song, sure of applause. He learned to finger
the powerful English concertina of Adolf
Woltermann (better known as Peter the
Dutchy), when the owner of that esteemed
instrument was absent on duty. _ If there
was some simple jollification, a birthday or
anything of the kind, he got rather more
than he ought to have had of much-watered
whisky; and he could not have been 12. when
he came to me in a woeful state obviously
traceable to indulgence in a very rank to-
bacco pipe. _
Between Graham and myself Shaver
learned by degrees to read a newspaper and
to understand most of what he read there ;
also, he could write a serviceable fist, which
all who ran could read, and the same can
no man say of mine. He was wiry and
tough of body, if meagre still; he was sound
in wind and limb; he had shrewd eyes and
a tight mouth and a firm hand for bridle
or rein. When he did talk he had a clean
tongue and brief, his head was clear, he was
thoughtful for animals, and he was grateful
to his friends —that is, to everybody. I do
not care where a boy comes from, or how he
gets his training—if these things may
truthfully be said of him when he is going on
for 16 years I’ll stand to two things: that
the boy was worth his training and his train-
ing was the best he could get.
It was a long while before Shaver left the
station. Once at session time, having busi-
ness in the township, 120 odd miles south, I
went there, and Shaver went with
strict command to answer only to “Ned.”
We came in by coach, the station horses (it
was drought time) not being equal to the
journey. After a dusty trip we got in
safely, had supper and a wash, and went to
bed. It was later than usual when I awok»
next morning, and after my bath I pushed
open the door of Shaver’s room to call him.
Shaver wasn’t in. While dressing, I won-
dered a good deal, and coming downstairs I
made search of him. That was hardly neces-
sary. for very soon I heard his voice. Used
to ‘the early hours of the station, he had
got up quite early, before daylight, I think,
and had spent the first hours of the Sunday
in helping the groom for Cobh and Co. to get
the mail coach ready to despatch. This done,
and the stable, loose boxes and horses having
been minutely and silently examined, the
young man ingratiated himself with the girls
of the hotel by lighting their fires and bring-
ing wood for them. He was receiving much
detailed information about the inner work-
ing of a country hotel, from the servants
point of view, when I found him. Now 1
had long been on the best of terms with the
girls at my favorite hotel, which I visit',
about once in four months, but Shavers
popularity soon threatened or promised—
as you prefer —to surpass mine. -There
really must have been trouble if Shaver had
been at all impressionable.
Out on Whylungera we never saw a par-
son. Ido not blame them. Roads that way
were generally bad, sometimes impassable,
distances were long, houses few, people
hearty and hospitable, but not at all devout.
It is said that the clergy can only be per-
suaded to come outback by the temptation of
a good wedding fee, or at shearing, when the
men can raise a collection. So Shaver and
I went to church. It was not very far from
the hotel, and the neighborhood caused the
church-going bell to be heartily cursed by all
the late risers. Some of them said that only
for the splitting headache caused by that
bell they would have gone to church
that morning. Luckily the headache was re-
lieved after breakfast, for those who most
complained spent a cheerful forenoon with
cards and clouds of smoke and sundry com-
forting draughts in the snuggery off the bar-
room. behavior W3,s cxGmpl&ry.
He got the swing of the hymns and the
chants, and joined them out of liking for the
tunes; he stood up or knelt or sat with the
congregation; and during the sermon he
bestowed a friendly but searching regard
upon the preacher which once or twice came
near to disconcerting him. Then he would
study the few boys in front of him; as to the
girls' he was indifferent. That service gave
rise to a long examination afterwards. I
explained how it was that the church was
not intended for a place of residence, why
the parson dressed up in that rig, why some-
times the people said their prayers out loud,
sometimes the parson alone, and then the
whole mob of them, what they did with the
money, and was that what the chaps on
Whvlungera meant when they talked about
“shovin’ roun’ the hat?” Then the boy
added judicially: “I s’pose them fellows
there” (meaning the boys he had observed)
“go ’cause they like it, but it wouldn’t suit
m< All next day I was present at court, where
the customary cases of fraud and so forth
were being hurried through by Judge and
Crown lawyer, anxious to close the sittings
so as to enjoy a day’s shooting, while the
local attorneys were bent on prolonging the
trials for their own kudos and gain, lo

make up for Shaver’s dull day we went that
night to a circus performance of the usual
up-country standard. As far as I knew, this
was the first time in his life that Shaver had
been present at such a display. The tent
was not large, or the company first-rate; but
the delight, the envy, the emulation which
they excited ! Not that the boy was at all
demonstrative. He sat among us in the
chief seats, upon a bench of rough hard-
wood, scantily draped with old coarse drug-
get, but he hardly stirred, his mouth was
firmly closed, while his eyes devoured all
which the ring displayed. It was a poor
show, certainly; you don’t get Barnums
in the backblocks. The japes were ancient,
the horses looked as if they wanted more feed
and more grooming, the men were red-
nosed, thick-spoken, and stiff in the joints ;
while the boys were ill-trained and cheeky.
But it was a revelation to Shaver. He
lost not a motion of the thin, wasted slip of
a girl in scant and faded finery who jumped
through the paper ring and kissed a mani-
festly dirty hand to us as she turned her big,
dull eyes unon the group which represented
the “boxes”; he was on tenter-hooks until
she had done her risky trick. It did not
seem to him a woman’s business to be a
bare-backed rider and control a horse to per-
fection ; that, in Shaver’s eyes, was a man’s
affair. Hence, perhaps, his keenest interest
was given to the girl. He watched narrowly,
but with a more indifferent air, the lads of
the company, whose spangles glittered in the
light of a ring of guttering candles overhead,
whose unyouthful faces met our eyes as the
riders swept round the ring in the flight of
their cantering horses; while their harsh
voices goaded on the animals as the ring-
master’s whip and the thought of to-morrow
goaded the riders.
Shaver smiled upon none; he heard the
clown’s sad jests without an alteration of
countenance; you might have thought him
quite indifferent. Ah, then, you should have
seen and heard him in our horse-yard the
Sunday morning following, when he did half
the tricks for the station hands at home, re-
peated confidently strings of patter of which
I could only recognise a word here and there,
mimicked the clown, the ringmaster and the
few remaining performers, and described
minutely the various costumes of the mem-
bers of the company. There is but little
that interests him which a bushman’s keen
eyes miss or ignore, which his eager mind
fails to record or his faithful memory trea-
sure permanently.
* * * *
Time passed. Three years later, Shaver
was grown out of his name. He was tall
and straight; thin, as of old, but no longer
with the attenuation of want and neglect;
he was now a young man who got a warm
glance from every woman on the roads and
the stations. The same frank true eyes ; the
same oval face, bronzed by sun and weather
in his out-of-doors life ; the same firm, closed
mouth; all these were unchanged. But he
had grown a moustache, heavy, black and
curling, though the strong chin and lower
jaw were shaved. He had the practised'
horseman’s slight stoop of shoulders and flat-
ness of chest, thin flank and firm, full thighs
with their powerful grip of the saddle. In
his saddle or out of it, he was as fine a
young Australian as could be seen. Brimful
of health and pluck, with a temper naturally
kind and slow to be provoked, yet, once offended,
implacable; good company with all he met;
by choice a bushman and best pleased among
men of his own class, but bearing himself
gravely and simply in the best society of the •
district; I tell you I was proud of my pupil.
In our journeys we sat together at the same
table with men of all conditions —squatters
like myself, their wives, their girls and boys,
Government officials, drovers, surveyors,
commercial travellers, sheep buyers, Wool
agents, and all travellers of the western
roads. Young as he was, I never saw him
at fault. He dressed as I did, in the rough,
strong tweeds that stand hard wear in the
bush—loose, open coat, half-buttoned vest
over a cricketing or tennis shirt, trousers
made to be comfortable in the saddle, neat
boots of soft leather, a loose grey hat of felt
upon his head —such was Slmver.
He had by this time become my overseer,
for he had clung to me all through the years.
When Graham got his promotion, a year be-
fore, Shaver transferred his account to me.
The other men about the station saw that he
was sure to rise, but as he was still the same
quiet, steady, kindly fellow as ever, no one
begrudged him his advance. The Melbourne
owners wanted to “ring-in” a newchum for
me to break in, but I said : “No, you don’t,
if you please” ; and Ned was appointed. It
made little difference. He had long had
quarters in bachelors’ hall, as we call the
rooms of the single station officials —the
storekeeper, the overseer, the experienced
hands, and so on. He took his meals with
us; he was as much and as intimately one
of us as if he had been sent up from town
and had not “growed” among us from his
four-foot nothing to over six foot. Mr.
Saunders to the men: to me he was always
Shaver.
We sat at breakfast one hot morning in
December. Early as it was, the blaze upon
the empty plain before our room could hardly
be endured. “Shaver, old son,” I said.
“What’s up, boss?” he asked at once.
I tossed to him across the table an opened
letter which had arrived by the morning’s
mail.
It was a scrawl from old Jimmy Robert-
son, of Cootralantra. The old boy had been
stocking heavily for shearing, and he was
caught in a bit of a drought; in consequence
he wished to sell me some of his prime sheep
for next to nothing, so that they shouldn’t
die on the run. lie wrote now to place a
ten-thousand lot under offer at 3s. delivered
on Cootralantra. I had room for them;
and, besides, I thought it likely the weather
would break, when my- speculation must
greatly improve. This was the letter Shaver
was reading.
“That’s a cheap lot,” he commented.
“Dead cheap,” said I, “and I mean to take
it. But the bosses in Melbourne won’t stand
a draft for ten thousand : I should be over
my limit with six. So I’ll buy five thou, for
them, and the other half I’ll take on my
own hook. There’ll be a year’s wool at least,
maybe some lambs, and I’ll sell off shears.
With anything like luck, I reckon on
clearing a couple of hundreds. Now, you
ain’t busy, old man ; suppose you take deliv-
ery and bring the monkeys over here. I’ll
stand you £3O for the job, or go you third
share, if you like.”
Shaver spread the marmalade thick upon
his bread, finished his cup of coffee, and re-
plenished the cup before he replied. Just
his deliberate wav : I knew what the answer
would be: “Please yourself, boss ; I’m ready
to go anywhere you like.”
I considerd the risks for half a minute,
then I said: “£3O let it be, then. The
cheque’ll be more useful to you for etceteras
when you tie-up with the little girl on
Yougoway.”
She was the manager’s daughter, a grand
girl, reared on a station, pretty as a picture,
and just as good, jolly and kind and straight,
but plenty of pluck and temper—whenever
I saw her she made me think of what such a'
girl might have made out of me if we had
met 20 years ago. Ah, well! I liked her
with all my heart, but Shaver worshipped
her, and I think she appreciated it.
He gave _me a glance and said: “Not
bound to give me a cent. I’m the firm’s
property.”
“Firm be hanged ! Think I want to use
you up for nix; what sort of a ‘shyster’ do
you take me for? Not my style, sonny.”
“I know,” he said. “Just do as you think
best.”
“Then it’s settled,” I went on. “You can
cut your stick as soon as you like. Go over
by Yougoway, and you need be in no hurry.
Robertson’ll be too glad to see you at all to
make any bother about a day here or there.
But, I say, old man. Don’t you come back
through Yougoway. They’ve got none too
much feed or water there, and I’m thinking
Feltham’ll be losing some of his stock. You
bring your mob down the CootreHntra boun-
dary, cross the Luck’s All Creek, then you
can follow the road round here. It’s a bit
of a roundabout, but it’ll never do to mooch
on poor old Feltham just now.”
“It’s dashed kind of you to think of that,”
he said in his slow way.
So, a few days afterwards, I strolled
down one hot forenoon to the first paddock
gate with the dogs, and there we said good-
bye ; Shaver and I. He gave me from his
saddle a grip of his firm, warm hand, the
clear eyes looking straight into mine as he
answered my “Take care of yourself, Shaver,
old boy; so-long,” with his cheery: “You
bet, boss; good day and gpod luck.” Then
he thrust his pipe back in between his
teeth, gave me a nod, half saucy, half affec-
tionate, and turned down the track in a
canter. The little mare swung along, the
rider firm as a rock in the saddle; the thin,
white smoke borne backwards by the* motion.
At the futher rise he halted a moment and
threw up his arm, and I waved my hat.
Then he was gone.
Poor old Shaver! Poor little lass of You-
goway !
After a week a man of mine who had been
sent with a few more to wait for Shaver’s
coming to take the Cootralantra sheep, came
back to the station, and the story that he
told, here it is :
“We got th’ sheep f’om Mr. Robe’son, an’
made a start. ’Twas rainin’, not much,
though. We fetched them sheep down along
th’ ol’ bound’ry fence, nex’ day we wus onto
the crick. I don’ think Mr. Saunders ’e
knew that sort o’ country, so I ups an’ tol’
’im w’at I thought. He says, cool-like: ‘This
’ere’s a bit of a spec o’ Mr. Hurst’s, an’ I
ain’t noways a-goin’ to deliver ’is sheep all
knocked :to pieces.’ ‘Right!’ I says, an’ says
no more, barrin’ to the chaps. Well, it
rained that night, like Hell, I can tell you ;
I carn’t rec’lect ’arder. My oath, it did pour.
Mr. Saunders ’e didn’t like it, not a little bit.
I knew we wus in for fits as soon as the
-crick come down. ‘Bill.’ says ’e. ‘we’ll try
and get the sheep on a bit.’ ‘Lor’,’ I says, it’s
as dark as inside a tarbarrel; sheep won’t
move!’ ‘We’ll try,’ he said, and so try we
did. But sheep ’t any time’s awful fools.
We wus hours gettin’ ’em a bir furder,
an’ then we camps. ’Ad to. Mr. Saun-
ders ’e was sorry and angry, ’f y’u
kin un’erstan’. Not to say much, though.
’Twas too miser’ble. Rain, rain, rain, an’
mud ev’rywhere, an’ I’d a gev a big slice out
o’ me year’s screw f’r us all to be ’way any-
where out o’ that. ’Cause I knowed that
crick. It’s a man-trap, that’s what it is.
W’en I wus carryin’, I see that crick’s dry
’s a skelington be 6 o’clock o’ night, an’ we
wus stuck there next mornin’, an’ f’r three
days afore we could cross it. That was up ’igh ;
down where we wus wi’ th’ sheep it spread
out f’r miles, an’ what give me fits wus to
think we wus right in the track of it. I
couldn’t tell f’r certain, ’cause them cricks
they ’as no channels to speak about, they
goes an’ scoops one out wheniver a
flood comes down. I wus dozin’ ’r some-
thin’, an’ all at once Jim sings out: .‘Christ!
Billy, what’s that?’ ’Twas the crick. We
could ’ear it like a roar, but couldn’ see fur
the dark. Mr. Saunders, ’e jumps fur ’is
’orse, says he: ‘Are you—goin’ to let the
sheep go to blazes whilst y’u scratch y’ur
’ead?’ I said t’ ’m, ‘For God A’mighty’s
sake, don’ you go ’way f’om this ’ere camp !
It’s ’ow us men’s to get quit of ’ere that
we’ve got to think about.’ He turns on me
short, ‘You a cocktail, Billy?’ ‘No, that I
ain’t,’ says I, ‘l’ll go ’long with you.’ No
more said. Got me ’orse, an’ on I jumps. Mr.
Saunders, ’e shook ’ands wi’ me, an’ says.
‘lt’s Mr. Hurst’s spec, this is Billy, an’ if
I lose them sheep I’d as well lose me right
’an’.’ ‘Oh, I didn’ know,’ I said. We wus
splashin’ away to th’ sheep camp. All of a
sudden we went souse into deep water. Mr.
Saunders ’e sings out. ‘My God, where’s them
sheep?’ I wus be’in’ ’im an’ hollers, ‘Turn
back, man, this ’ere’s the crick!’ ’E didn’
take no notice, on’y says, ‘The sheep !’ We
wus tryin’ to get furder, an’ the curr’nt wus
sweepin’ us down. It all ’appened like a
heap, I couldn’ say how, but ’is ’orse come
down on to mine, an’ we wus both under.
Lor’, ’ow that water took me down. I felt
meself goin’ like fun. I reached out after
somethin’, don’ know what, couldn’ hold it,
an’ went furder. Then I got among scrub,
an’ pretty soon clutched for life, an’ ’eld on.
Findin’ it wus a biggish bush, I climbs up
an’ sits there shiverin’ all night. When
mornin’ come, lo an’ be’ol’, shallow water
wus on one side an’ the crick ’alf a mile
wide on the other. Well. I wades in an’
gets back to the camp. They wus in a nice
way, too! Jim ’e went back to Cootralan-
tra, an’ me an’ the boy we ’unts roun’ f’r
Mr. Saunders. We didn’ do anv good till
the crick goes down ; be that time Mr. Robe’-
son come down. We foun’ Mr. Saunders in
one o’ the ben’s, near knocked to bits with
the timber. Hawful. Sir, ’e wus a man, by
God !
“A man. by God !” That was his epitaph.
Boor old Shaver!
THOMAS HENRY.
